This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably-
known Dispensary that now bears his name, re-
ceived his medical education and surgical training
in the leading colleges and hospitals of Europe,
lie has devoted a life- time to the study of
Nervous Complaints.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated; with these widely recognised Qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
DO YOU SUFFER
from headaches, drowsiness during the day, circles
round the eyes, giddiness, irregularity of the
bowels, specks before the eyes, bashfulness, dis-
contentedness, weakness or pain in the back, liver
or kidney complaints, buzzing in the ears and
head, pimples, a fear that something dreadful is
about to happen, weak stomach, yellow or blood-
shot eyes, constipation, impure blood, hasty,
uncontrollable temper, hacking cough, nervous-
ness in society, wakefulness, palpitation of the
heart, general weakness, a fear of insanity and
death, if so, send 6d. in stamps to the above
address, and you will, by return of post, receive
postage
FREE, A LARGE
CLOTH BOUND BOOK,
showing plainly how all diseases, which result in
the above-named symptoms, may be cured with-
out unnecessary expense. This book will prove a
valuable companion, and even though you be per-
fectly well, its general information and prescrip-
tions may assist you in restoring some poor friend
to health, even though his case has been previously
pronounced incurable.
One Pound is Sufficient to Send
for remedies necessary to cure any ordinary com-
plaint. Should you require medical treatment, as
procrastination is sometimes dangerous, it would
be advisable for you to sit down quietly in your
chamber and write to the Doctor detailing all
experienced symptoms. He will, on receipt of
such a letter, write you promptly and courteously
a candid and straightforward opinion of your
case. If you do not think you understand your
case, send for his famous medical work, the price
of which is but 6d. ; enclosed in the volume
will be found a list of carefully-prepared questions
which will assist you materially in describing
your ailments. Dr. Richards uses nothing but
the purest remedies, and never prescribes injurious
drugs under any circumstances. Dr. Richards
has successfully treated nearly 30,000 cases since

A Month, in a Sydney Hospital.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The world is full of men over 40 who are
not physicians. I’m one, else would I have
applied at once for admittance to one of the metro-
politan hospitals. The symptoms were those of
lumbago, sciatica, an apparently- twisted spine,
and a temper like that of Mr. Justice M illiams, of
Victoria.
“ My dear fellow,” said thesurgeon, “you’ve got
a lumbar abscess which, from a professional stand-
point, leaves nothing to be desired. It is a credit
to you, and is a pathological lecture in itself. It
liiiist have been of slow growth, for no merely
human system could develope such a bulge at
short notice. I’ve seen but two like it. One was
grown at sea and brought to the Edinburgh In-
firmary by a Russian sailor. His skeleton is in
the Museum now. I almost forget where I saw
the other. However, the thing to do now is to
send at bnce for the Alfred Hospital ambulance.
I shall be over there all the afternoon as it is
operation-day.
“ Do you think there’s arty danger, doctor ? ”
“ Well, in every operation there is, of course;
an element of danger sometimes from the
patient’s low condition Or faintheartedness, some-
times from the character of the work to be done,
and, once in a way (blandly), from the person who
does it. But cheer up. I see no earthly reason
why you shouldn’t be well again—a new man, in
fact—Within a month or six weeks.”
Hardly knowing at the time where the bigger
swelling was, at my back or at my heart, I kept
my appointment.
* * *
“ Have you had anaesthetics before ?”
asked the ether-giver as I lay on the table in ray
native innocence.
“Never,” I replied.
The droll, little, oddly-shaped ether-cup was
now over my nose, a sweetish taste in my mouth,
a sense of pressure on chest and ears, recalling to
my mind a lang-syne experience when, while
endeavouring to rescue a man who had jumped
into 20 feet of water, I toed the border-line of
unconsciousness; in my soul a desire to blather,
and prate, and then—oblivion.
# * *
“ When are they goin’ to operate ?” I
yawped at an uncanny thing that appeared to
stand a long way off beckoning and grinning.
It was only the wardsman putting me to bed and
smiling at the resources of my half-loosened
tongue. “He must be povverful when in proper
trim,” he observed methodically. It is but fair
to say, lipwevey, that the face of a smiling man is
not at its best when seen through ether fumes. It
is too faithful an imitation pf a gargoyle to inspire
confidence.
“It’s all right,” replied the wardsman, “and
what you’ve got to do now is to keep quiet and go
to sleep. ”
To sleep I went, but not to rest. The wards-
man was the official who helped to carry subjects
to the operating-room and bring back what was
left. Sometimes the latter didn’t amount to
much— a nucleus of half-awakened and blatant
consciousness, with most of the tentacles lopped
off. For days I was light-beaded at night (nothing
like putting a thing properly), but gradually the
lightness left my head and went to my stomach.
By degrees it dawned upon me that life would be
idyllic if the days were bounded at each end by
pork chops, while corned beef or legs of mutton
occupied the middle. It may be a gross way of
viewing things, but it is open to argument whether
any pleasure of a merely mental or ideal nature
can compare with the first faint promptings of
returning appetite after a serious illness-promp-
tings which, if unsatisfied, quickly develope from
whispers into bellows. But the doctors were in-
exorable, and the nurses followed in their foot-
steps—no difficult thing in some eases. At the
head of all ridiculous ideas is that of expecting an
Alfred Hospital nurse to give you a pdte in mistake
for porridge. Low diet is the first rule of the
institution.
The iyard contained 14 beds, with a bath-
roonj at one angle and a general-utility apartment
at the opposite. “ General-utility” is perhaps the
expression that best describes the latter, since it
was used, as a pantry, a kitchen, a general store,
and a convenient retiring-room for the nurses
when, they wished to exchange confidences over a
cup of tea. Occasionally a mild flirtation was
carried op there, just to keep the ladies in touch
with the outside world, divert their minds from
the grqesome sights of. the ward, and, in a
meiasure, keep their hands in till the weekly or
fortnightly leave-day. Not that a woman, when
young apd prepossessing, as many of the Alfred
nurses ate, is likely to forget how to make eyes
even if in a convent-cell with none but a palsied
priest to practise on. Fut her to sweep out a
morgue and she’ll regard with interest the
undemonstrative persons on the tables.
But tjiere were necessarily few eligibles among
the'patients. Some Were tooold, some too young,
some too broken-up or down, and all too poor. In
the whole room there was probably not enough
money to send an overWeight'letter as far as the
Suez 1 Canal. Besides, tenderness is a plant of
delic'ate structure. It will not flourish in the
vicifiity of two people one of whom has either a
wooden leg hr an uncontrollable desire to place a
hydatid cyst at the other’s feet, At 8 p.ni. the
lights were turned down to a point, and all good
wrecks expected to rest,
“Ah ! terrible Gahagans,” remarked
Napoleon, on his way from Moscow, as his sledge
topped a mound of the Old Guard, all bearing the
Gahagan mark. Ah ! those terrible hours between
eight and midnight in the accident-ward of the
Alfred. How all the mistakes and maunderings
and meannesses' of yesOta parade fbr inspection
before you—each foolishness leering as it
swaggers past the' saluting-point, in step witli
the half-stifled whimperings of men in pain!
A head and shoulders’ above this dismal litany
were two sounds that followed and chivvied each
other like the strophe' and anti-strbphe of a Greek
play. There Was no seasoning of suffering in
them. - So fur from it, they Suggested that the
persons in charge of them were convinced that
whatever is, is right. They were snores from two
juxtaposed bullock-drivers snores that cotild
hold tip their htiiids in any society. It is singular
what the human nose is capable of when it
is neglected. The mouth, the eyes, the hair, the
chin, even the ears, have been suiur. But the
most prominent feature of all—the organ which
gives'tono and light and leading to the others
has hoert* s<o slighted by Wribulhrs 1 thht, like the
dying swan, it is obliged’to sing for itself. It was
a mistake to put these two patients together.
They merely encouraged each other. A cow had
gored the first, a horse kicked the second. J u
each case the animal had unfortunately missed
the really objectionable feature. The lower
animals are irreolaimably stupid in that way
they always leave a job half-done.
Strange what grotesque fancies will arise in the
minds of sufferers kept from sleep by noises like
the foregoing! The man next me, crippled
through colliding with a fire-engine, said that
when the duet started, his memory at once vaulted
over a full score of years. It was the village
green with a stump in the middle over which the
wheel of the parson’s buggy had just passed. The
divine was on the ground, his list, wife in the air.
She was quickly beside him—on him, in fact.
And as she alighted on his diaphragm with a blunt
thud the poor lady had just time to say, “lam
innocent!” before his sternum collapsed,
# * *
I used often to wonder why children’s
hospitals should be established —why boj's and
girls couldn’t be treated in institutions for adult
patients as well as in buildings dedicated to
infants alone. I wonder no longer. To explain.
There Were two lads in the Alfred surgical watd
during my stay, and it is no traveller’s tale to say
that their chatter all the day long, and fat into
the night, made many a mature sufferer regret not
having died in infancy. Close upoii this feeling
came the more earnest sorrow that the youths
themselves hadn’t been still-born, or, at least,
tongue-tied in a novel and incurable fashion. At
last, one evening, a tumor man, who’d been
hacked at during the forenoon,. and naturally
yearned for quiet, put all the Venom of a long
life into the question—“ Will nobody squelch
those brats?” During the remainder of my
sojourn he uttered no further query. For One of
the nurses dived from behind a screen with
a bow-wow that turned him on his back, limp and
abject. In her eyes they were not rowdy larrikins
who kept a roomful of sick men in a high state of
nervous tension, but a brace of thoughtful children
who were sure to become useful citizens. _ The
fact is, whether in hospital or outside, it is the
women who are mainly responsible for the
abominable manners of Australian bantlings.
The larrikin will never be suppressed till the
hysterical or slobbering mother', who cuddled his
tender caprices into robust vices, is first put down,
or else relieved of maternal solicitude by the
remoVal of her darlings.
It is open to argument whether, for male acci-
dent wards, male attendants wouldn’t be best. It
is men, not women, who are specially modest. A
woman will submit to bodily examination by a
number of male surgeons, old or young, with far
more composure than a man to a similar experi-
ment by female ones. There are plenty
of men, too, who, through prejudice, or sen-
timent, or some obscure mental peculiarity,
or even the character of the operation undergone
by them, do not take kindly to female minis-
trations. At any rate, had there been male nurses
at the Alfred, the two youths mentioned would
have been heard and pawed less. At the same
time, it is but fair to remember that male patients
always obey a female nurse far more cheerily than
they do a man. When she happens to be fair
and they convalescent, they fetch and carry, scrub,
swab, wash dishes, build fires, roll bandages, and
perform many more objectionable offices at her
bidding. They even take medicine for her, and
look as if'they enjoyed it, which is about the best
proof a man can give of a sincere desire to please.
On the whole, it might perhaps be as well to con-
tinue employing women in male surgical wards,
provided talkative boys were either gagged or
removed.
# # •
Ladies occasionally called at the ward with
flowers and books and tracts. Sometimes they
sang, very sweetly too. But the songs mostly
related to Jerusalem and jasper gates.. Once they
asked our only Chinese patient— he had been
gored by a pig—to favour them with a ditty. He
did,. There was nothing in his countenance or
. voice to show whether the stave was devotional or
amatory ; martial or Anacreontic. But it shifted
the ladies. Orad.ually they stole out one by one
till the daintily-fringed petticoat of the last
flattered in the distance, when from parapet to
i basement of John’s face crept an expression of.
profound peace which said plainly enough, “ alone
L did it.”

How She Died.
Thp moon shone down most dutiful,.
Out of an azure sky,
]7ull on a creature beautiful,
Who slow my hut passed by.
Her limpid eyes were glistening
As though some grief she’d hide
At times she halted, listening,
Anon would onward glide.
An air, she had of haughtiness
(The pride of long descent),
And yet a deed of naughtiness
’Twas on which she was bent. ..
In state, with such celerity,
Towards the shaft she swept
On seeing such temerity
Strong men might well have wept.
She glanced, around affrighted)y.
But climbed the heap o* dirt,
Then cast her eye, delightedly
On my old flannel shirt!
I’d left it out, unthinkingly.
When going home for tea ; .
She lifted it unwinkingly,
And chewed it up with glee.
This was a find encouraging,
But more came after that—
She got, by skilful foraging.
My waistcoat, watch, and hat.
Adown her sweet tesophagus
The flannel shirt they chased,'
And found a safe sarcophagus
Beyond her slender waist.
With appetite diminishing
11 er ardour seemed to flag ;
But wound up all by finishing
That little canvas bag.
The whole earth shook convulsively,
Destruction reigned complete,
And down there fell, impulsively,
(Heat gobs of gore and meat!
The residents of China might
Have hoard the fearful row :
The bag was full of dynamite,
Sire was Smith’s p ley cow !
A. CHEE.

What folly it would be to cut grass with a pair of scissors! Yet peo-
ple do equally silly things every day. Modern progress has grown up
from the hooked sickle to the swinging scythe and thence to the lawn
mower. So don’t use scissors!
But do you use SAPOLIO ? If you don’t you are as much behind the
age as if you cut grass with a dinner knife. Once there were no soaps.
Then one soap served all purposes. Now the sensible folks use one soap
in the toilet, another in the tub, one soap in the stables, and SAPOLIO
for all scouring and house-cleaning.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which yon may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—yon can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In'that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
m the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., andall Business Communications
to be addressed to William llacleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

land, South Australia and Tasmania.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Inter provincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N. S. Wales in future will be £l, with 2s. 2d.
added for postage.
No papers will be sent unless the subscription be pre-
paid. There will be no exception to this rule.
Bulletin Newspaper Co., Ltd.
SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 4, 1893.
The Gasbag Regime.
Another want-of-confidence motion—the
third or fourth during the present session—
looms in the New South Wales Assembly as
we write, and the course of what is humor-
ously described as public “business” pro-
mises to be again blocked for an indefinite
period. Government by Contradiction is in
a bad way in Australia. The time has
arrived when Parliament can accomplish
nothing of any value owing to the ceaseless
rush and bustle of small adventurers hunger-
ing after office. A South American republic
is the emblem of political stability by com-
parison with almost any of the Australian
provinces, and particularly by comparison
with New South Wales, where politics are
fast approaching a permanent deadlock.
Party Government, in its final and complete
state, knows only two alternatives. There
is, first, the normal or orthodox period when
the Opposition opposes everything, and
blocks everything by ceaseless debates, by
want-of-confidence motions, by questions,
motions for adjournment, personal explana-
tions, recriminations, demands for useless
papers, demands for select committees,
amendments, motions that the chairman
do leave the chair, motions that the
chairman do report progress, motions
that the bill before the House be read
that day six months, motions that the
House be counted, and several dozen other
soyts and varieties of stonewalling. This
was the state of things in New South Wales
during the period when Pakkes and Robert-
son shuffled in and out of office alternately,
when the feeble Jenninos-Dibbs Cabinet
held the fort against a small but rowdy and
drunken and venomous Opposition, when
O’Loghlkn attempted to govern Victoria
with a hand of straw, when Maoriland was
getting ready for the Stout-Vooel combina-
tion ; and it is now becoming the permanent
state of things almost everywhere. While
this condition lasts, no measure, however
beneficial and however urgently required, is
passed unless by some extraordinary acci-
dent. And then there follows the inevitable
period of Coalition when every villainous
and fraudulent proposition is passed without
question, when the Parliament feels itself
independent of the nation, and gives up the
last pretence of attempting to do anything
for the public good, and settles down to
hard, continuous, unmitigated swindling of
the most glaring description. But for one
exception in a generation or thereabouts, it
is blank anarchy and highway robbery turn
about.
* # #
Queensland, where the unspeakable Coali-
tion has re-established Kanaka slavery and
the importation of cheap labour at the public
expense, has made the franchise a mere
perquisite of Property, and is engaged in
appropriating for its gang about one-third of
the whole area of the province, is now enjoy-
ing the second of the two alternatives.
Victoria, since the overthrow of the Gillies-
Deakin boom Cabinet, and New South
Wales are in the anarchistic stage. In the
latter province nothing of the slightest public
value has been accomplished for the past
eight years, and during the last four or five
sessions even the hope and pretence of doing
anything seem to have been abandoned.
The Tory press habitually charges the re-
sponsibility for this state of things upon the
Labour Party, but the falsehood of this
accusation is so apparent that it hardly needs
to be refuted. The New South Wales
Labour Party is the only one which has
never attempted to grab at office—which has
never wasted time over want-of-confidence
motions—which has never been an element
of disorder but always one of order. The
final disorganisation of the Legislature dates
from the troubled times of the Stuart and
Jennings-Dibbs Cabinets, when the Foreign-
trade party gave itself up to utter black-
guardism, and the lamented Gibbes, M.L.A.,
occupied the Speaker’s chair with a waste-
paper basket on his head in lieu of a wig,
and when Neild took to making nine-hour
speeches, and Robertson poured forth his
resources of bullock-driving eloquence, and
Parkes blasphemed at the Premier, and the
whole Opposition swore, and yelled, and
cursed, and spat, and drank, and committed
violent assaults, and raised weird and horrible
cat-choruses for 12 months at a stretch.
The system of Party Government reached its
fullest development of uselessness in these
days—the tide rose to high garbage mark,
and, despite an occasional transient ebb and
flow, there has been but little variation ever
since.
# * *
The Bibbs Government—like the Parkes
Government which preceded it—has ob-
viously given up attempting to do anything
save hanging on to its billet, and its hopes in
that direction are not very high. All the old
parliamentarians now practically accept the
inevitable, and expect nothing in order that
they may not be disappointed—it is only
such novices as Shiels, of Victoria, who
come into office with an inflated idea of
doing something great and noble, and leaving
a No. 19 footprint on the sands of politics,
and because Siuels aimed higher than the
majority he fell all the lower and the more
hopelessly. The only country where Party
Government now bear’s any semblance of
practicability is Britain, and it is practicable
there only because the Commons consists, to
all intents and purposes, of Gladstone and
another man, and their followers are dialled
into such absolute obedience that, on all
party questions, they are mere dummies
who might as well leave their px-oxies and
stay away for the rest of their lives. In
other words, Party Government succeeds
only because Parliamentary Government has
been, to all intents and purposes, abolished,
and, except when a great malcontent like
Parnell rises to the surface of events, the
country is run by two practically irrespon-
sible despots.
* # #
If Australia is so deeply devoted to the
old British fetich of Government by Con-
tradiction that it must needs maintain the
venerable incubus regardless of cost, it
might at least be well to devise some modifi-
cation whereby the cumbrous scheme would
be x’endered a little more workable. One
idea which here suggests itself is that the
Legislature should be elected in duplicate.
The duplicate House would contain a
Government and an Opposition and a
Hansard, and all the other necessaries, and
the salaries would be the same as in the
original Chamber. In it all the questions
would be asked, and the personal explanations
made, and there would be a motion for
adjournment every night, and a want-of-
confidence motion once a week, and every-
body who had a personal grievance would be
allowed to bring it up there, and everybody
who had a log to roll would roll it there,
and everybody who wanted a money vote
would get it, and everybody would bo
allowed to call everybody else a liar.
All the members would talk as long as
they pleased. There would be the annual
financial statement, and the usual big
deficiency, and the usual loan would Re
agreed upon to cover the deficiency ; and
the Ministry would have an alleged
policy, and would abandon it after the
customary fashion, and the House would
pass bills, and decide great public
questions, and its proceedings would be
duly reported in the papers just the
same as if it were a i*eal Legislature, and any
member, or editor, or private citizen who
ventured to cast discredit on it, and suggest
that it wasn’t real, should be promptly sent
to gaol for three months, in order to keep
up the public respect for parliamentary in-
stitutions. And the only unreal part of the
business would be that the laws made by the
duplicate Parliament would not be enforced,
and its loans would never be floated, and the
financial statement would refer to imaginary
cash supposed to be in an imaginary Trea-
sury, and nothing would be genuine save
the rows and the stonewalling and the
salaries. The duplicate Parliament would
be provided as a safety-valve for the ventila-
tion of real and imaginary grievances, and a
place for Legislative execration. And, mean-
while, the genuine Assembly would conduct
its proceedings in solemn silence. Nobody
would utter a sound except the individual
addressing the House, and even he would
only be permitted to speak for five minutes.
Nobody would be allowed to move an ad-
journment or a want-of-confidence resolu-
tion, or to make any personal explanation,
or to ask any questions, except in writing,
or to howl or execrate, or to be absent
without leave, under pain of a heavy
fine. In short, as Parliament has not
sufficient time both to legislate and to exe-
crate, and as, for many years past, it has
attended strictly to execration and allowed
legislation to slide, therefore the work would
be divided, so that any member who must
make a scene or burst could proceed to the
duplicate Assembly and make his scene
there, and then come back refreshed and
attend to business once more.
# # #
The only other visible alternative, if Party
Government is to continue, is the sub-divi-
sion of the Assembly into committees some-
what after the plan of the U.S. Congress.
One of these might have sole charge of the
finances, another of public works, a third
might deal with all general measures and
amend, pass, or reject them as it thought
best, and the fourth could devote itself
entirely to questions, motions, general
obstruction, objurgation, and putting out
the Government. Then the decisions of the
committees could be affirmed or negatived
by the whole House without debate, and
thereby the Legislature might at length
succeed in accomplishing something for the
good of the country at large. Failing some
such remedy as this, however, Government
by Contradiction must come to an end at an
early date. If the nation will not adopt the
only remedy which has yet presented itself—
the election of Ministers by the House
for a fixed period—-then Parliament, out
of its own uselessness, will probably die a
natural death. The evil will grow and in-
crease till the Legislature ceases even the
pretence of doing anything, and it will be
left to sit and babble unheeded in its deceased
and unburied condition while the Ministry
runs the State by itself, and the National
Ass and the Trades and Labour Council, or
some similar combinations, take turns at
being the power behind the throne, accord-
ing to the fortune of war or the assumed
sentiments of the Cabinet in office. Mean-
while New South Wales has almost ceased to
care whether the Dibbs Government remains
in office or the Paekes Government rises in
its stead. Ministries in Australia are all
very much alike. They have different pro-
grammes, but as no one of them ever gets a
chance to carry out its programme the ulti-
mate difference is not perceptible. More-
over, very few of them desire to carry out
their programmes. They live by promising
reforms in the blessed certainty that they
would never be allowed to execute them
even if they so desired. In most cases they
do not so desire, but if they did they would
end in dust and ashes just the same.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
On “Esquires” and Other Matters.
Nelson P. Whitelocke, of the Bathurst
National Advocate, an English gentleman, is
obviously feeling ill over' The Bulletin’s
announcement in a recent issue that it proposes
for the future to disregard all Imperial titles
held by Australians. He is a confused indi-
vidual and his woes are difficult to sort out.
The following are a few of Mr. Whitelocke’s
troubles :
When the Sovereign of the Empire has given a subject
a title why should Tin; Bulletin, which circulates by
virtue of registration of the oath of the proprietors to
remain loyal to that Sovereign, refuse to recognise it?
The Bulletin doesn’t circulate by virtue of
any such “ oath,” and has never sworn to be
loyal to any Sovereign, so the Advocate man
is out. It circulates because the public find it
worth buying; if it weren’t worth buying a
whole cartload of oaths wouldn't make it circu-
late. Again :
The Bulletin is thoroughly Australian in. its knowledge
of British social degrees, which means it has little or no
knowledge of them at all. It will, for instance, speak of
the Sovereign of England as “The Queen,” instead of as
“Her Majesty”; and of the Emperor of Germany as
“His Imperial Majesty,” instead of the German Emperor.
The Bulletin does not speak of Victoria as
the “ Queen,” it only speaks of her as “ Queen
Victoria.” If Nelson P. Whitelocke pro-
poses to deny that she is Queen Victoria, then
he had belter do it at his own risk. But this
paper doesn’t allude to her as the “ Sovereign
of England,” seeing that no such title has been
in existence for many generations ; though it
will allude to her that way any time it chances
to feel so inclined. Also, it doesn’t speak of
William of Germany as an “ Imperial Majesty.”
Some more:
The title “enjoyed” by Sir Henry Parkes is just as
much a title in Australia as in England ; every hit as
much a knighthood in British New Guinea as in the
British London ; and a knighthood of England wherever
England’s flag may fly.
Quite so, and if Parkes “ enjoyed ” a boil
instead of a title, it would be a boil not only
all over the British empire, but all over the
world as well. Therefore, if a knighthood is
honourable because it holds good throughout
the empire, a boil is more honourable still,
because it is absolutely cosmopolitan. All the
same, Parkes isn’t a knight of “ England,’
and “England’s” flag doesn’t fly anywhere
Apparently, Whttelocke doesn’t know the
difference between England and the United
Kingdom. Further :
It (The Bulletin) seems to forget that we are not in
dependent, but simply subject to the Throne of England.
We are not. There is no “ Throne of England,’’
but Whitelocke’s history is so loose that he
isn’t aware of the fact. Again :
In those days we will, no doubt, be in a position to
allowTiiE Bulletin or any other paper, to swear allegiance
to the Commonwealth only ; and then, doubtless, the little
pieces that fall into the pockets of the republican pro-
prietors will have something on them suggestive that the
Republic will live long and flourish. At present, we must
all accept them stamped with “ Victoria Dei Gratia."
The Bulletin would respectfully reply just
here that most of the money that comes its way
has nothing about “ Victoria dei Gratia ” on it,
but bears the autograph of a potentate who
seems to be called the “Bank of New Zealand.”
1 hat is a mere detail, however. Mr. White-
LOCKE winds up with the following burst :
“Mr.” is a title of courtesy in most cases, and in certain
others in Great Britain it is, when spelt out, hereditary •
whilst the same thing npplits to “Esquire a title that
is the right of English barristers, sheriffs, and magistrates
on llieir attaining those dignities, but which is more
properly the right of gentlemen of English birth whose
forefathers were “gentle ” by law and who are by reason
of the registration of t ieir families in the Heralds’ Court
entitled to bear those families’ arms—not crests-and’
therefore, assume the dignity of “Esquires.” It may be
a silly thing to observe all these things, but whilst we
live under the Crown that rests thereon, we must observe
the authority, and, what’s more, we can all do that with-
out the slightest saorifii e of our republican desires or our
democratic aspirations.
The Bulletin cheerfully endorses one of Mr,
Whitelocke’s statements, anyhow—it is a
“silly thing” to worry about crests and
armorial bearings and wild beasts rampant and
the like—more than that, it is childish, idiotic,
and supremely ludicrous, and in any case the
“ Crown doesn’t rest “ thereon ” —it might as
well try to rest on a rainbow. Also, Mr.
Whttelocke has considerably mixed up his list
of “esquires,” from which The Bulletin
suspects that he got his alleged information
out of the eneyclopiedia, and inadvertently
strayed into the adjacent article on “Esqui-
maux. However, the difference between an
“esquire” and an Esquimaux is of such small
importance that this discrepancy really doesn’t
matter.
1 hat old-established citizen, “John Smith,”
of Congewoi, writes to Sydney Telegraph to
point out that the N.S. W. education system is
a hideous disgrace to the country at large,
John Smith’s view of the case is so refreshingly
novel as to be worth quoting :
I read your remarks in yesterday’s paper about our
State schools ; this is, I know, a sacred subject, which can
only be mentioned with bated breath, lest the Kiama
ghost should “appear,” as he often did, when the pet
hact had been in danger. For years it has been evi-
dent that the weight of this iniquitous measure would
drag it down, and surely now, when public dishonour is
staring us in the face, we should look at the matter from
a common-sense point of v iew. The system costs now
about ±11,000,000 yearly to educate about 50,000
children—say, ±2O uer head, and it is well known that our
wealthiest people educate their “curled darlings ” at our
high schools ; even our “poor working man,” if he could
do with less beer and tobacco, should'pay for the educa-
tion of his children. We are making paupers of our
people, and, what is worse, the whole scheme was started
in avowed bigotry or hostility to one section of our people,
and since then it has, with the Kiama ghost, been fastened
on us to our utter disgrace.
In the first place, the New South Wales school
system costs just about £750,000 for the educa-
tion of nearly 200,000 (not 50,000) scholars ;
cost per head, about £4 (instead of £2O) per
annum. And if the “ people ” don’t pay this
along with the rest of the taxes then who on
earth does ? The maniacal Smith evidently
thinks that the money comes from some out-
side source, and that a pauperised nation is
being educated out of charity. Possibly he
fancies that Queen Victoria stands the racket.
People like Smith always travel on the assump-
tion that the “ working man’s ” inordinate con-
sumption of beer and tobacco is the cause of
all his troubles, and that if he gave up these
soul-destroying commodities everything would
be well. But as, in the present case, the “ work-
ing-man ’ pays most religiously for his children’s
education the sneer falls flat, and only helps to
display the wretched Smith’s ignorance. When
that individual asserts that “ we are making
paupers of the people it would almost seem as
if he imagined that he paid the hill himself,
and that he (Smith) was not a part of the
people but a separate and distinct institution.
The spectacle of Smith making paupers of the
people is touching, hut as the people are equally
making a pauper of Smith that citizen should
lie low. Who is Conger-eel Smith, anyhow ?
“ Timotheus, ’ in the Argus, drivels concerning
“ the subtle intellect ” of Shiels, and yet sym-
pathises with the member who interrupted
Shiels* dying speech by requesting him not to
talk “rot. Unquestionably the ex-Victorian
Premier talked “ rot” under the delusion that
he was wise as a serpent. His cheap reputation
for cleverness and culture had been taken
seriously by the weak young man, and his
belie! that he confounded the politics of his
opponents witli Latin quotations which they
didn’t understand was quite sincere. But
nothing about Shiels not even Ids conceit—
seemed subtle. As a shuffler he was absurdly
shallow, as a talker he always suggested the*
aspiring tutor who had plodded through
volumes of debates until he felt qualified to’
take a class in oratory. He aimed at being a
diplomatist, and took infinite pains to make a
mysterious mess of everything lie dealt with.
If Shiels ever tried to see a month in front of
him he can have no perceptive power whatever,
for he was invariably unprepared for difficulties
as they came in their due course. His chances
were so many and so showy, he might so easily
have taken a commanding lead in a halt and
maimed Parliament, that his abject failure
can’t bo ascribed to want of pluck, or experi-
ence, or want of anything except ready wits.
False surveys, forged records, suborned and
scoundrelly Consuls, in collusion with lying
interpreters—these things explain the raison
d’etre of the Treaty Powers’ Land Commission
in Samoa. By last mail we are told that
30,000 acres have reverted to the natives. The
history of the earth greed in the Navigators is
that of Maoriland, of the American Indians, of
the Zulus. A native of Samoa, eager to buy a


breech-loader, sells, as he thinks, a small portion
only of his inheritance to some cunning white
trader or trading firm. Nine chances to one
he sold without the consent of other members
of his family. That does not matter, as he will
keep it secret. The buyer and the Consul
concur (we. are speaking of from 1870 to 1880)
and he signs the transfer, which is carefully
read over to him. Then he departs and the
“true and proper record ” is written out by these
heartless swindlers whereby the ten acres poor
Taualoa has parted with at Falifa or Falealili
are made a hundred. Then he is told to call
next day, bring witnesses, and get his money.
They come, and are also asked to witness the
transfer, and the precious deed is then duly
recorded in the consular archives. And as
things were in those days in Samoa, so are they
now in the New Hebrides. Soon there may be
a Land Commission there, and then will be
revealed the same villainy, forgery and anointed
scoundrelism. But in the New Hebrides the
purchasers are not obscure traders, but “verra
respectable” Australian citizens. The black-
labour land-boom has not come off there as yet,
and so the land remains in its primeval state,
but the robbers that “ bought ” it are eagerly
awaiting it. And perhaps it will come, but
with it a Land Commission and—collapse and
exposure.

The “ New Australia ” people, having sensibly
given up the Argentine deserts as hopeless, are
now reported to have obtained a free grant of
some 230,000 acres of at least reasonably good
land in Paraguay. If this information is strictly
true, the prospects for the Australian emigrants
—not for the Australians that are left behind—
are a good deal improved by the change. Para-
guay was a tolerably prosperous country in
days gone by, and has had fewer revolutions
than any other South American republic. When
it started its career as an independent state,
Feancia promptly set up as Dictator, and held
the post unchallenged for 26 years. He was
about the most wonderful tyrant that the
nineteenth century has produced ; he permitted
no Paraguayan to go abroad, and arrested—
and generally executed every prospector,
land - boomer, missionary or other stranger
who entered the country. Also, he established
so wonderfully elaborate a spy system that a
man could hardly even think evil of Feaxgia’s
policy without dying shortly afterwards. All
the same he made the country prosperous, and
died at the age of 84, much regretted. Carlos
Lopez gently annexed his situation in 1844,
and held it with a strong hand for 18 years.
During his time, Paraguay continued to
prosper, and it was a good land for anyone who
didn’t criticise the Government. His son and
successor, Solano Lopez, was a madman, a
drunkard, a frantic murderer, and a sincere
Christian “according to his lights.” He
started a war against Brazil and Argen-
tina, and in the course of five years
Paraguay lost nearly half its territory,
and most of its male population, its towns were
reduced to ashes, and utter ruin was every-
where. Since that time the little State has
wanted men more than anything else, and it is
quite possible that it will welcome any number
of New Australians so long as they help to
build up its shattered prospects and get things
ready, so that the little fire-eating nation may
make a good show in the next war. And when
they start Griffith will go down to the wharf
to see them off, and the Kanakas and the
Chinamen and the cheap Italians and the
coolies will all wish them “ Godspeed ! ”
♦
The New South Wales public-school teacher
now enjoys two years of University life at the
expense of the State. This, it was thought,
would cure him of his excessive pedantry, that
instead of being a man among children and a
child among men he would become a person of
liberal thought and common-sense behaviour.
It was thought that two years daily intercourse
with real University students, the most
democratic youths in the world, would rid him
of those characteristics which expose him at
once to the contempt of his acquaintances and
the derision of his enemies. The result has
not been answerable to the design. Attached
from infancy to one set of persons and one set
of ideas, saturated with an unreasonably exag-
gerated respect for constituted authority, he
finds, when atthe University, that he has nothing
in common with the majority of students. The
inevitable result is that he segregates—he
herds with his kind alone. He and his
fellows carry with them into the Univer-
sity their small primary-school ideas, and
thus intensify that mean, petty, pedagogic spirit
which the regulation was intended to combat.
Before obtrusive, that spirit now becomes
aggressive. He imports his feeble Latinity into
his ordinary conversation. In the tram he
exposes the small section of Cicero he stumbles
through in a year, and blatantly informs his
bored fellow- passengers that he has a whole
Alexandrian library like that to get through.
In his holidays he haunts Schools of Arts, and
indites vapid commentaries on the margins of
periodicals. Also, he generally acts as if the
■whole fabric of the universe were centred in him.
At an expense of £5OOO New South Wales
yearly ejects 30 of these horrid bores upon
suffering humanity.
♦
It is alleged that the Dibbs (N.S.W.) Govern-
ment, having pledged itself by way of adver-
tisement to sundry reforms which it would be
very sorry to see carried out, is now reduced
to putting up its own followers to stonewall its
own measures for fear they should be passed by
accident. Whether the allegation is true or
not, the proceeding is believed to be by no
means uncommon in Australia. Under
such circumstances a strong and thoroughly
coherent Opposition, led by a really strong
chief, could make dreadful embarrassment for
the Ministry. It only requires to enter into a
conspiracy of silence—to roll up in all its
voting power every night, but to say nothing—
to make no speeches, never to move the adjourn-
ment of the House, to propose no want of confi-
dence motions, to ask no questions except such
as are absolutely essential, to make one man do
any speaking that is necessary in the way of
moving amendments, and to restrict him to 15
minutes every night, to make it a cast-iron
rule that all the rest of the eloquence of the
Opposition shall not occupy more than another
15 minutes, to sit in grim silence when anybody
heaves insults at them, to vote solid against
every attempt at early adjournment, and to
yell, “ Go on !” with one voice whenever the
Government shows signs of flagging. And
then the miserable Cabinet would have to do
its own stonewalling, and to do it openly and
glaringly, and have to block its own measures,
and get its own supporters to oppose it, while
a hyena-like laugh would go up from the
Opposition benches.
May not this be written of Queensland by
the future historian :
Slavery was introduced at an early date and flourished.
The result of this influence was to develops in the South
(read North) a system of large, ill-worked manors or
estates. The predominance of slave-labour discouraged
the immigration of free labour, and the South (read
North) remained comparatively thinly settled. The
moral effect of slavery upon the white population was
bad. Habits of thriftlessness and laziness were engen-
dered among the free population, and their social relations
corrupted.— Westel W. Willoughby: “ Government and
Administration of the United States.”
When Minister Lyne, N.S.W. , goes out of
office, his successor should obtain an Executive
Council minute that Mr. Lyne’s pet valuator
should not again be employed while land-valuer
Thompson and his expensive valuation staff
remain on the Government pay-rolls. Either
Thompson is superfluous or it is a scandal to
employ the other man. By the way, the full
value of an Executive Council minute as a means
for trammelling the hands of a successor in Minis-
terial office has never been sufficiently appreciated
in N.S.W, In another province, though, its force
has been quite understood and applied.
According to a workers’ authority :
A cablegram from the New Australia prospectors states
that the forty leagues (230,400 acres) secured are in
Paraguay, lat. 25deg. 70min. S. (sic) and lon. 56deg.
50min. W. This is exact. The adjoining forty leagues
(230,400 acres) have been also promised to the association.
The land is free of charge on settlement conditions, near
navigable water and described as “splendid land, well
watered and timbered.”
25deg. 70min.! It sounds like 195 minutes past 13.
And in any case the place described is only a
“point” on the map, and 230,400 acres can’t be
crowded into something that has neither length
nor breadth.
The S.M. Herald prides itself on having
discovered that State services in railways, post
and telegraph matters are not wholly socialistic,
indeed, the Herald says that they are not
socialistic at all. The Herald, as usual, is just
half right. Such enterprises are socialistic in so
far as they are founded and carried on at public
expense, and because the returns go into the State
purse. They are not socialistic because the wages
of those employed thereon are very unequal and
do not rise or fall with increase of profits, or
otherwise. But, above all, they are not socialistic
because the increase of values given to privately-
owned land by such public expenditures invariably
goes into the pocket of its owner and never into
the purse of those—the entire community—re-
sponsible for its creation.
A cable tells us how a Mauritius loan of
<£ooo,ooo, bearing only 3 per cent., has been
subscribed for eight times over at an average of
<£los. Doubtless the Property organs are pre-
pared to raise hosannas over this. Yet what does
it really mean ? That slavery, as a money-making
institution, is one of the revered idols of Britain.
Mauritius depends chiefly on sugar-growing, and
its sugar is cultivated by “cheap and reliable ”
Coolie slaves. For similar greedy reasons, Queens-
land sugar-planters want to run their province
with Kanaka slaves. If the Kanaka slave once
became the recognised staple labourer of the
North, Queensland debentures would probably go
up and its loans would be hungered after ; but the
white worker would have to disappear from the
land. Successful loans abroad bring no happiness
to the workers in Australia, no food to their
homes, no money to their pockets. The fat man
gets fatter and more oppressive. That is all.
This is indeed almost a truth—abolish loans and
you abolish poverty. Work it out by process of
inversion.
Editor Bulletin, —Mr. J. A. Andrews’
attempt to prove Communism and Anarchy
to be identical cults afforded me much
interest, inasmuch as he came very near success.
There is, however, this vital and irreconcileable
divergence between them— i.c., that while both
aim at the perfection of individual autonomy in
society, the Anarchist, as the representative of
the strong, self-confident, egoistic and reckless
members of the community, considers that each
individual would obtain the maximum of pleasure
derivable in this life as an independent free lance.
The Communist, representing the cautious, altru-
istic, and lymphatic temperament, holds that
only by a series of periodical comparisons and dis-
cussions re his views and those of his more
vigorous (and possibly less scrupulous) fellows,
after which a common policy could be decided
on, would his ends best be served. The outcome
of Anarchy would be the formation of innumer-
able syndicates of persons agreed on certain lines
of action, among which the interests of the weaker
portions of society would receive no consideration
whatever. Certainly there would be no written
laws to oppress, but the unwritten ones— i.e., the
fluctuating and irregular desires of the various
syndicators—would probably soon reduce society to
the depths of barbarism. —Yours, &0., Charles
JUNOR.
The eternal conflict between the Eatans-
will Gazette and its foe, the Independent, was
a bright and sparkling matter compared with the
latest outbreak of hostilities on the fiscal question
between the Age and the Argus. The two ,
“great ” Melbourne papers roll heavy, lumbering
articles on the fiscal question at one another, and
on one and the same > morning the unfortunate
constant reader is called upon to dodge such
missiles as these: “Refutation of Freetrade
Statements” (from the Age). “The Freetrade
organ in Melbourne, which during the last few
weeks has been attacking the protective system of
the colony, has apparently experienced consider-
able difficulty in supporting its cause, and has
been driven to make wild and inaccurate state-
ments.” And so on for 2£ cols, concerning the
boot industry. “Protection Pantomimes ” (the
Argus) ; “ The interesting imitation of the Xmas
pantomime is still on show- in the pages of your
monopolist contemporary. The mental contor-
tions, enlivened by stale jokes and startling
assertions ” —and so on for 2 cols, of triumphant
“ refutation ” of Protectionist maundering. It is
an awful thing, this interchange of scorn a,nd
contumely by morning journals, both of which
might more usefully employ their nughty intellects
on the mystery of the Arms and Legs, or minute
details of the latest thing in indecent assaults.
If the Argus and Age must rage together, why
can’t they have it out over murder cases, allowing
their respective special reporters to controvert the
other theory as to the exact causes that led up to
the corpse? An argument wherein one organ dis-
cusses boots whilst the other goes back to the
Corn Laws, is but a sorry contribution towards
the education of a young country. Indeed, it is a
shocking waste of animosity. Now, why doesn’t
the Argus publish a daily list of the Protectionist
members who were drunk in the ’Ouse overnight?
Then the Age would call attention to the fact
that another Freetrade legislator had secretly
compounded with his creditors, and the public
might soon discover which kind of settled policy
was best for trade.
We Want to See the Will.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Once, Adam had to delve the ground, and sweat
was on his brow,
But that was ere he multiplied—there’s quite a
diff’rence now,
For Adam only worked to live, and now it seems
to be—
Some only live to work, and some just live to look
and see.
If Adam only worked to live, it’s plain enough
that we
Should only have to do the same and be like him
as free
To work and live, or loaf and die, to feel life was
worth living.
Or not, just as our conscience took the gift some
god was giving.
If Adam’s sons have got his right to work on earth
and live,
What means this sick’ning cry we hear so many
of them give—
A cry to be allowed to work, a prayer from man
to man,
A breath, but what a hell on earth that breath —
for some—can fan !
That legacy that Adam left—the sweat upon the
brow—
Is more than his executors grant all his offspring
now.
A force is slowly pressing out the men who live to
toil,
Like horses, for their fault’s the same—their food
1 _• costs more than oil.
What makes some people stand upon their hinder
legs and say,
“Free Competition and Freetrade will give us
work alway,”
When ey’ry year “so much ” per cent, of all the
human race
Is now supplying more than full per cent, can
well displace ?
There’s something that we have to-day, that Adam
never had,
A trouble that he never knew, his sons have got it
bad,
For Adam had no rent to pay—the earth was all
his own—
His big estate was left entailed, and so the trouble
sown.
We want to see the title-deeds tlvit Adam left
behind,
We think there’s been some swindling done —we’re
hung’ring just to find
If ever Adam’s will was proved, and if it’s not
the truth
He left a hunk of Windsor Park to be submerged
by Booth.
We want to know if Coopers have the right to
close in land
With simple hoops of legal tape, and where on
earth we’ll stand
If they should form a union that will make the
earth a “ trust; ”
We want to know where ice come in, and see the
will we must.

The 400 odd French deputies who refused
to bite at the Panama bribe bait were mostly the
poorer members of the Chamber, ic is said. Living
well within their modest means they could better
afford to be honest than could the “representa-
tive” persons, who are “ repreeentative ” just as
long as they incur heavy social expenses.
Captain F. C. Rowan, Australian repre-
sentative of the Brush Electric Co,, who recently
died at Melbourne, was born in 1844, in Antrim,
Ireland, was educated for the army at Woolwich,
and in ’G3 joined the 59th regt., at Aldershot.
In ’64 he joined the 43rd Light Infantry,
and went to Maoriland where he served with
bravery and distinction during the war, and
saw a great deal of active service. He led the ,
attack to the right, on Te-Ngatu-o-te-rnanu.
Major Von Tempsky, Capt. Buck, Capt. Palmer,
Lieuts. Hastings and Hunter were killed during ,
the action, and while carrying the latter under
fire, Capt. Rowan was severely wounded by a
bullet which shattered the left jaw, cut through
the muscles under the tongue and made its exit at
the right side of the neck. In 1877 became to Mel-
bourne where he engaged in engineering pursuits.
Ho afterwards assumed the management of the
Australasian Electric Light Co,, and subsequently
became the representative throughout Australasia
of the Brush Electric and Engineering Co. He
left Sydney in good health on 2nd Dec., but
caught a severe cold on the overland journey; ■
acute pneumonia set in and he died on the ilth. He ■
married that well-known painter and popular lady,
Marion Ellis Ryan, eldest daughter of Charles
Ryan, of Derroweit, Macedon, Vic., and leaves one
son, Eric, aged 16. He held the post of Consul-
General for Denmark for many years and ;
took the keenest interest in all literary and i
artistic pursuits. He wrote and spoke well, !
was a generous man in thought and action, a keen
sportsman and an accomplished athlete. In his *1
own regiment he went by the name of “Hercules.” 51

will become a new art with the incoming of the
Empire gown. Lovers have grown accustomed to
the unyielding circle of the corseted waist. They
have not complained. In fact, they have thought,
when they sat in bliss, their girl within the clasp
of their protected embrace, that they were having
all they could expect in the way of a good time.
The Empire gown will give them more. They
will feel the gentle yielding of an untrammelled
waist. Just how they are going to like it, they
only know. Maybe, a word of advice may come
in here. For one thing, the girl is not going to
be half so hard to manage as she used to be. She
will be able to bend now. Another thing, she
really could not put her arms about a man’s neck,
as, to do her justice, she often wished to, because,
you see, her tight waist and sleeves would not
allow it. Men have thought her cold and avvk-

My Dear Moorabinda —
Apropos of the all-absorbing topic—the resigna-
tion and departure of the Jerseys—we have had
columns about Gov. Jersey’s reasons for going
away, but nobody tells us specifically what every-
body is literally dying to know—why Lady Jersey
is packing her bag? One can see many water-
tight reasons why Lady Jersey should gather her
chickens around her and fly to a safer clime.
“Yillars”is getting a big boy now, and will be
legally qualified to marry a chorus-girl in a very
short while—therefore, it is unsafe to have him
lying about loose for any designing Nobody to
pick up and appropriate. Tottie—her ladyship
probably argues—you have always with you,
wherever you go, but her well-known wiles are
not half so dangerous in a wide atmosphere as the
possibly original arts exercised in our Little
Pedlington by some attractive Miss Impossible or
other, who has to be given the run of the premises.
And then, there are demure, light-haired Jersey
girls to be reckoned with. You can’t bring up
your daughters in an absolutely friendless state,
and Lady J.’s experiences of the barn-dancing
coterie (about whom, en parenthese, many new and
shocking stories continually crop up), made her
shy of “colonial” girl-friendships for her duck-
lings. So, as their frocks are creeping downwards,
it is time to transplant them to a land where
young ladies don’t break loose so unmistakeably—
and, besides, isn’t it just possible, in the opinion
of the worldly-wise, that Australia is too many
thousands of miles away from the marriage-market
for the mother of a girl of sixteen ? Lady Jersey’s
reasons for leaving this horrid country would be
far more to the point than Lord Jersey’s defunct
agent—which interesting corpse has, of course,
Kot about as much to do with it as a dead donkey
has with the morning coffee and toast of yours
truly.
There is at least one vice-regal entertainer who
can bear the early departure of the Jerseys with
great fortitude, and that is the unhappy individual
who entertained young Lord Viiliers at his
country residence for a little shooting. The host
—so the story goes—had a couple of pet kangaroos.
On the first morning the energetic Viscount spied
these innocents at their gambols, and thinking
that all marsupials were game in Australia, mis-
took them for prey.
To hunt up “my gun ” and start in hot pursuit
was the work of an instant, and as the tame
animals were accustomed to regard man with an
unsuspicious eye the victim just sat upon his tail
and folded ins forepaws complacently while Lord
V. took deadly aim and bagged bis host’s pet
wallaby in the click of a trigger. The scene that
followed is easily imagined—and peace was only
restored by an effusive letter full of “ so sorry’s ”
and “very much regrets,” backed up by the gift
of another kangaroo, broken in to lawn-work, to
take the slaughtered innocent’s place.
Judging by the fatal aptitude for potting
confiding domestic animals (probably because
they don’t bother your aim by running away)
displayed by visitors from ’Ome who are given
the freedom of a gun—it would not be a bad
plan for the Australian host to hang tags on to bis
livestock, from the family cow to the chickens (or
even to the baby and the cockatoo in the cage and
the family dog). “Please Don’t Shoot,” or “ Be-
ware of the Pet,” ought to give pause to the
densest sportsman who ever drew a bead on a bay-
stack.
The early departure of the Jerseys for the land
from whence they came but two short years ago,
isn’t by any means an unexpected calamity. For
one thing, their sightseeing was too indefatigable
for people who intended to stay the whole period.
When you come to calmly look at it, there are
many less pleasant ways of doing Australia
than by occupying a Government House for a
year or so and radiating round—and the Jerseys’
plan, accidental or otherwise, is worth the notice
of the itinerant aristocrats who are dying to
“do” us and don’t want to be entirely at the
mercy of the Australian hotels.
For my part, I think a frequent shuffle of
Governors would be the very thing to sustain
public interest in Government House and keep
things lively all round. There are certain people
who never get on paste-board terras with vice-
royalty, but who always live in hope—and to such
each new and unsuspecting potentate is a field of
unlimited social possibilities. Nothing much
comes of him as a general thing, because somebody
always interprets the visitors’ book for the new
secretary’s benefit, but he keeps a lot of people up
to concert-pitch until he’s given a ball or two. It
is always interesting to watch the manoeuvring.
Sydney “Ministering Children’s League”—the
title of which alone is calculated to turn out more
prigs than a pattern ladies’ seminary—held a
meeting recently, when it appeared that the
League is in great going order. “ This great
growth of enthusiasm in the work,” it candidly
confesses, “is attributable, in a large measure, to
the visit of the Countess of Meath.” How
familiar that sounds ! The St. John’s Ambulance
movement filled the country with reckless maidens
who smashed their umbrellas into splints on the
slightest provocation—because Lady Brassey bad
a fad that way. Lady Carington got up a howling
enthusiasm for the Women’s Jubilee Fund and
every second girl you met was “collecting ” while
little Lady C. actively mothered the idea—but
isn’t it rather sickening to find that even the
babes and sucklings regulate the fervour of their
crazes according to the titles in the prospectus ?
It detracts a great deal from their philanthropy
that they should only rally round the League
because a Countess had a hand in it —and, viewed
in that light, the statement that 731 articles of
apparel were cobbled and so many worn clothes
patched by the League has no charitable signi-
ficance whatsoever. Those rags were constructed
and mended not for the poor and seedy, but for
the Countess of Meath. The Ministering Children
are being trained, not as angels of mercy, but as
poisonous little snobs.
Miss Helen Dight, who, together with the
amiable and popular Mrs. E. M. Mort, was so un-
fortunately drowned at Bodalla, last week, was
probably the most strikingly handsome belle of
Sydney society. She would indeed have made an
excellent representative of an Australian type of
beauty. At the Sydney balls last year she looked
a veritable queen among the somewhat squat and
dumpy damsels whose names may be seen hustling
for the very front places in the fashion reports
Why don’t women leave well alone? Ere the
female-suffragists began to insist upon feminine
brilliancy men took us sugar-coated, and perhaps
were ready to believe that we were like the
monkeys—full of unrevealed possibilities concealed
for diplomatic reasons. But since my dear sisters
have gone in for public undertakings and got into
the most hopeless tangles in the process, it has
gradually become a painful and evident fact that
women on the average don’t go much deeper than
the surface, and can impress men far mo«e effec-
tually by lying low and simply trying to look
clever.
The fact that a well-known Sydney Judge has
had to give his lOU for the whole of the sub-
scribers’ money in a recent feminine undertaking
encourages me to advance the above heresy.
There was a long subscription-list on which Mrs.
Blank wrote herself down for two figures, and
Miss Blank was very liberal on paper—but col-
lecting-day told another tale, and there was abso-
lutely no money to pay the piper ! Not, mind
you, that I blame the committee for piling all the
damages on to a man. Woman has been taught
to regard him merely as a creature with an auto-
matic arm for sliding in and out of his pocket, and
she is only obeying her inherited instincts when
she “goes through him ” like a bushranger. But
I do think the untrammelled female who cuts
an independent dash to show the superfluity of
the masculine half of creation ought not—no,
she really ought not —to fall back upon him in
the old, old way when the bill comes in.
Sydney Turf Club races, at Rand wick, on Anni-
versary Day, were a very lively episode, although
the M.S. attendance would have stood a little
thickening and the stand was overrun with
infants. “Is it a racecourse or a day-nursery?”
asked a Melbourne visitor, and really when it
comes to a baby-in-anns propping itself up on its
two bandy legs and applying a huge pair of race-
glasses to its fishy eye, one has a conviction that
“infants in arms not admitted” might have a
beneficial effect writ large over the gate—if only
to postpone the advent of the “ Baby Bookmaker”
or the “Juvenile Juggins” hypothecating his
rattle or teething-ring to “put something on the
j'Rger.”
Mrs. Harry Levien wore the most ginger dress
visible. It was a shot opal on a grey ground and
trimmed with effective linings of opal cream lace
applique ; picture bat of black velvet trimmed
with jet buckles and black feathers and a couple
of mauve orchids. Mrs. Sydney Burdekin wore a
grey-greenish “chow” costume trimmed with
cream lace and sash of black moire ; Mrs. (Dr.)
Sly got up a Dresden china effect in pale-grey
with a pink spot and trimming of pale-pink silk
covered with black lace. Miss Burdett-Smith, in
semi-mourning, came with Miss M‘Donagh in
maize mull with a pink pattern, and hat covered
with crushed strawberry feathers. Mrs. S. A.
Moss, recently returned from a trip ’Ome,
sported a well-cut black Bengaline with big sleeves
and yoke of blue silk covered with black lace, hat
cn suite. Myra Kemble had a glove-fit in ecru silk
lustre. The Misses Williamson looked natty in
brown checks, with coarse straw hats trimmed
with ribbons and quills ; but a charming society
girl turned out as yellow as an orange under a
veil of violent violet. The human visage was
never meant to be shrouded in the festive tints of
a chemist’s window, and those who do so do it at
their own risk.
“Uncles and Aunts” was revived at Sydney
“Royal,” last week, in the presence of a well-
dressed circle. There was only one artistic frock
on the stage —Mollie Browne’s tea-gown of yellow
and white. I can’t understand the dressing in
“ Uncle and Aunts ” —in one act one lady is lurid
in red velvet and satin dinner dress—another
sports a tea-gown, and another is in ordinary
morning dress ! Also Harry Hodson’s method of
eating a stage fish was an astonishment —but stage
eaters are proverbially original. They speak with
a full mouth, leave their spoons in their cups—-
and dine in two seconds. Bellew’s “ Scarpia ”
was the only naturally-deliberate feeding man I
have ever seen on the stage.
From W. H. Paling and Co. I have received
two songs in one cover—“ The Course of True
Love,” and “The Vision ’’—music by Frank E.
Down, to words by Heine, “ The Course of True
Love ” is the more successful of the two, but both
are pleasing melodies set for light tenor or mezzo-
soprano voices, and are just the length of the
ideal encore.
The interprovincial cricket-match attracted in-
spiriting crowds to Sydney Association Ground.
The gathering on Anniversary Day was absolutely
phenomenal in size, and last Saturday—the third
day—people hung on to their Members’ Stand
chairs with Melbourne-Cup-like tenacity. A
multitude of gay garments were brought out by
the beautiful day, and the bright, well-kept little
garden “wasn’t in it” for colouring with the
garden of girls—altho’ one of them did look rather
odd with her head tied up like a dumpling in
thick white gauze, and two damsels in skittle-fib
blue and pink of the crudest colour, were dread-
fully like a pair of cheap vases.
Mrs. Brough—who came with Miss Emma
Temple—looked charming in China-blue cashmere
trimmed with black and silver gimp—and black
lace and bonnet. A millionaire’s wife sported
cream silk, draped with biscuit lace and a Tuscan
bonnet—but there was nothing very new on show.
The woi’ld is now knocking down its summer
frock, which generally limp garment hasn’t much
heart in it at the best of times after a day’s wear.
The spectacle of the day was afforded by a gor-
geous male robed in spotless white right down to
his boots, relieved by a crimson silk sash and a
hat-band in three flaring colours. When you had
got over the impression that he was a Cyclorama
“ sandwich ” he looked quite fetching.
“Much Ado About Nothing ” was strictly V.R.
on 27th ult., when Gov. and Lady Jersey turned
up in the curtained square of patronage —where,
by the way, no managerial bouquets were laid on
as a shelter for weary yawning. The white satin
bills —like little bits of rag—were the only differ-
ences observable on the face of the Criterion —
except that I thought I remarked a tendency on
the part of the talented company to slightly bob
—like a devout religionist in front of a crucifix —
towards the vice-regal box on first entering. Lady
Jersey wasn’t particularly smart in pale-blue
brocade —also, she recognised nobody, despite the
fact that one or two matrons made elaborate duck-
ings in her direction and retired hot and disgusted
behind their fans for the rest of the show. If
there is one accomplishment “ M.E. J.” will leave
behind her in these outlandish parts it is the
mesmerised look of an absolutely unseeing eye. It
is rather comical, by the bye, to see the woes of
vice-regal expression pass over the features of
“Society.” When a facially-amiable woman is
lording it over us then everybody grins at you like
a hungry dingo—but when Lady Blank’s working-
day expression only wants two degrees more stoni-
ness to pass for a petrification—why, then you
have your Block peopled with Sphinxes.
By the way, that was rather a blushful accident
which lately happened to a Queensland legal
dignitary as he was publicly—oh, so publicly—
bidding adieu to some ladies who came to see him
off the other day. And the moral of it seems to
me to be —“ Beware of patent braces.”
The engagement of the attractive Miss Nellie
Hill, of Sydney, to Mr. “Jack ” Wagner, of Mel-
bourne, is announced.
Queen Victoria, I read, stood godmother to
Lady Carington’s new baby.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.

“Falka” departed from Sydney Royal on
Friday night for the place, wherever that place
may be, where the unappreciated comic operas go,
and on Saturday “Uncles and Aunts ” was pre-
sented under the control of a new company. The
new company is apparently run on commonwealth
principles, for all the names on the programme
are printed in the same type, and no one poses as
a particular star in big letters. “Uncles and
Aunts ” is a shadowy and somewhat unsatis-
factory comedy ; it has bursts of wild humour, but
the motive of things isn’t so apparent as it might
be, and the bursts are disconnected, and there
aren’t enough bursts anyway. The audience is
always catching a shadowy glimpse of some-
thing that never developes, like a transient
vision of a camel in a thick fog. There
are fleeting views of a wondrously comic person
named ’Kiah who wants to marry a pretty girl for
no definite reason, and ultimately jilts her for a
swollen old woman and for reasons that are still
less definite. ’Kiah, like the comet, travels very
fast and doesn’t stay long, and for some very in-
adequate cause he tells tremendous lies which only
lead to his immediate discomfiture. There is
also a vague outline of a girl who affects to go
mad in a wildly grotesque fashion but without any
coherent obje it; a bright red uncle who objects to
a marriage trim motives not stated ; an aunt who
objects for no motive at all and who talks in her
sleep, a father who doesn’t turn up, but who is
also understood to object, another uncle who
never appears at all, and yet one more
uncle who, for some purpose that no one
even attempts to define, personates the first uncle,
who is also personated by ’Kiah. There is some-
thing particularly ungraspable about all these
uncles, and the relationship between them and
the two overgrown aunts is slippery and elusive.
Also, on the first night, several members of the
company seemed to suffer from a serious want of
rehearsal, and the cry of the prompter was heard
in the land. Mr. Tom Cannam, however, is ex-
cepted from this reproach ; his 'Kiah is one of the
most brilliant things he has attempted, and if it
was built on the foundations of a more substantial
comedy might almost take rank with the boneless
curate of the “ Private Secretary.” The want of
foundation damages it ; “Uncles and Aunts” is
a comedy that was evidently picked before it was
ripe. Messrs. Leopold and Hodson, two of the
unaccountable uncles, are always reliable and are
calculated to lend strength to any company ; and
Mr. Diver’s expressive eye shows no signs of
decadence, though his character is six sizes, or
more, too small for him. Also, Miss Alice May
fills a fascinating girl-part, and she and Mr.
Leopold share all the applause that is going.
Despite the merits of the company, “Uncles and
Aunts ” isn’t going to have an extended run. Its
one incurable fault is that it doesn’t last long
enough to allow the uncles to explain themselves,
and that ’Kiah is hustled off the stage before he
has time to offer satisfactory cause for his conduct,
while two men who might throw some light on
things never appear at all. One of them sends a
letter, but even that is short and incomprehensible.
*» * »
“London Day by Day,” at Sydney Her
Majesty’s, is a bulky and imposing spectacle with
many gorgeous effects, and the usual moral
that the wicked shall be suddenly cut off.
The story is reasonably new, and the characters
are tolerably ancient. There is the inevitable
villain, half-cockney, half Hebraic, who snuffles
comic observations through his nose, and perspires
profusely, and groans piteously in the upper end
of his nasal orifice. Lionel Rignold plays this
part with eclat, but all Lionel’s humour up to
date has been more or less of the costermonger
description, and The Bulletin still waits
anxiously to see him in some character that
doesn’t bear the imprint of Whitechapel. There
is the inevitable villain in evening - dress and
broken English, represented by Albert Nor-
man ; and the massive hero, who is done by
George Rignold. The latter looks in better
health than he did, and plays well, and
is as nervously anxious about his hat as
usual—Mr. Rignold’s hat always weighs on his
mind more or less. The suffering, injured, but
trustful girl is supplied by Miss Hilda Spong, who
looks as pretty as ever; and the deserted wife of
the foreigner with the battered accent and the
evening dress is played effectively by Miss Kate
Bishop. Also, Miss Watts. Phillips appears as the
best girl of that supremely objectionable person,
the comic lout with the red hair, and The Bul-
letin’s angry passions rise as he paws her about
and squints lovingly at her. The balance of the
exhibition consists mainly of J-. W. Sweeney’s
gigantic and refreshing Irishman, also the
policeman, the inspector, the coffee-stall female,
the crossing-sweeper, the big flunkey, the
brainless lord, the broken-down lawyer, and
the miscellaneous females who are out late at
night, presumably for nefarious purposes.
¥6 * * *
Shakespeare retires from Sydney Criterion
this week after a career which ought to warm the
soul of any dramatist, and “ The Times ” takes
his place on Saturday. Also, the new act-drop is
to be lowered that night for the first time. A
full account of this ravishing rag will appear next
week. The Lyceum pantomime shows no sign of
age or decay, and continues to look with a painted
front down a long avenue of prospective success.
Rickards’ minstrel-show holds the Opera House
with satisfactory results.

Coogee Aquarium made a special effort on
Anniversary Day, and held a variety of olde
Englyshe sports,'including a cigar-smoking con-
test, grinning through a horse - collar the
ugliest grin to win, picking half-a-sovereign out
of a bowl of treacle—the competitor to use his
countenance only and the winner to keep the coin,
doing ditto out of a bowl of flour, bun-eating com-
petition, &c. The Bulletin still waits hopefully
for news of the death of the successful bun-eater,
but that heavy-laden individual is reported to be
getting the better of his dyspepsia and coming
round.

Royal was the last visitation of ill-luck upon the
head of a lady who deserved better fortune.
The company did its part manfully, but an
execrably poor audience formed only a few
islands in the great ocean of deserted seats, and
blank misery sat heavily on them in their loneli-
ness. The Bulletin here drops a painful subject
** * *
“ Tommy” Hudson, after 11 years’ absence
from Sydney, opened yet another minstrel-show
at the Garrick on Saturday. As a rule, one
minstrel-show is very like another, and “ Tommy’
Hudson’s programme presents the same unfailing
resemblance to itself which characterises all the
rest. The company is particularly strong, how-
ever, in the musical and vocal line, and if the
murky end-men and their conundrums were
abolished, a most respectable opera troupe might
be made out of the balance. Miss May Habgoqd
is a musician of a very high order, and Miss
Isabel Webster (both ladies hail from the Royal
College ot Music) and a singer far above the usual
variety-show standard. The same may be said
of Misses Busch and Roberts, and “Tommy 1 '
himself was built for better, though, perhaps, less
profitable, things. The show was received on the
opening night with enthusiasm, almost every item
being encored, and several being demanded three
times. Even the fact that some of the conundrums
of the corner-men obviously belong to the vintage
of years gone by, didn’t make any appreciable
difference.
