SOHO SQUARE, LONDON.
Fillis’ Circus, which, on its artistic merits
alone, would have repeated the Sydney success in
Melbourne, secured an irresistible advertisement
on its opening night. The boss lion, as everybody
knows, decided to “gag” his part by knocking
Cantain Russell down, after which, inspired by
the yell of excitement that greeted his new busi-
ness, Pasha made a playful bite at his fellow
tragedian, and the triumph of the new Circus was
complete. Deep down in every human breast is
implanted a desire to be present when something
happens to the lion-tamer, so as soon as a ghastly
element of uncertainty crept into this last item on
the bill the multitude began to roll up with dis-
tended nostrils, sniffing for a sanguinary sacrifice.
Pasha, a naturally peaceful beast bred in cap-
tivity, has studied mankind to some purpose, and
his nightly wink at the management, after he has
gazed around and made a mental calculation of
the money in the tent, is full of meaning. No
wonder that his salary has been raised several
ribs of beef per diem. Next to Pasha in public
estimation comes Fillis, whose joyous monkey act
seems very little short of a full-grown miracle.
The infant Bertie on the high trapeze, the senti-
mental clown, and the vision that flitted through
hoops, were likewise received with enthusiasm
during the first week of the show. This week
Madame Fillis is a leading feature in the change
of programme, and the famous Water Pantomime
looms in the near distance.
** * *
Meanwhile pantomime is going the way of
the Roman Empire which declined and fell. Even
a second edition of “ Forty Thieves ” is as naught
compared with the possibilities of a hungry lion,
and the humour of the average harlequinade pales
beside five elephants standing oh tubs. The
second edition of the Princess’s pantomime would
probably be an improvement on the first if it in-
.oluded the old original ass. On Saturday last
.another performer was wearing the skin of the
-star donkey, and the show seemed a lot worse for
>the change. Apart from this calamity there were
signs that an earlier revising of the business might
'have delayed the day of doom for the W. and M.’s
.gorgeous delusion. More use is now made of
Bnazelle, who breaks out in operatic selections
and divides a topical song with Lonnen. It isn’t a
very catchy topical song, but it takes the place of
something that was much more painful, and
arouses a feeling of gratitude accordingly. “Forty
Thieves” may possibly recover popular favour
before the final curtain-bell rings, inasmuch as the
'rival panto, promises to die at an earlier date.
Wednesday matinees are still advertissd, and on
Friday next (Feb.:3) the drama will start at seven
•o’clock, for the benefit of children who might
•otherwise,fall asleep ere their rapture terminated.
-» * * *
“Babes in the Wood” has also been im-
proved to the extent of a harmonious whistler and
a few-other matters. Moreover, Shine has publicly
apologised to Bella Bashall, a circumstance which
adds a little human interest to the festive scene.
The ancient kings of England continue to weaken
on acquaintance (most of them being very palpable
'girls when you look hard at their personal appear-
ance), but Ada Bemister manifests a noble ambi-
tion to succeed in something before the season
•ends. And when a fine girl, in an astounding hat,
begins to let go, she usually gets there, more or
tess. One more week will bring the poor old
■Royal annual to a not inglorious end. After that
—the Deluge.

Frank Clark, who was a plucky importer
■df music-hall novelties for some years, took a fare-
well benefit at Melbourne Opera House on Satur-
'day, when his former services to the perfesh and
'his character for always facing adversity instead
•of bolting with the tail end of the cash-box, were
•recognised in a liberal manner before and behind
'the curtain. Frank Clark is off to America with-
out any fixed intention of returning, but he will
■ever keep a warm place in his heart for Australia,
'because it is the birthplace of the man who gnawed
this ear.

116th under the direction of W. A. Jinkins. It
appears that the production of babies has been in

a brilliant display of fatted calves is confidently

cases, pawning their clothes to nourish the only

Fred. Leslie left Nellie Farren a legacy of £2OOO. It is
rumoured that she has quite gone off her head with grief
at his death.
Who wouldn’t own a boxing kangaroo ? The gay mar-
supial now on show at Westminster draws large crowds
nightly and is said to earn for his owner no less than
£24,000 a year. This is more than any two-legged pugilist
can do even with the help of mammoth purses. The
no-called boxing between the kangaroo and his ovvnt r is
an outrageous piece of buffoonery; but then, English
‘people are easily pleased, arid it is necessary to play down
’to the level of their intelligence.
The story goes that after Leslie’s death Danby
ordered his things to be taken up to Fred’s dressing-room
’it London Gaiety. On being asked why he had done so
'ais reply was, “lam now the leading comedian of th’
Gaiety.” This was promptly told to the management, and
the “ leading comedian ” was requested to take his things
back again. He demurred, and demanded that he should be
considered Leslie’s successor, threatening, in case of
refusal, to leave the show. The refusal came, and Danby
thereupon ceased his connection with the theatre. But
reflection brought home to him the folly of his hasty
ultimatum ; he sought to be restored to his position, but
met with a polite refusal.
Writes The Bulletin’s London correspondentPenley
has a fortune in “Charley’s Aunt," a farcical comedy by
Brandon Thomas, which I ventured to predict in your
columns months ago would be a big success. It is cer-
tainly the best and funniest thing of the sort which has
been ever produced in London, and can give points to the
“ Private Secretary.” The first performance in London,
even though it took place at that little hole, the Royalty,
was a thing to be remembered. I have never in all my
experience seen so enthusiastic an audience, and from
start to finish they were kept in a continuous roar of
laughter. In any other hands but Penley’s there would
have been a risk that the character of “ Charley's Aunt”
might degenerate into vulgarity, but the clever little
comedian managed to steer clear of anything of the kind,
and what in other hands would possibly have been offen-
sive, was simply an admirable piece of comic acting.
Penley will probably make £40,000 or £50,000 out of it.
It will be sure to have a two years’ run in London and
the provinces, and then you may have a chance of seeing
it in Australia.
Fanny Enson has a London engagement in George
Alexander’s company, and plays the feline-widow line of
character as of yore.
The Fitzgerald Bros, have bought a a fine lioness and
three splendid six-months-old cubs from Sydney Zoo.
(where, like their parents, the interesting animals were
bred), and are in treaty with that fetching tamer,
Madame Scherazade, late of Fillis’s Circus. The
Fitzgeralds’ lions, after a severe course of train-
ing, will presently appear on horseback and other
unlikely places, in addition to going through the time-
honoured cage tricks. Meanwhile, there is a large fortune
sticking out for anybody who can leach a crocodile to fly
a kite with his tail.
The Australasian conceived the brilliant idea of inter-
viewing Lonnen, a gentleman who, according to that
bulky organ, “discovered” the national larrikin to the
Australian stage. This statement need not wound the
feelings of Whitburn and other individuals who consider
themselves ignored in the matter. Lonnen did discover
his particular kind of larrikin—in London. He “uncon-
sciously cerebrated ” the young man from Chevalier’s
coster, and traded him off upon gallery-boys and weak-
minded journalists as the genuine Australian article.
Also, he has set a young Hebrew to reproduce the awful
hybrid thing in miniature, and the infant Hebrew seems
to have “ improved ” on Lonnen’s effort by instinct.
A certain much-travelled organist now travelling the
Australias has a habit of airing his grievances in public.
Recently he said to the “musical critic” of a paper up
South: “Look here, sir; you know nothing about music
whatever, so if you dare to criticise my performance
unfavourably, by Heaven I’ll denounce you from the
platform as a humbug.” Notices laudatory up to date.

N.S.W. State asylum.

public shows, as with Bernhardt, that the first-class thing
is wanted.
O’Grady is one version of Agrati’s name, in which he

Geo. H. Wood is on the point of return to America,
disgusted. We dare say he is the best negro comedian of
the U.S., but the troupe could not run on him alone.
Prof. Archibald, the phonograph showman, is now
managing a freezing work in India. His show froze so
badly for the last two months of its existence that the job
will just suit him.
At one Sydney “ panto.” house the ballet girls get
12s. 6d. per week.
The only uncommon feature in the artistically success-
ful Kellermann-Deslouis concerts in the Temperance Hall,
Hobart, was that baritone Deslouis got a bouquet with
streamers and all complete—an instance of what may
happen to a man in Australia. Patey’s husband is—thank
Heaven!—the only other singer whose feminine friends
have embarrassingly bouqueted him on a five-foot plat-
form !
Melbourne Standard wants to know why the promoters
of the latest baby-show do not offer a prize for the finest
Chinese baby.
A young woman went home to North Melbourne,
after visiting one of the pantomimes, and incon-
tinently cut her throat. A Melbourne evening paper
asserts “ that no reason can be assigned for the rash act.”
The poor girl had no alternative—she couldn’t kill a whole
pantomime cast. Manager Coppin’s theory is that having
seen the panto., she felt she had exhausted all the joys of
earth.
The Smythe now calls himself the Most Travelled. He
isn’t going to leave an opening for Carlyle S. to jump his
claim.

hunable to perform a duet by himself, but Miss Jones and

A certain well-known comedian has now visited Aus-
tralia three times, and brought a different wife with him
each trip.
In the recent racket between Captain Russell and Fillis’
big lion, Tub Bulletin’s sympathies are with the lion.
If this paper had been the lion, and the gallant Captain
had knocked it about in his usual somewhat-brutal
fashion, the possibilities are that he would have been
chewed a good deal worse than he was.
The next Savoy opera, says our correspondent, will he
by Ernest Ford, a very promising young musician
who lias, until recently, been conducting the orchestra at
the Trafalgar Square Theatre. The book is by an un-
named author, generally believed to be Justin Huntly
M’Carthy. And, speaking of the Savoy 7, reminds me that
it is said the proposed collaboration by Gilbert and
Sullivan is off. Old sores cannot be easily healed.
I often wonder, remarks The Bulletin’s Lond n corres
pondent, where dramatists expect to find customers for
their work. Their number is ever increasing. Christie
Murray has been busy collaborating with his brother
Henry—an exceedingly clever fellow, by the way—and
Johnny Shine, and the outcome of their labours is three
plays, one of which has been offered to Alexander, another
to Terry, whilst the third has been written specially
round Charles Warner, and it is thought will fit him like
a glove. I hope one or other of these works will succeed,
for Christie is fairly due for a favourable turn of
“ fortune’s faithless wheel.” Haddon Chambers, too, has
been away toiling (o complete his commissions. He has
three plays on the stocks, but he is bound hand and foot
by William Terriss to have a play for him ready before
March next, and has covenanted not to produce another
until this has been delivered. Terriss, in fact, has paid a
good bit down on account, and means to have his money’s
worth. Haddon’s three successive fiascos will, no doubt,
have taught him the lesson that if he hopes to compete
Successfully with more experienced writers he must not
do any slipshod work. Clever as he undoubtedly is, he
has not yet arrived at that pinnacle of fame which will
make a play go down by his name alone. The success of
“The idler, ’’ following so quickly upon that of “ Captain
Swift,”caused his head to swell a bit, but that is over now
The Wild-Cat Column.
The Federal Bank (Melb.) suspended payment
last Monday, and the usual liquidation is expected. The
Federal was originally one of Janies Munro’a institutions,
and the fatal influence of James possibly had something
to do vyith its downfall. At the same time, the collapse,
according to the directors’ own showing, is somewhat
inexplicable. The reserve fund, or a great part of it, has
gone in the shrinkage of securities, but the capital of
£400,000 is said to be intact. This will almost certainly
vanish in the process of liquidation—it always does —so
the directors appear to be throwing away £400,000
of their own and other people’s money without any
real effort to save it. The Federal had £400,000
unpaid capital, and although, apparently, it was suffering
from nothing save temporary pressure and withdrawal of
confidence, and only wanted time to make everything
right, yet no one seems to have dreamt of calling up this
amount to save a valuable institution from destruction.
Somehow the average Australian bank will rather go into
bankruptcy than make calls ; if anyone recollects any
Australian bank of recent years that has called up its
capital in preference to liquidating, The Bulletin, at all
events, does not. The Federal’s position, judged by its
published accounts, seems to be just this: it might have
saved the £400,000 already at stake by calling up
the other £400,000, and it wouldn’t do it—or else, for some
occult reason, it couldn’t. Further, apart from this pro-
blem, the directors’ explanation seems unaccountable.
The Federal, it is claimed, has sufficient assets, even
under the ruinous process of liquidation, to pay all its
creditors in full and leave something over, altogether
apart from the £400,000 uncalled. With such solid
security behind them the directors could have gone on
paying their depositors so long as a solitary coin remained
in the bank, without injury to anyone, and before they got
down to that last sovereign the tide of public con-
fidence might possibly have turned—at all events, on the
published statements, nothing could have been lost by
that process and everything might have been gained.
Further, taking all things into account, it seems abso-
lutely amazing that a syndicate of wealthy directors and
others did not proceed to buy up the 50s. shares when
they were abundantly available at 2s. 6d. or thereabouts,
aud then pay up the unpaid capital and keep the bank
afloat. There ought to have been £200,000 profit
in such a transaction if the Federal was anything near as
sound as it is made to look. The Bulletin suspects that
the public don’t yet quite know the worst about the
Federal Banking Co.
* * *
The new Queensland loan is being offered on
the London market at something like 85 net—a melan-
choly £B5 for every £lOO debenture. It would probably
be a lot cheaper to file the national schedule than borrow
at such a rate as this. And yet while the miserable,
stone-broke Kanaka Government is struggling to raise ij
millions on terms that a respectable three-ball Hebrew
would be ashamed of, £2,000,000 are lying unused in the
Queensland National Bank, and have been lying there for
months—or years. Several influential local politicians
are understood to he large shareholders in the Q.N. Bank.
Is there anything wrong with that institution so that they
are afraid to withdraw the money? Or does it simply
mean that is a good spec, for the Bank to get the public
funds at 4 per cent, interest and lend them out at 7 or 8,
while the Treasury goes broke? Or if this isn’t it, then
what does it mean ?
* * *
The present condition of the Balmain Steam
Perry Co. is an excellent illustration of how even the very
best of properties, dependent for its prosperity on public
support, can be smashed up by lordly pigheadedness and
offensive bluster. It is clearly to the interest of the
Balmain people that they should continue to support the
new company at whatever cost. The shareholders of the
old co. want their directors to resign. But the horse is
stolen.
* * *
The Perpetual Trustee Co. of N.S.W. forges
steadily ahead, is now more than out of debt, and has
now the management of cash and securities representing
two millions sterling. It should yet achieve the magni-
ficent success attained by its Southern prototypes. No one
should make his will without naming such a co. one of
his executors.
# * *
Among the N.S.W. Parliamentary papers
printed to the order of the Assembly is the “ corres-
pondence, &c received by the Government on the Broken
Hill strike.” Some portions are suggestive. On 4th
July ’92, labour-leader Richard Sleath (now a prisoner in
gaol) wired to Attorney-General Barton, and used this
expression : “ Thanks for services offered.” On the very
same date, William Knox, the mineowners’ secretary, in
Ms wire to “ Toby ” Barton, said : “ I am desired ” (by
the mining companies) “to thank you for your offer.”
Now, what some inquisitive N.S.W. member should do is
to move for a return of all the “correspondence, &c.,"
sent by the Government in connection with the Broken
Hill strike. What “services” did Barton “offer” to
Sleath? What*“ offer” did he make to Knox? What
was his relation to the hare ? And what his connection
with the hounds?
* * *
What has become of those tremendous steel and
iron works so boomed by a Sydney paper some months
back as if they were an accomplished fact ? True extracts
from a letter from one Albert Leahy, C.E., to one W. M.
Foote, M.E. (always), have been published, wherein we
are told how very averse the British capitalist is to any
“Colonial ” investment—all, of course, on account of the
Barrier strike. One might reasonably think the igno-
minious defeat of the miners, the gaoling of the leaders,
and glorious victory for Capitalism would have turned
things the other way about. But it is a good thing to
have an excuse for failure. It is somewhat indefinite to
hear that, bad as affairs are, some two or three firms are
ready to finance the iron-smelting, and there is a hope of
the works being established “on a small scale.” And yet
the public were led to believe that men and money were
ready.
• * *
Gann, the member for the Barrier, says that the
most fiendish thing he ever heard of is the mine-owners’
black-list of the men who took any prominent part in the
strike. “ Because you had the courage of your opinions
you shall not live if we can help it.” Such cold-blooded
cruelty is a challenge to civil war. The Bulletin re-
commends the mine-owners to bury the hatchet
like men.
# • •
The right of might. Thomas, one of the miners’
advocates in the Barrier strike, and a member of the
lead-poisoning commission, has been refused permission
to descend the mines in the performance of his duties on
the ground of the possible disaffection he might thereby
cause. Great Heavens ! has it come to this—the lessees
of the State’s property prohibit the entry of the State’s
commissioner! As the N.S.W. Government has not
insisted, we have another proof of Ministerial weakness
and of the brute force of Barrier capitalistic strength.
* # *
The banks are as largely responsible for the
collapse in Broken Hill shares as is the fall of silver. By
their freedom with money they first fanned the boom
in them to such a degree that they reached unreasonable
prices. Now, in the midst of a train of adverse circum-
stances they are practically shovelling them into the
market to protect overdrafts. There is just as much mad-
ness in one proceeding as the other, and in each case it is
the invincible big holders, the men who originally got
their shares for next to nothing, who alone continue to
accumulate money.

N.S.W. 1892 gold-yield, 144,2590z5.—an increase
on the previous ydar of about 26,0000z5. As elsewhere
the interest in the industry goes down as the yields go
up. The yield is made up by a great number of districts,
Armidale being top with 33,0000z5. The total is miser-
ably small considering the number of “ gold-fields.”

Recent rains have put six months’ supply of
water in the Spa-Jessop, down at Shoalhaven, N.S.W.,
and hydraulic sluicing operations commence immediately.
The Shoalhaven river drift will be proved this year.
* * #
Too much has been made of a silver and gold
discovery, recently made at Peak Hill, N.S.W. Assays
showing considerably over lOOoz. gold and over 10000/.
silver were published far arid wide. A ton of the ore was
forwarded to Sydney for immediate treatment, and it was
estimated to lie worth £5OO in gold and silver. It has
been crushed —and so have the enthusiasts. £5OO ex-
pected ; about £lOO received. The return was composed
of 20oz. gold and 139 of silver—truly, a most sensational
return, if the optimistic estimates had not been so widely
published. The result of the Peak Hill Proprietary crush-
ing was much less than one-fifth of that predicted by the
local newspaper correspondents.
* # *
George Lansell, having in his 180 mine reached a
depth of 2860 ft.—we believe it is now the world’s deepest
gold mine— has determined to go down another 300 ft.
Lansell led the way to deep-sinking on Bendigo, and, as
he often predicted, he has lived to see the day when the
bulk of the field’s gold would come from greater depths
than alleged geologists predicted it could exist at.
The Bulletin hopes he will live to see the 180 down to
3500 ft.
• # #
Nambucca Heads Gold and Silver Mining Go.
has held its half-yearly meeting. The shareholders
listened to a tale of woe. The chairman admitted that
prospects of the possession of large and rich ore bodies
had been dissipated, and indicated much managerial dis-
satisfaction. Hope is now centred in the undeveloped
parts of the property. Remote hope ! Another instance
of mad mining. The sensible plan would have been to
prove the ore-bodies before spending thousands in chlori-
nation and other machinery. This was a pyritous forma-
tion, out of which a few rich tons for Sydney, Ballarat, or
any other place could always be got. In mining one diffi-
culty is to find the lode ; but after that a much greater
difficulty is to find the man. Somebody was at last found
for Nambucca, and whether one or many they are deserv-
ing of sympathy. A few 7 days ago the writer deeply sym-
pathised with a man w 7 ho, three or four months ago,
strongly advised his friends to purchase these shares at
£l, on being sadly “left” himself. The latter coolly
answered; “ Why, I sold every share !”
* # #
Mandurama, N.S.W., formerly known as the
Junction reefs, is, it is reported, being worked by Vic-
torians. The results have not been up to expectations,
but it is intended to float the property into a large co., in
England. It narrowly escaped flotation in England, five
years ago, for which the English people have, w 7 e take it,
never expressed their sorrow 7 . It has a nominal capital of
£BOO,OOO, if you please. The mine cons'sts of a series of
flat decomposed auriferous formations, plenty of ore, but
very poor in gold, the average being, by the ordinary
methods of treatment (and it is not rich enough to stand
any other), from three to four dwts. to the ton. The gold
is fine but free. The mine has abundance of water-power,
and a plant consisting of 30 head, and a Huntingdon Mill,
all driven by a turbine wheel. The owners, Sydney men
at one time, expected to make a pot of money out of the
concern, but it boomed not, and if it floats in England
they should consider themselves men of luck.
# # *
During a brief stoppage at a Western N.S.W.
railway station the writer heard something re the late
auriferous discovery in the Gordon Emery mine. An
excitable individual, conversing through the carriage-
window in tones so confidential that most of the pas-
sengers in the train could hear, declared that it was
amazingly rich, and being asked if it were intended to
float it in Melbourne or Sydney, said, “There isn’t
enough money in either place to buy it.” For further in-
formation reference was made to some men in the train
who had examined the discovery. They were consulted.
They were of cooler heads and judgment, and said
the find was rich in places, poor in others, and generally
patchy. Thus we have a glimmering of the authorship of
the reports of this pigment-mine which has afforded the
public so much amusement. By the way, what has
become of the many tons of “ brown,” “ red,” and other
“oxides” sent away from this property? Of course it
went to London and was sold. What did it realise, and
were the expectations of the shareholders fulfilled?
# * *
The writer has been shown some wonderfully
rich specimens of reef-gold from W.A. The metal is
embodied in solid quartz, and in several pieces composes
50 per cent, to the matrix. It is represented to have
come from inland Pilbarra, and the finder had to leave
the place for want of water.
# * *
Some of the stories told of W. A. goldfields
to-day eclipse those of the old digging times of the other
provinces. Here is one: A young man—call him Smith-
cam e across, away beyond the Murchison district, a rich
vein, from which, in a few days, he dollied out £BOO. He
then sold his claim for £2O, and made for the seaboard
with his money to await a steamer for Adelaide. Ere he
left, however, the purchaser turned up with the same
intention and £lOOO worth of gold. He, too, had sold out
for a small sum, and those who are left are doing equally
well, and, what seems more sensible, are sticking to it.
The fact that many W.A. quartz specimens contain nearly
one-half gold will not he regarded by many miners as
a sign of the permanent or uniform richness of the reefs
from which they were taken.

A correspondent wants to know what lias become
of the Argentine silver mine (N.Q.); why is it that no
meeting of shareholders has been held for 15 months, and
what has become of the capital subscribed ? Thus we are
pleasantly reminded of the floating of this concern, and
the reports in which the “ Jerky Jim " lode played a pro-
minent part. When the prospectus of this mine was
issued in Melbourne the public were invited to come
early so as to avoid being crushed to death in the rush for
scrip.

• « *
The secretary of a mine at Swamp Oak, N.S.W.,
is delegated by the shareholders to float a co., and he
applies to the warden for a month’s suspension of labour.
Request refused. A manager is then appointed, and he
works the concern with three shareholders, and puts
through the stone at grass, some 30 tons, for (i ozs. The
shareholders sue the manager for wages and cost of
carting and crushing. They obtain judgment; and the
manager has to pay. He fails to get his own wages
because the company is not registered.
* # #
“ Quid Nunc,” of the Mining Standard
(Sydney), is doubtful whether the reef recently struck in
the Baker’s Creek North is Smith’s reef. If he had ever
been in the mine, he would know that the reef recently
struck has been worked in the upper levels to the Baker’s
Creek boundary, and that no sensible man has any possible
shadow of doubt about it.

Personal Items.
The spicy “ Mount Macedon ” libel action,
instituted by J. S. Butters, comes on in April.
Premier Patterson, a cross-orbed man,
promises to “ look to Federation with a practical
eye.”
Mr. W. A. M‘Arthur is the only native-
born Australian who has ever been in a British
Ministry.
Ex-Premier Shiels wears his fast-greying
hair—what there is of it—shorter than any other
man in Melbourne.
Lord Elgin, who, it is said, will succeed
Gov. Jersey at Sydney, is a Canadian by birth,
and 44 years of age.
Gov. Hopetoun’s three months’ holiday
also preludes an early departure. He will return
to England a year before his full time is up.
Mr. Henry Parkes really did all he
could to make his book saleable. He did not
publish in it a single line of his own poetry.
Chief Justice Way (S.A.) says that the
man who does not change his opinions in the
space of two years is either a fool or a fossil.
The recent attitude of Mr. Dibbs to Gov.
Jersey was that of a Republican who had helped
himself to a cigar from the Heir-apparent’s case.
Rudyard Kipling writes of “ The Broken
Hill of 20 years ago.” This is the cocksure cus-
tomer who settled the Australian destiny in two
s econds.
A son of G. R. Dibbs, after six years in
the N.S.W. Civil Service, has £2O per annum less
salary than when he started, and not through lack
of merit.
Willis, M.P. (N.S.W.), has given Premier
Dibbs’ nephew, according to his own statement,
“a billet droving out west, at £1 a week and his
tobacco.”
Fergus Hume says the world will persist
in judging him by “The Mystery of a Hansom
Cab.” It will not admit he has done better work.
Why should it ?
William Myles, aged 96, died last week at
Yass, N.S.W., where he had resided for 64 years.
He was in his youth an intimate of Hamilton
Hume, the explorer.
Victoria’s leading Tory organ piped very
small concerning the alleged embezzlements of
Humphries. Peculations by the humble secretary
of a friendly society are usually announced in
large type, but an “ unfortunate scandal ” at the
office of the Employers’ Union is an event to be
deplored in an unobtrusive paragraph.

Glebe (Sydney), was 86 when she died, and was
married in old St. Phillip’s Church as far back as
1823. She had lived at Toxteth for 62 years.

of several hundred per cent, more brainy men

Queen Victoria has refused to join an
Anti-Crinoline League. The fact is she can-
not forget that her late lamented husband
professed to admire her in crinolines, and she has
secretly resented every change of fashion since
Albert died.

Justice Bundey (S. A.) never forgets that
Colton (then Premier) promised, if he accepted
the judgeship, to raise the salary from ,£l7OO to
£2OOO. The extra money didn’t come, however.
Bundey, it is said, was previously making £5OOO
a year, and kept a yacht, which he has had to give
up.
Burke, the explorer, buried at Innamincka
(S.A.), has a very humble grave. The only indi-
cation is an old tree, with a piece cut out, and
inscribed—
R. O. H. B.
21/9/61.
The body is buried opposite in the sandy soil.
Edmund Mitchell’s (ex-secretary Victorian
Pastoralists’ Union) syndicated novel opens well.
Mitchell can write much better than he can
speak, like most born journalists. Like Whiteley
King, Epps, Cockburn, and others of the men
who have swapped journalism for Capitalistic
Association secretaryships, he is a democrat at
heart.
George Milner Stephen has been once
more perambulating Adelaide, S.A. as a great
healer. He simply “commands” the pains and
ailments to go, and a hiatus is here left for
prayer. In ’3B, this Stephen (who is a brother of
ex-Judge Alfred Stephen, of N.S. W.) was Acting-
Governor of South Australia —warming-pan for
Gov. Gawler.
A letter has been received by a friend of
Charles Lilley, the evicted Chief Justice of Queens-
land, stating that it is within the bounds of possi-
bility that he may be asked to resume his former
position. It is generally believed, however, that,
should Griffith by any chance not hold on to the
“good thing” he has made for himself, Pat. Real
will be the new Chief Justice, and that Bruin
will retire on his pension.
Bruin, a Queensland Supreme Court
justice, sworn to uphold the law, is a deadly
opponent of the law-cheapening Legal Prac-
titioners’ Act. A respectable but poverty-stricken
solicitor apologised for appearing in court, on the
ground that his client was too poor to employ
counsel, whereupon Bruin insolently and sarcasti-
cally referred to him as “a gentleman of poor
dress and worse address.”
. Phillip Menncll (writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent by last mail) has now the
chief interest in the British Australasian,
which was originally founded by him. He has
bought out Nash, who goes to Sydney next month
as financial editor of the Daily Telegraph. He
is well up in figures. I hear that he expects to be
extensively consulted by the Treasurer, and to be
a sort of financial adviser to the Government.
D. B. VV. Sladen has returned to London
from a two years’ trip through the United States
and Japan, and is now located (says our corres-
pondent) in a charming flat at Kensington, where
every Tuesday Mrs. Sladen gives smart “at
homes ” to people of note in the artistic and
literary world. D.B.W. is hon. secretary of the
Authors’ Club, which is on the high road to being
the most distinguished literary centre in the
metropolis.
G. W. Rusden has turned up again in
Melbourne. Daddy Longlegs is a nickname he
chuckles over, for he is a reminder of Cruikshank’s
drawing of Nobody. He has a nut-cracker face,
or call it a crab-apple, wrinkly, crinkly. His
shrill voice, common to people of his construction,
pipes piquantly. The current praise of Higin-
botham induces theßusdenites to rake up G. W.’s
allusions to him, in that beautifully lopsided
work “ The History of Australia,” even more
bitter than the M.L. history, for which he had to
back down to Bryce.
Ex-Premier Munro : “I came to Mel-
bourne over 30 years ago with hardly a penny in
my pocket. I worked hard as a working man,
and received every honour which a free State
could confer on any of its citizens, until at last I
held the highest position in the land; Now I
“THE DREAD OF SOMETHING AFTER DEATH."
WOULD-BE SUICIDE : “It is not the awful, headlong plunge into
eternity makes me hesitate. It’s the dread of being
referred to in the daily papers as a ‘Determined
Suicide.’” (So he takes up the burden of life again.)
stand before yon, 61 years of age, without a shilling
in my pocket. For the first time in my life,
through liabilities which I undertook, never ex-
pecting to be called upon to meet them, I am
unable to pay 20s. in the pound. I have now
returned to Melbourne, and intend, if spared, to
work hard to retrieve my position ; but I have
made up my mind never again to incur any debt
even if 1 have to starve.”

Austin Brereton, late of Sydney (wrijbes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent), is now
doing Clement Scott’s work on London Daily
Telegraph, and is in great feather. He occupies
Scott’s chambers whilst the latter is away. There
is a rumour that Clement took unto himself a wife
before he left, but this is denied by Brereton. It
is certain that for a long time past Clement has
paid marked attention to a certain lady well-
known in London. But there is also another who
claims the gay critic as her fianci, and it would be
a dreadful blow to her if it should turn out that
he is married.

short-lived, and the islands will soon be depopu-
lated.

The first petition for the Australian vote
by ballot came from 800 Forest Creek diggers.
The Act was passed on Wednesday, 19th Dec.,
1855.

into the Queensland Labour Party, because the
latter supports free, secular and compulsory
education.

had declined, both the Chief Justiceship and the
Agency-General.

The Medical Congress, lately held in
Sydney, is having its reports and papers printed
free at the N.S.W- Government Printing Office.
Iniquitous! Five hundred pounds saved a wealthy
trades-union at the expense of the People.
A great many members of N.S.W. Upper
House, including Hoskins, are in favour of the
Elections Bill because of its provision for single
electorates. They think it will cripple the Demo-
cratic vote and make elections more expensive.

with government advts. : but directly he hinted

neutral pages of the Government Gazette they
clamored for his dismissal from the Premiership.

In Adelaide, last week, a man charged
with having no visible means of support said it
wasn’t true and produced 6Jd., and the court held
it wasn’t enough and sent him up for two months.
Being content with a little doesn’t always answer.
The Melbourne (Vic.) syndicate which
owns Tindarey station, Bourke, N.S.W., the other
day gave the hands there notice of reduction of
wages to 15s. per week. A general exodus re-
sulted, and the Sydney Government Labour-bureau
furnished substitutes.
The vice-regal persons of Australia are
undoubtedly useful as instruments of jobbery,
anyhow. Lord Hopetoun delayed his jaunt to
Maoriland, in order that he might be at hand to
sign anything he was told to sign, and when the
political corpse of Shiels put an order for 98 new
J.P.’s under his noble nose, he just wrote his
name as usual.

He continually argues that the Upper House has
no right to reject or nullify measures passed by

a large majority of the people,
Patterson and O’Loghlen in the same

It is estimated that the big Robb Arbitra*
tion case, now dragging through in Brisbane (Q.)>
has already totted up £25,000 worth of expenses.
Does any sane man believe that any sane con-
tractor would run all this rig it the result were
not almost a foregone conclusion?
The opening mass meeting in connection
with the Labour election campaign at the Albion,
near Brisbane (Q.), was remarkable for being
numerously attended by female workers, married
and single. The Bananalandress intends to be an
active element in future local politics.
A telegram in a London paper stated that
the Premier of Queensland, having gone on a pro-
longed tour North, had left the Ministry in charge
of the leader of the Opposition. Which is
not so unusual an in coalition
government as it appears on the surface.
The new Victorian Premier, Patterson, is
reputedly “a strong man ’’and plays the game for
all it is worth. Beetling brows, a rakish jaw, and
a habit of looking ugly when he talks, have won
him the reputation of being a devil of a fellow
amongst those who are awed by bulk and splutter.
From the form of ex-Premier Shiels’
declaration that 2| years of office have hardened
his opinion that the present system of Party
Government—Government by Contradiction—is a
failure, it is plain that Shiels is a Bulletin con-
vert, without the heart to crystallise his political
beliefs into action.
The “Year-book of Australia,” special
edition of 5000 copies—issued under the authority
of the N.S.W. Government at a cost of £250 —is
distributed, with a special cover, by the Depart-
ments. “ Mr. So-and-so, with compliments and
greeting for the New Year, 1893.” Lovely
method of retrenchment!
The N.S.W. legislator evinces an over-
powering lust for the bawbees in his country’s
extremity. Contrast his voluntary abandonment
of 5 per cent, on £3OO a year, with an expected
exemption of £2OO, with the statutory shearing of
the Tasmanian politicians’ screw by 25 per cent.,
with no exemption at all.
Premier Dibbs (N.S.W.) says that his
Government is still Protectionist, but it considers
the time is “inopportune” for dealing with
the fiscal question. As Dibbs was made Premier
for the express purpose of dealing with that
question the time must also be “inopportune”
for him to remain in office.-

fr®m paying income-tax. The rumour is in tem-

Let the public keep in view the fact that
all this eable-talk about Australian public insol-
vency is the outcome of a gigantic conspiracy, in
which many local public men take part, to grab
the existing State railways. Let the electors never
forget that the control of the railways means even
more than the control of the land.
Softgoods Reid is the new Minister of
Defence for Victoria. Robert Reid, only three
months a member of the Leg. Council, is coming
to the fore as violently as did Matthew Davies.
The sudden importance of this person from “the
Lane ” passeth all understanding. The Bulletin
will watch his career with much interest.

wraith proposals, the land-grab lines passed by the

One thing must be said for Deakin. He
was pressed hard to accept a knighthood, and un-
like G. Republican Dibbs, he had the courage
(and the sense) to refuse. Until Australians look
for honours only to their own land their average
Parliamentarian will ever stand expectant, with
painted face, on the sidewalks of political prosti-
tution.
A certain Australian Postmaster-General
handed in a bill for cab-hire for one year : ,£2oo—
quite 13s. per day. It was paid without a
murmur by his bleeding country. But the wife of
a ganger who died on the 27th of the month
wasn’t allowed to draw her husband’s pay for the
remaining three days. Both events happened' in
the same province.

“Defence League The League is distinctly

the day when the One-man-one-vote clause was

pending in the other House.

The Argus says that a pleasing feature of
the Patterson Ministry is that only two of its
members—O’Loghlen and Richardson—support
One-man-one-vote.
Salomons and Dibbs have parted on bad
terms politically; but a public washing of minis-
terial dirty linen is postponed for the present.
This exhibition was quite expected by Dibbs, last
week, for he went to the trouble of sending a
shorthand writer into the Council gallery to take
a full note of Salomons’ statement. All this
trouble was superfluous as Salomons kept the cat
in the bag.
The Brisbane Courier’s biting article on
the disgraceful position now occupied by Griffith
as a self-appointed pluralist was evidently inspired
by The Bulletin’s paragraph in a recent issue,
having reference to Parkes’ dictum that the in-
stant a Premier decided to appoint himself to any
stipendiary Civil Service position, at that instant
his office as Premier became vacant —and all future
acts of his Ministry illegal.
N.S.W. Upper House unsuccessfully tried
to reduce the Assembly to 100 members under
the new Elections Act. Only roads and bridges
members would have objected. Nearly every
member of the Lower House is privately of opinion
that 125, like 141. is too great a membership for
a population of 1,140,000 or so. Great Britain’s
37| millions have 670 members ; France’s 38 mil-
lions, 584 deputies; Switzerland’s 3 millions, 145
members.
Let the test-vote for Australian Republi-
cans be on the question of passing a law, pro-
hibiting any Australian citizen from being recog-
nised legally under any titular distinction other
than one defining his occupation. Let anyone not
content with plain “Mister” cease to have the
privileges of Australian citizenship. If this were
so,-how many,_ if any, Australian “ my-ladys ”
would save their lords’*citizenship by graciously
acknowledging the humble “Madam ?”
W. G. Wilson, of Port Pirie, S.A., wrote
the following protest on the back of the Income-
tax return which he made under legal compulsion:
“Furnished under protest, the requirement to
furnish being an immoral one, demanded by a
Government representing an ignorant, enslaved
people, who permit the great fund of land-values,
created by themselves, to go into private pockets,
instead of into the State Treasury, thus requiring
immoral means of raising revenue to be resorted
to.”
It is alleged that there is not one Jew on
the share-list of the defunct Panama Canal Com-
pany. The Hebrew names are conspicuous among
the contractors, the financiers, the people who
supplied stores, who indented labourers, who sold
their votes and interest to the company, who took
commission in a thousand different ways from the
battered enterprise. But not one Israelite of
them all put his good red gold into the great ditch
which swallowed so many Gentile fortunes. When
the crash comes Abraham is never there.
With a look of deep displeasure on it*
intellectual face, Melbourne Age: “There is no
doubt that France has behaved in a very shabby
way to these colonies, by taking advantage of her
possession of an island in our neighbourhood to
turn it into a convict settlement.” Dear old
Hingland couldn't be guilty of such shabby
conduct. The idea of depositing her convicts in
the vicinity of free-born Australians until they,
the f.b. A.’s, began to grow restless, would never
have found standing-room in the Great British
brain.
The Queensland (1892) sugar season has
closed with better returns than in any previous
year. Who has benefited ? In the principal
centre, many men have not been able to get any
work at all, and among those who have been
employed on the plantations, the average wage
has not exceeded £1 a week, without rations.
The Times says that the Kanaka in Queensland is
better off than the English agricultural labourer.
It might have truthfully added that the Kanaka
is better off than the average white labourer in
Queensland.
W hen an article is brought forward and
all its patrons praise it, and allow that it possesses
the properties that the manufacturers claim for it,
and which possesses virtues and merits which are
universally acknowledged both by press and pub-
lic, such endorsement must really prove beyond
the slightest shadow of a doubt the intrinsic merit
of that article. Ihe unparalleled success which
has attended the introduction of Clements Tonic
to suffering mankind, and the universal praise
bestowed upon it by influential and well-known
persons in the community, conclusively prove
that this article is undoubtedly genuine. Clem-
ents Tonic has done more good in the last two
years in Australia than all the other medicines
put together can ever claim to have done, and
there is not a city, town, village, or street but
what there are people resident in who are willing
to testify to the wonderful efficacy of this re-
nowned remedy; for evidence comes to hand
proving this by every mail in letters similar to
that of Miss Trances INI. Cairns, Clengarry, Vic.,
who writes I have much pleasure in testifying
to the curative powers of Clements Tonic. I had
suffered from liver complaint and indigestion for
some years ; but the latter became so bad early in
1891 that I was obliged to get leave of absence
from my school duties for a month. I returned to
work, but was very little better. The most pro-
nounced symptoms were sleeplessness and severe
pain in the chest after eating the least morsel of
food. This pain commenced in the chest, passed
round the heart, and thence into my shoulder I
had read several of the testimonials re Clements
Tonic, and as yet I had not received any lasting
benefit from doctors, I decided to try it. By the
time 1 had taken one small bottle I was very
much better. The pain was less, I could sleep at
least part of the night, and I was not troubled
with the horrible dreams that had haunted me
when asleep. I continued taking it, and by the
tune I had taken three small bottles the pain was
gone, and the other symptoms with it. Since
then I have had one bad attack, during last
Christmas vacation. I again took Clements
lonic, and soon became quite well. Since then I
have not had any return of the indigestion, and at
this present time I feel much better than I have
done for years. I have no hesitation in saying
that Clements Tonic cured me. I was very ill so
ill that I could not have lived long in the state T
was in, and here I am to-day, well and strong
with a good appetite, and able to enjoy a good
night s rest, all of which I owe to Clements
tonic. I first took it about this time last year.
I might say, in conclusion, that I was very pre-
judiced against what some folks call “quack
medicines.” I had always called in medical aid.
—I remain, yours most thankfully, Frances M.
Cairns, Glengarry, Vic.

Society
Lord Jersey says he did not apply for
leave of absence, because “he believed it would
not be. right to leave so large and important a
colony as N.S.W. without a Governor for any
length of time.” That’s the way with these vice-
regal gentlemen : they believe they are the gover-
nors on the national steam-engine, whilst they are
really only the brass filagree on the fire-box doors.
It was as The Bulletin conjectured. The
Governoresa sent in her resignation and the
Governor resigned himself to it.

him to ‘ kiss the dar-

concerning A list alian

a fact that few men
can make a rapid

means without find-

a wife to help spend
it.

The residents of a Sydney suburb are now
getting used to seeing a “ through ” train, time-
tabled not to stop until it should arrive at a
distant station, slow down at the local platform
for the sole purpose of depositing thereon one
middle-aged matron, wife of a Cabinet Minister.
And yet we .are told that N.S.W. railway-
administration is quite beyond the reach of
political influence.
Within a couple of miles of Hall’s Gap in
the Grampian Ranges (Vic.) —the scene of the
boom-broke province’s pray-and-cuddle picnic—
live, in the heart of the hills, two maiden ladies
of curious pedigree and eccentric habits. They
are among the finest wild-flower painters in
Australia, and make a good income by painting
and illuminating presentation addresses for a
Melbourne stationery firm.
* * *
Mr. Donald Wallace —and he ought to
know —writes to say there isn’t a particle of truth
in the rumour that he is about to marry again.
# * #
An interesting social item from the West.
A limb of the law was engaged to the daughter of
a wealthy land-owner and Legislative Councillor,
without the knowledge of the latter, who would
not on any consideration have given his assent.
The couple agreed to elope, an appointment being
made for the local railway-station to catch the
Sydney mail. The “ limb’s ” courage having, how-
ever, oozed out, he had word sent of the intended
elopement to the young lady’s father, who met
her at the railway-station and took her home to
ruminate on the fickleness of man.
• • •
The Judge at Glen Lines (N.S.W.)
Quarter Sessions, recently sentenced two men,
first offenders, who had pleaded guilty to robbery,
to three years penal servitude each, remarking
that if they conducted themselves well for ten or
eleven months they would be enabled to earn 12s.
per week for the remainder of the term. Twelve
shillings a week with food, clothing and lodging is,
in these days, surely offering a premium to theft.
# * #
Just listen. Mr. Aitken, the Sydney
Exchange Hotel Co.’s manager, the other day
advertised for a man at £1 a week and his keep to
look after the hotel donkey-engine. He had over
200 applicants, most of whom said that they were
competent, and many of whom produced evidence
of the fact. Some of them absolutely cried to be
allowed to stay and do the work in exchange for
their food and lodging only. Every day, the
manager says, he has a dozen or more men who
want to work for their food alone.
* * *
A correspondent: “As to ‘short and
natural Australian love-stories,’ perhaps the finest
is ‘ An Unseen Foe,’ contributed by ‘ Ada Cam-
bridge ’ (Airs. Cross) to the defunct Centenn'al
Magazine. It was short —it was true—and
Heaven knows, it was natural, inasmuch as the
woman was drowned a few days before marriage.
That’s the love-story that’s ‘natural.’ The fact
is, people don’tiwtn/ love-stories that end naturally.
They want’em to continue the hot, perspiring, love
business after marriage. That’s where they’re un-
natural.”

Yet another love story. A year and a
half ago the drunken captain of an island labour-
trading vessel, leaning over the bar of the Cafe
, in Noumea, was overheard by a Sydney
barrister hiccnping thus to the rowdy-looking
English barmaid : “ Look here, Nell, I'm a beast
and yon— -(Am?)— ain’t no better. I’ve lost my
berth, and if you’re agreeable I’ll kick my nigger-
girl out and make a decent woman of you, and
we’ll go and live where no one knows what you
are.”
* * *
The conversion of the borough of Port
Melbourne into a “ town ” (or city—the import of
either change is equally mighty) gave the local
Mayor a chance to beat the record in municipal
grovel. This happy man “alluded with .pride to
the fact that he had been his Excellency’s ship-
mate in coming to the colony, and had that day
had the honour of riding in the same carriage with
him.” The fervour of the little potentate’s utter-
ance suggested that he would willingly lay down
his life for Hopetoun if his bones might ultimately
be stowed away in his lordship’s own private box.

There is a mysterious something in the
Australian climate which causes every Governor’s
wife to suffer from “indifferent health.”
Editor Bulletin,—
In re The Bulletin’s
remarks as to the con-
fused decisions in Vic-
toria about . a wife’s
right to sue for divorce
when her husband is
not locally domiciled,
the Vic. case of Ho-a-
mie versus Ho-a-mie,
tried some years ago,
and reported in 6 Vic.
Reports, p. 113, is in-
teresting. A China-
man married an Aus-
tralian girl in Vic-
toria ; afterwards de-
serted her, went to
China, married a
Chinese woman, and
established a Chinese
domicile. The Victorian
wife followed him there,
but got no satisfaction
and returned to Mel-
bourne, where she insti-
tuted divorce proceed-
ings. The court granted
the divorce, and ruled
that although the domi-
cile of a husband is
the domicile of bis
wife, yet if the hus-
band purposely changes
his domicile to injure
the wife, the wife may
sue for a divorce in the
courts of the matri-
monial domicile. —G. A.

Lord Carington, says the cable, has opened
an exhibition of pictures of Australian life,
“designed to encourage the spirit of emigration
amongst the London poor.” Query !
Most Australian city churches now issue
a “monthly paper ’’—usually a puny whine for
the grace of God and more funds. It is in accord-
ance with the nature of things that the majority
of these sheets issued in Sydney are printed at
“ rat ” offices.
# # #
One of the wealthiest (as regards its con-
gregation) of Sydney Anglican churches accepts
yearly a donation from the English Church Society
funds, the original design of which was the pro-
vision of religious teaching in sparsely-settled
districts.
* * #
An assault case in Adelaide police-court
last week disclosed these facts. A Sunday-school
teacher and a choir-singer “ got mashed on ” the
same choir songstress. Result: On Christmas
morn the chorister invited the teacher out from
church, service to fight. Offer declined, time
being considered inconvenient. But the teacher
waited iu a quiet street a fortnight afterwards
and caught and “ bashed ” the musician. All
the witnesses were church-officials. Fine, ss. and
£3 16s. costs.

action : “You know what I want—the arm-chaii

arms round my neck asking for another kiss.
You are fond of them ; so am I since you taught)

girl. Verdict for <£loo.

# # *
Unsavoury, but true. A N.S.W. Legisla-
tive Councillor had a hot-bath the other day. He
was ill and his doctor made it a condition of his
continuing to attend the case. The statesman
survived it.
* * *
Queen Victoria may shortly be repre-
sented in N.S.W. by (apt name !) John Lackey.
So far as The Bulletin remembers, no successor
has yet been named to Alfred Stephen as Lieut.-
Governqr ; and, in default of such appointment,
John Lackey will assume the vacant Governorship
under the open commission which was issued in
1879 to the President of the Legislative Council as
a sequel to the Robinson-Martin embroilment.
Lackey cannot well hold on to the President’s
chair while he is administering the Government.
How can he fill both billets without bringing one
office or the other, or both, into contempt ? How
can he, for instance, when the day comes for pro-
roguing or dissolving Parliament “in person,”
walk solemnly to the steps outside the Council
chamber and, as President of the Council, receive
himself, as Victoria’s representative, and then,
being seated in the Governor’s chair, or the Presi-
dent’s chair, or both, how can he, as Administra-
tor, order himself, as President, to send Black Rod
along for the Legislative Assembly? And,
finally, how can he, as Administrator, after read-
ing the Speech from an imaginary Throne, bow
himself off the premises, and in the course of two
seconds come back as President and receive the
Speaker’s obeisance as he leads out the Commons,
and then report to the Council that he, as Admin-
istrator, had been pleased to deliver a speech, &c.?

Humphries, the defaulting secretary of
the Vic. Employers’ Union, cut his connection
with Capital in the most suitable manner possible.
He shaved off his board and dressed himself as a
working-man—
Over two months ago, a youngster escaped
from Nudgee (Bris.) Orphanage, and stole a horse,
saddle and bridle. Hue and cry was raised, but
he safely remained in the thickly-populated suburb
of Fortitude Valley, brazenly riding the stolen
horse daily in the main streets of the city itself.
And the alert detectives would never have found
out even that much, had the boy not left his
boarding-house without paying up. Gaboriau and
other detect! ve-idolators are phenomenal roman-
cists.
LINES TO A NOBLE FRIEND.
Well, Jersey, if depart you must,
Financial matters to “adjust,”
We’ll grieve to say “ Good morning.”
Despite your whiskers and your hair
We learned to love you pretty fair,
And never caught you yawning.
The earl who sticks to such a hat
Doth not incite the democrat
To curse creation madly ;
And, if the tailor makes the man,
Your’s made you on a simple plan
(And cut the trousers badly).
No jingo altitudes you’ve struck,
Nor made believe to push a truck,
And seem unduly chummy ;
You seldom talked Britannia “ rot,”
You didn’t tell the people what
A friend you had in Tummy.
No old official Loftus style
Have you inflicted on us, while
In tanks you masqueraded ;
Nor with a ghastly gallows crime
(The fruit of early convict time)
Shall Jersey be upbraided.
In short, although ’tis very true,
There’s little for a Gov. to do,
And what he does is nonsense.
At seven thousand pounds a year
We (this is funny) held you dear,
Yet rather cheap, in one sense.
Lord Jersey, tell yer what it is
Our gaudy snobs do shady biz—
The fellows can’t be trusted ;
Now you deserve your noble rank
F’or being partner in a bank
Which hasn’t gone and busted.
We would we were a harmless lord
With stocks and shares and acres broad,
And agents rent-collecting.
A coronet upon our brow !
And oh !—excuse us stopping now,
The subject’s too affecting.
Have they shown great circumspection in arranging that
collection,
Or gone straight for facts, and fixed them with a legend
underneath,
To prove “ law’n order ” stifles labour troubles here with
rifles ?
Do they give a speaking likeness of the cropped and
handcuffed Sleath ?
Do they picture the Kanaka in his paradise of “ bacca ”
And rum, amongst the planters where the sugar-cane is
grown,
And the whites who’re “broke” and stranded, in the
cities grimly banded,
Or all sleeping in the moonlight, softly pillowed on a
stone?
Do they add a cartoon truthful wherein thousands old
and youthful
Who’ve been killed or badly crippled in the fall of banks
are shown ?
Have they views of arid acres that at present have no
takers,
And of green and grassy valleys that the wealthy
grabbers own ?
Not a bit! The views that suited without shades were
executed,
And show landscapes ever smiling and a prospect all
serene,
Or fat people, clean and sunny, in a land of oil and honey,
Where the skies are always cloudless and the fields are
very green.
But the British workman, seeing he is pictured as a
being
Of beauty blessed with peace himself, will, if he’s any
brains,
Treat these pictures as deceiving, and be wary of
believing
Any views of Australasia that the Baron entertains.
Whilst Humphries represented Cash
And had the Argus for a pal,
He wore the hat and garments flash
Of one whose cry is “Capital !’’
For which his ready tongue and pen
Were fighting bravely night and day ;
He scorned those “dogs” of working-men
And laughed to see them “ lose the day.”
Corrupted by the smell of wealth
(Let circumstances take the blame),
Poor Humphries slunk away by stealth,
Intending to disguise his shame.
Thought he, “ I'm going to the dogs,
I’ve done with the Employers’ elan.”
He clothed himself in common togs,
And called himself—a working-man.

At Auckland (M L.) recently a little girl
fell into the fire, and was removed to the hospital
in an almost hopeless condition. She ultimately
recovered, but it was necessary to graft new skin
on the surfaces where the cuticle had been
destroyed, and for this purpose 150 bits of skin
with occasional scraps of flesh adhering were
snipped off one of the female nurses. A local
paper started to enthuse over this noble sacrifice
on the lady’s part, and then learned, to its horror,
that it was simply part of her regular work—when
any such case occurs the doctor selects some
member of the staff and takes from him or her
what skin he wants as a matter of course. There-
fore, the lady has practically no say in the matter,
and apparently it isn’t even considered in her
wages. Presumably the staff takes turns at the
work, though if the doctor had a disagreement,
say, with one of the nurses, he could always get
level by taking more skin off her than off the
others.

The supreme audacity of Australian police
officialism was well illustrated in the action of the
Mittagong (N.S.W.) officer who arrested Frederick
Humphries (as he, luckily for the officer, turned
out to be) on a charge of having stolen his own
watch and chain.
* # •
When certain persons advertise for
“clean ‘ O.S.’ stamps ” that is, stamps
issued to Govt, officers for “On Service’ pur-
poses, they just raise the question whether
they are not parties to felony. Nobody can sell a
clean “0.5.” stamp unless he has first stolen it,
or bought it from a thief.
* * *
Under the South Australian Administra-
tion and Probate Act of 1801 the illegitimate
children of a woman dying intestate share her
property equally with her legitimate children.
Shirt-collar stiff, without a tie,
Becliamonded, and gold pince-ncz,
Demeanour ’aughty, likewise ’igb,
Our matchless, aitchless M.L.A.
Is off to England, s’elp our boh,
A specimen colonial man,
Expenses paid. Who worked the job?
Will some friend tell us if he can.
We wonder if the rumour’s true
Smith treated him who stole the mace
Free gratis, medicine inclu-
Ded. Had he pimples on the face?
But what about the patients left
Behind to mourn—their cure a myth.
We hear them groaning, all bereft,
“ Have mercy! where’s that Guinea Smith.”
Distributing his pamphlets free
We see him ’mongst the English swells
Advising gratis Albert E.,
And finis—“ Rise, Sir Smith, 2££’s.
Crawlalong.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The “township” of Crawlalong lies “out-
back.” It is so often mentioned in stock reports
that city people regard it as being quite an
important place. The traveller, on first arriving
there, will most likely ask the coachdriver how
much further it is to Crawlalong ; and the driver
will answer, with a twinkle in his eye :
“This is it!”
The principal buildings are “The Carriers’
Arms,” the blacksmith’s shop, the “Commercial
Stores,” and a weather-board box, roofed with
galvanized iron, and called a “cottage.” The
main street is that portion of the Government
road extending from the blacksmith’s shop to the
pub.—about 50 yards. The smithy is a slab shed
with a flat, bark roof and kerosene-tin flue. The
pub. is a low, weatherboard building with a very
low verandah round two sides, and a door in the
corner. The verandah slouches down over the
door and windows like the hat of the common back-
lane variety of larrikin. The bar is low, dark,
dingy, and altogether evil-looking. The sinister
doors and windows look furtively out from under
the verandah—like the eyes of the aforesaid
larrikin scowling under his hat-brim.
The water-supply of Crawlalong is invisible.
The town’s average temperature is Sheol in the
shade. Visible population : Two men going in to
have a drink, and a drunkard lying bareheaded in
the blazing heat by the dusty roadside. Trade :
A bullock-team and a hawker’s waggonette stand-
ing outside the “Carriers’ Arms.” Politics:
Freetrade and Protection. Religion : Liquor and
horse-racing. Church : The “ Carriers’ Arms:”
Principal Imports : Beer, rum, brandy, whisky,
gin, flour, tea, and sugar. Exports: Dead
drunkards—as far as the cemetery. Products :
Jim-jams. Chief Industry; “Lambing-down.”
Principal occupation of population when not dead-
drunk : Fighting three luke-warm rounds fear-
fully, and shaking hands afterwards with great
enthusiasm.
* « *
The town is supported by a few old shearers
who come there regularly to be shorn. The
government is communistic to a certain extent, and
“liberty prevails,” but the law of the land is
represented by two mounted troopers who visit
the place about once a year, and borrow—in the
Queen’s name—a horse and dray and pick and
shovel from the local blacksmith, and bury the
remains of any dead bushmen that may be lying
round.
HENRY LAWSON.
Pretty Lady Randolph Churchill is said to
be suffering from a painful disease common among
American-born women.
In Victoria, cancer claims the enormous
percentage of five in men and twelve-and-a-half in
women of all the deaths that occur of persons
above the ago of 35.

1800, had a congregation of CO in the morning and
92 at night.
A good deal of settlement is now taking
place on the west coast of South Australia,
hitherto held bv squatters, and for trying to open
which Bartlett, M.P., has been constantly laughed
at for the last four years.
. S.M. Herald has got so confoundedly
cautious of late in its references to more or less
convicted criminals, that any day it may be
expected to allude to the “ alleged treachery of
the late Mr. Judas Iscariot.”
A quaint suicide was Neil M'Auley, who
was found standing upright under water in Boma-
derry Creek, Nowra way (N.S.W.), a
mutilated prayer-book handy on the bank. The
inference is that the old crank read his own burial-
service and then waded into eternity.
Specimen gush from a letter by Rev. J.
B. Sharp, of Fitzroy (Vic.); —
I had a sweet but unexpressed dream that I might con-
scientiously remain in my pretty cottage home, and
among mine own loving and loyal people, and evade the
Fitzroy sodden streets, foulsome and low.
Dear Bulletin, —I have noticed some
forty instances of the use of “ warrigal ” for
“wild horses,” by genuine sons of the bush, not
merely literary bushmen —never once its use for
“ wild cattle ”or “ wild dog.” O’Hara’s lines ;
And here the wild swan used to swim,
The warrigal was heard.
■ might suggest anything from the dingo to the
bull-frog, with the exception of the wild horse.
That’s the worst of learning your bush-lore on
suburban pavements and among the stuffed
carcases of the Museum.—Yours, X.
Dear Bulletin, —
Re the New Australia
scheme. I have just
received a letter from
Argentina, written by
a man who has excep-
tional opportunities for
observation. The fol-
lowing extracts are
worth your notice
“Like all South Ameri-
can Republics, we are
in a continual state of
muddle. Every other
day there is a bank
failure or a revolution.
At the time of writing
(April 7th, 1892), the
country is declared to
be in a state of siege,
and the leaders of the
Radical party are in
prison. This is said to
be only a dodge to get
them out of the way
while a now President
is elected. After that,
we shall either settle
down quietly or have a
revolution. Now, with
regard to business. I
would strongly advise
you not to come to this
country, as things at
present are in a fearful
state, and the railways,
etc., are on the verge of
bankruptcy. The cur-
rency is greatly depreci-
ated. With regard to
camp (bush) life, the
pay is simply wretched ;
about 30s. a month,
and very few English
fellows go to the camp,
and then only to gain
experience, with a view
of taking up land. It
is very hard to get on
with the natives, how-
ever well you speak
their lingo. Their ways
are so different, and, I
may say, dirty. Later :
I have just seen in the
papers that the leaders
of the Radical patty
are to be exiled to an
island near the Magel-
lan Straits. To-day
(April 10) the elections
took place, but only one party went to the poll
the others were afraid of a row, and kept safely to
their homes. How would that style suit the
kangaroos ? I may tell you that it is only Argem
tines who cai’e to take any part in politics, and
Argentines are only those born in the country ; so
you see foreigners cannot take any interest in the
affairs, and never talk politics. There is not much
opening, therefore, for a man like B here.
The worst thing about the climate are the storms,
or pamperos. When one of these is in full blast
you have to look out, for houses go down like
cards (the houses are built chiefly of mud), and the
wind is so strong as to stop trains. It is no un-
common thing to see the guards’ reports with
‘ two hours detained by heavy wind, as the ex-
planation of delay,”—C.R., Melb.

Our Ambassador; or, Sharp Practice on
the Darling.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
There is an old, furthest-out bushman who
comes to Sydney once every year and always calls
upon certain members of the staff of this paper.
He seems to regard himself as an ambassador
from the backblocks, and he lays down the law on
all bush subjects in great style. He is a bearded,
freckled old pirate, with a bald-head. His hands
are scarred with “ Barcoo rot.” There are not
many parts of Australia that the ancient doesn’t
know; and to hear him chin off the bush
names is a perfect treat. He has a slightly Scotch
accent, and he was at first suspected of being
“Scotty the Wrinkler ” in disguise, come “to take
a rise ” out of us. He always has a backblock
story or two which he thinks would be highly
suitable for publication. They never are. They
mostly consist of yarns about the things which
some renowned bullock-driver said to his bullocks
when he was fast in a river with a flood coming
down. Or else he tells how, out in the far-back
country, the supplies got low and the only flour
available was full of weevils and other live stock, and
the storekeeper used to classify the flour according
to the condition of the creeping things which in-
habited it. The fatter the crawlers were, the
better quality the flour was held to be, so that
“ prime fat flour ” sold at a considerable advance
over that which was only in “store condition.”
His great idea is to have these stories illustrated,
a process to which they do not lend themselves
readily. But when he came to town this last time
he was so genuinely distressed at none of his
previous yarns having appeared that he was
promised faithfully that “Sharp practice on the
Darling ” should be published, even if the editor
had to be knocked on the head.
« # #
“I’ve just come in from the Paroo,” he
said, “ with cattle. And cornin’ down in the
camp the fellers was sayin’ I ought to come and
tell you some of the things as is happenin’ up our
BREAD AND LOVE.
MISTRESS : “Mary, this bread is as bad as ever. Did you change the
baker last week as I told you!”
MARY : “Well no, Mum. You see it ain’t convenient just now, as I’m
expecting him to declare himself any morning.”
way now. There’s some tremenjus funny things
in the bush, you know.”
“Yes,” we said. “What is the funniest thing
that has happened lately ?”
“Well,” he replied, “ we reckoned this was the
funniest. We talked over a crowd of things in
the camp, but we reckoned this was the best. It
would make good pictures, too,” he said, earnestly.
“We call it sharp practice on the Darlin’River
just now, and if a man has a fi’ pun’-note he can’t
get past a public-house with his life, you know.
They’ll get that fiver out of him, somehow. But
there was a mate of mine that took ’em down all
right ’’—and the old man chuckled at the remem-
brance.
“ How did he take ’em down? Did he swallow
the fiver and then cough it up after he got past the
hotel ?” we enquired.
“No,” he said. “When he went to the pub. he
had three fivers, and he gave the landlord two of
’em, and started a spree. So I come along and
was havin’ a drink with him, and he showed me
the other fiver. ‘ I’m goin’ to get away with this
one,’ says he. ‘ Does the publican know you’ve
got it ?' says I. ‘Yes,’ says he. ‘ Well, he’ll have
it off you somehow,’ I says. ‘ There’s no publican
on the Darlin’ River has let a man with five
pounds get past him this seven years,’ I says,
‘and you ain’t goin’ to be the fust man to do it.’
‘ Won’t I?’ says he. ‘ You see, now, I’ll get away
with it all right.’
“So, when he’d about cut out his ten pounds,
he slips down to the river one day, and takes the
canoe, and paddles across, and makes off down the
river to strike the coach road. The publican come
out and ‘ Where’s Hazard gone?’ says he. Hazard
was the name this mate of mine went by, and he
was a tremenjus clever fellow—a real larrikin. ‘ I
don’t know,’says I, ‘ where he’s gone.’ ‘Well,
says the publican, ‘he owes me some money and
I must have him back.’ So he calls two chaps ha
had there, and he sends one to swim the river
with a horse and go down the further side, and tha
other to go down the near side, and they was
bound to have him that way. So Hazard, he was
makin’ down the river and he saw this cove cross
over after him, and just then there was a steamer
coming up the river. And he didn’t want to go
up the river, you know, but it was any port in a
storm with him, so he runs to the bank and waves
his swag, and the steamer sent a boat and took
him off. And as he went up past the public-house
he saw the landlord standing there, and he ran to
the stern of the steamer and waved the fi-pun note
at him. ‘Ha! ha! old man,’ he says, ‘this is
sharp practice on the Darling !’ ”
* * *
We agreed that this story would appeal
strongly to bush readers. We also took notes
of another narrative about a publican and a
boozer. In this case the boozer had some pound
notes and he let them blow out of his hand in the
yard on a windy day, and then he, and the
publican, and a retriever pup, were down on all-
fours together in the dirt grabbing at the notes as
they blew about. The puppy secured the bulk of
the capital. The old man pointed out that this
would make a spirited illustration, and we
promised to have a full-page drawing made of it
when the artists had time. He went on to,chat
about things in general,
“About this Argentina scheme, now,” he said,
“ you fellows in Sydney don’t think that the bush-
men are in earnest over that.”
“Well, are they’” we enquired. “It doesn’t
look a very good game to leave this country and
go to a strange place where they’ll get potted like
’possums in the first revolution.”
“Ah, well, they’re in earnest about it,” he said,
“ I’m not going myself, but there was six men at
the last shed I was at that are going, and they
have put down their stuff, too. It seems a pity,
don’t it, for them to go away. Fine strappin’
young fellows as the sun ever shone on ! But it’s
terrible hard to get a livin’ these times.” he said,
“ if you go shearing and get one shed it’s all you’ll
do. And in the old times they was glad to get
shearers. If it wasn’t for the rabbits there’d bo
no work at all on a lot of the runs.”
“Yes, they’re too clever for men to keep out,”
he answered ; “they burrows under the wire nett-
ing, and they can’t be stuffocated in their burrows
nohow, ’cos they camp out under the saltbush
these times. And they crosses the Murray River
where it’s a quarter of a mile wide.”
“ Oh, come ! that’s too stiff. You don’t tell us
the rabbits can swim a quarter of a mile ? ”
“ In corse they don’t,” he said ; “ they burrows
underneath it! ”
After that we took him out and stood him a
drink, and sent him on his way rejoicing.
THE BANJO.
The infamous, insolent Queensland Coali-
tion now intend to face the country on a “one-
man-one-vote ” programme. Should they manage
to get home, the Upper House will be nudged to
floor the measure every time. Every district
which returns a Griffilwraith, no matter what he
promises, will be a blackleg constituency.
Editor Bulletin, —Captain Mackay’s re-
marks quoted in last week’s Bulletin re the
little island of Tucopia offering a field for mis-
sionary enterprise may possibly be the means of
sending some good man, eager to further exploit
the Lord’s Vineyard, on a soulless chase, and this,
no doubt, would pain The Bulletin. He, would
be too late, for both on isolated Tucopia and
Anouda (Cherry Island) there are resident native
teachers of the usual sort manufactured in the
Fijian and Tongan training schools, white-shirted,
sulu’d, and neck-tied, oleaginous, canting and
dollar - grabbing. It would be a hard matter
now to find any island where living is good
and money to be collected (or its equivalent in
copra) where you won’t find a native missionary—
especially does this apply to Polynesia. Of
Melanesia these gentry are rather shy, there being
too much club and spear and too little harvest.
Some of the teachers, however, are very good
fellows—especially the L.M.S. Samoans in the
Ellice and Line Islands—with a noted pen-
chant for filling their houses with the best local
beauty as servants, nurses, etc. When the first
Samoan teacher landed on Nanomaga (Hudson’s
Island), in the Ellice Group, a meeting was called
of the “Kaupule” (town council) and the following
rigid laws passed :
Neglecting to attend daily morning prayers, flue of
sdols. (one-half to go to teacher).
Neglecting to attend Sabbath-day service, fine of Sdols.
(in cash or mats).
Cooking on Sunday, sdols.
Fishing on Sunday, lOdols.
Covering a canoe with mats from the sun on Sunday,
sdols.
Adultery, Idol, (first offence), and Idol. 50c. for second.
Seduction of a betrothed girl by a married man, 1 dol.
or 200 cocoanuts.
Seduction of a betrothed girl by a single man, 50c. or
100 cocoanuts.
Wife-beating, 10 head of puraka (a species of coarse
taro) or 100 cocoanuts.
Calling out “ May your throat rot!” “May your wife
conceive devils!” &c., &c., 50c.
Smoking inside church enclosure on any day, Sdols.
And so on ad infinitum. In the course of a few
months the teacher’s stipend had grown fat,
doubling his income of 100 dols. from Gospel
headquarters. Then, as most of the young men
and girls were hopelessly insolvent and plunged
up to the necks in fines (not, it is hardly necessary
to state, through any infringement of religious
observances), the teacher went back to Samoa, and
a smart, active young fellow came with a pretty
young wife and inducted some sense into the
“Kaupule,” doing away with all fanaticism and
teaching them to" live cleanly. As showing the
innate honesty of these people, a trader who lived
there nearly two years with a large stock of goods
lying about loose in a native-built house without
either doors or keys, states that he never had one
cent’s worth stolen from him. Everyone on the
island came in and went out as they pleased.—B.
