late private secretary to Gov. Jersey, has been
married to Miss Evelyn Gathorne Hardy. By
the way, there are some queer rumours afloat as
to the amatory exploits of certain young gentle-
men in a certain Government House circle.
Blaine, whose death is cabled, illustrated
Emerson’s saying, “The more you take away
from a great man, the greater he is.” He was
foiled for the Presidency by Hayes, Garfield,
Cleveland, Harrison, and again this time. Yet
he always went one higher. He grasped the full
meaning of Gladstone’s saying, that in 1993 the
U.S. will have 500 million people. Beyond
Monroe doctrine he went, and unwisely, in trying
to annex Hawaii, Cuba, and Hayti, but otherwise
his ideas about American unity were noble —all
North and South America to be one.
N.S. VY. Treasurer See, in his financial
statement, mentioned that the revenue derived
from spirits showed a decrease of £36,000 during
the last year. Yet no one seems to know where
the shortage comes in. The old-established
brands show no falling off in consumption if we
may judge from the following statistics : During
the past week Messrs. Sandeman, the agents for
John Robertson & Sons’ famous JDR whisky,
have imported 5000 cases, equalling 10,000 gallons,
or 60,000 bottles. If all these bottles were put in
line they would reach from the G.P.0., Sydney,
to Parramatta, and the corks alone would extend
from Sydney G.P.O. to Redfern Railway Station.
When we consider that there are numerous other
brands in consumption the question arises : what
distance would they all reach if put in one line ?
No one but a profound mathematician refreshed
with the non-headache-producing JDR whisky
could tackle such a problem and come out alive at
the other end.
Dear 1 Bulletin,—l notice in Bulletin
just to hand, a letter from “Teamster,” tdbching
the camel question. Let me, as a digger, give my
views on the subject. The four teams that left
the Cross to cut the road to Gnarlbine, “120
miles,” charged .£ll2 per ton. They got to their
journey’s end in two weeks, and used neither
shovel nor axe upon the way ; and they tried to
keep up this monstrous imposition. Had it not
been for the camels taking loading from York at
4d. per lb. (300 miles), they would have succeeded,
too. The horses are a perfect nuisance : they
drink the water-holes dry, and hang over the holes
when unable to help themselves. The poor things
beg a drink from each man who bales a billy of
water. This occurred on my way down. The
teams drive tandem. One particular team had
not watered for two whole days. When we over-
took two swagsman with full bags of water, the
horses, smelling it, encircled the men, and took
not the slightest heed of the driver’s “gee-up.”
The men parted a drink for the poor nags, which
carried them on to more water. Every digger here
who can afford it, dispenses with his horse and gets
a camel. I have found teamsters (i.e., natives) the
grandest, bravest and most generous of men, but
the old Greenlander, with the harvest stubble
round his jaw, and the cockney Jew, are such
natural usurers, that they cannot recognise their
impostures, believing themselves to be the most
liberal-minded drivers on the road. — Fossioker.
Southern Cross, Westralia, Jan. 18, 1893.
IT’S GONE, ALL GONE, AND I’M GOING
TOO.
For many a year did the same man sweep a
certain street-crossing in Hampstead. Through
all seasons and in all weathers, there he was,
sweeping the crossing and taking such gratuities
as were given him. Time wore away, and he came
to be eighty years old. He appeared at his post
no more. A lady district visitor looked him up at
his lodgings. What a picture of squalid destitu-
tion. No fire, no food, no friends. Wife and
family he had none—never had. The poor old
fellow was perishing of starvation, of want. Borne
money was raised for his benefit and he was re-
moved to a London hospital. Here he lay several
weeks, sinking daily. One night he was clearly
very low. Near him stood one of the hospital
physicians and a nurse. Seeing him clutch
nervously at his pillow, the nurse, supposing the
patient desired to be raised up, put his arm.
beneath him to perform that service. In doing
so the nurse’s hand came in contact with an
object which he withdrew. It was a dirty little
canvas bag tied with a leather string. As it was
laid aside the old man perceived what had been
done, lifted his skeleton frame partially from the
bed and, trembling with excitement, said in a
shrill whisper, “Ah ! my treasure, my treasure !
It’s gone, all gone, and I’m going too !’’ and sank
back dead. The bag contained <£soo in notes —
the savings of his miserly life. And he, there,
dead of starvation, even more than of age. Well,
what of it ? you say. The wretched old man was
better dead than alive. Quite so, but most
human events have a moral, a lesson, about them,
if we keep an eye out for it.
What, for example, can we learn from the
following facts ? —One night about ten years ago a
man, whose name we can furnish, went to bed as
usual, apparently in good health and spirits. A
few hours later he lay unconscious on the floor.
In explanation he stated that he had been seized,
suddenly, with a pain of such violence that he was
compelled to rise—a pain in the chest. After
rising he lost his senses and sank down on the
spot where he had stood. His wife, aroused by
the noise, struck a light and saw her husband in
that situation. She afterwards declared he had
gone black in the face, and that his eyes looked as
if they were starting out of his head.
Restoratives were applied which brought him
to, but he was not as before. So quickly and un-
expectedly do we cross the boundary line between
two opposite bodily conditions. It is like
stepping from the broad blaze of day into a damp
cavern packed with darkness. He felt weak and
sick, with a strange “all-gone” sensation through-
out his whole system. His mouth tasted badly,
and was filled with a slimy sort of phlegm, his
head ached, he was unable to draw a deep breath,
he walked with difficulty, and went about his
business like a man who is haunted by a para-
lysing dream.
Perplexed and alarmed he consulted physicians,
who prescribed for him, without, however, pro-
ducing any noticeable improvement. The strong,
clear-headed man of previous years was gone—
changed as by the wand of a vicious magician into
the feeble being he now was. Even with this
dismal prospect before him our friend travelled
not on level ground ; his path led downward ; he
grew worse. In December, 1888, he had a dis-
tinct and bad attack, gave up business, and went
to bed. There he remained for a weary painful
month—thirty days, as long as thirty years of
power and occupation. The doctor said there was
something wrong with the stomach and bowels.
After he once more rose from his bed he still
suffered dreadful pain and could rest neither day
or night. Indeed, some nights he never slept a
moment. So weak had he become that when he
attempted a short walk he was obliged to aban-
don the effort, return and go to bed.
His own words are these: —“To give you an
idea how reduced I had become I may mention
that I lost over three stone weight and was wast-
ing away. I kept on like this until January,
1891, when Mr. Everson, of Occold, told me of a
medicine called Mother Seigel’s Syrup and the
good it had done. I tried it and in three days I
felt better. Cheered and encouraged by this I
continued to use it, with the result that I wholly
recovered from my mysterious malady. lam now
strong and hearty, and business is again a
pleasure. The Syrup did me more good in a few
weeks than all my ten years doctoring put to-
gether.” (Signed) Albert Thorndyke, Proprietor
of the “Grapes Inn,” Church Street, Eye,
Suffolk. May Ist, 1891.
What do we learn from this? We learn that
while a miserly fool like our crossing-sweeper may
starve for money, a wise man with more reverence
for his bodily temple, seeks and finds a remedy
for a tendency to starvation , induced by disease —
that the disease was indigestion and dyspepsia,
and the remedy Mother Seigel’s Syrup.


A fine constitution may be ruined by simple
neglect. Many bodily ills result from habitual
constipation. There is no medicine equal to
Ayer’s Pills for restoring the system to natural
and healthy action.

I“ The Bulletin’s” Missing
Word Competition.
Missing Word Competitions have become a perfect
mania in England and elsewhere. The Bulletin
is going to inaugurate them in Australia.
Any purchaser of The Bulletin who can
correctly supply the word missing from the para-
graph on the subjoined coupon—which coupon is
to be cut out and forwarded to The Bulletin,
accompanied by one shilling, will be paid the total
amount received in response to this advertisement.
Should there be more than one successful com-
petitor, the total amount received will be divided
amongst such successful competitors pro rata.
Coupons will be received at The Bulletin
•Office within four weeks from the 21st of J anuary,
1893.
The missing word is placed in a sealed envelope
in the custody of the manager of the Bank of
'New Zealand, Sydney, and the contents are
known only to the manager of this paper.
When the prizes are awarded, the winning
■word and the names and addresses of the success-
ful competitors will be published in The Bulle-
tin.
Should it happen that no one succeeds in naming
the word, the whole of the money will be added
to that of the succeeding competition, particulars
of which will duly appear.
The sum of one shilling may be forwarded by
Post-office order, postal note, or stamps of any
province ; when the latter are sent, an additional
penny to be added for exchange.

This is a business announcement, and that is
just the reason why you should read it right
through. One of the greatest
SECRETS OF THE DAY
is to successfully combat diseases and attain to a
serene and comfortable old age. Strategy is the
greatest
ART OF WAR.
By the aid of strategy you can get the enemy into
a fog and lose him there. Of course it is not con-
sidered strategy to find him again. But no
matter. A vegetarian tract recently published
imparts the cheerful intelligence that more people
are killed or disabled by
EXCESSIVE EATING
than by drink. This is, doubtless, true. Take
the tramp, for instance, who lives principally on
suction, and see how hardy he is. His excuse for
drinking is that it aids digestion. You can get
trichinosis from eating pork, he tells you, but not
from
DRINKING BEER.
About the only thing you can drink that is
really injurious to health is water. When you
move into a strange city you are generally advised
to “look out for the water.’’ The best way to
carry this instruction into execution is to inquire
for whisky at the threepenny bars.
NO SANE MAN,
however, will think of drinking St. Jacobs Oil,
because it is not a beverage for internal use. But
if he is troubled with neuralgia or rheumatism, or
is of
AN ACHING TOOTH,
a cold on the chest, or a sprained foot, reason will
dictate to him that an external remedy such as St.
Jacobs Oil, which never fails to effect a speedy
and permanent cure, is worth
A MILLION OF MONEY.
No other specific ever placed in the trade has
met with such unprecedented success, a fact which
is verified by the numberless testimonials re-
ceived proclaiming its sterling value. It is beyond
doubt the
BEST DOMESTIC REMEDY
that can be kept in the hduse, whether the home
be that of a merchant prince or the humble son of
toil.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Eight hundred pounds taken at Sydney Asso-
ciation Ground sates on Thursday last, the opening day
of the Victoria-New South Wales match. After this, the
authorities might well think about erecting seme kind ot
shelter from the blazing sun for the “ bob-a-head ’ oontin-
jrent—a sort of a leger stand, as it were. The old Albert
Ground used to boast of a covering for the rank and file,
and, considering the tenure on which the Association holds
a portion of the people’s park, they might now give the
palatial structures for their own comfort a small rest and
put up some sort of a cowshed for the crowd who actually
keep the game going.
* * ■*
The feature of the Interprovincial match was
Coningham’s play. He obtained a splendid average in
bowling against first-class men on very good wickets. His
method seemed to be to deliver three or four over-arm
balls of medium pace, and then to suddenly whizz in a
round-armer at tremendous speed. He clean-bowled most
of his victims in this way, and several times sent the
stumps flying out of the ground. He certainly should be
taken to England with our Eleven, as he is a man of
splendid physique and an enthusiastic cricketer. His
style of bowling should suit the wet wickets of England.
* * *
Iredale was not given a chance to show his
bowling abilities against the Victorians. This is strange,
as changes in the bowling were constantly tried and he
has a good recoid as a change bowler. Can it be that the
fact of hi's not belonging to the Carlton club had anything
to do with it? His batting was first class, quite equal to
Graham’s, and if the Australian Eleven were being picked
on its merits instead of on a family basis Iredale would
certainly be selected.
* * *
Pierce holds the position of gatekeeper at the
Hospital for Lunatics, at Gladesvilie. He will have the
job of locking Coiiingham up one of these days, to judge
by the latter’s antics in the field.
# * *
Fielding is the weak point of N.S.W. cricket.
Good batsmen and bowlers are ot no avail while execrable
fielding gives the opposing eleven life after life. The good
field who saves 40 runs, but goes out for a duck, is of
more service to his side than the brilliant bat who scores
39, but gives4o to his opponents by loose fielding.
# * *
For instance, ere Bruce had scored a run in
Victoria’s second innings he should have been run but,
but Pierce and Wales mulled the chance between them.
This piece of loose fielding alone gave the Victorians 86
runs.
• • *
In the second innings of the Victorians, leaving
out the chance Bruce gave Pierce and Wales before he had
scored, he was missed by Pierce when his score stood at
eio-iit • Trott was missed by Coningham before he had
made a run; Barrett was missed by Callaway before he
scored; and Graham was missed by Pierce when he had
six to his credit. These were all reasonable chances, and
had they been taken the Victorian score would just have
totalled 50.
# * *
Pierce’s laziness in the field —or, more properly
speaking his affectation of laziness—should for ever
debar him from inclusion in a representative team.
Pierce’s general cricket is not of a sufficiently high order
to allow of him airing a fad in the field.
•. • •
The fates seemed altogether against New South
Wales. After being 70 runs behind in the first innings,
their chances were spoilt by wet weather, and in the
result “ Wales ” was defeated by 232 runs.
* * *
Bruce and M‘Leod took a lot of “pressing”
before consenting to go to Sydney. They “ didn I like to
be so often away from the bank.” Which workmanlike
sentiment getting whispered in the right quarter, a
deputation of M.C.C. officials to Frank Grey Smith,
manager of the bank and president of the club, resu Itha
in the granting of what is euphemistically called
“ accountant’s leave, ** and —‘‘ Messrs. Bruce and M Leod
were induced to alter their minds.”
* # *
A. E. Trott, brother of Harry, plays also for
South Melbourne, and generally keeps his end up while
the international min hits. Meanwhile, the proud father
officiates in the scoring-box, and follows the operations of
his offspring with snatches of soliloquy; “ Another 4 to
Harry -2 to Harry-missed that one—that’s an uppish
one I'll talk to him to-night about it—a single to Albert
Edward-4 to Harry, that boy plays as well to-day as lie
did four years ago ; Pd talk about him if he was a
perfect stranger—another single to Albert Edward—3 to
Harry.” Small hoy puls his head in: “’Ow many’s
’Any Trott now?” ‘‘Ninety-eight, my boy—another
single to Albert Edward-200 up—all the way I—4 to
Harrv— made his hundred ! Ha!”
* * *
George Giffen was once employed in the pub-
lishing department ot S. A, Advertiser.
* * *
George Giffen works in the S. A. General Post-
office and can he spared at any time without interfering
with the efficiency of the department. Walter Giffen
makes meters for the S.A. Gas Co. and cannot be given
holidays without causing inconvenience. That is why he
didn’t play in the recent interprovincial matches.
# # *
George Giffcn’s club average is, at present, 11)9
for 4 innings ; twice not out. Giffen has made 189 and
172 not out this season. Walter, the other day, made 5,
run out.
# # *
The writer gets this on splendid authority ; If
Walter Giffen does not go to England, George doesn’t go.
This settles any doubts about an understanding as to
George’s terms, which the selection committee quite
understood when Walter was picked.
# # *
“ Huge ” Trumble ran up a pair of duck-eggs in
the S.A. match—bowled third ball by Jones each innings
—but no one shrieks against his selection for England on
account of “lost form.” Yet several of the Australian
Eleven, as at present picked, have been decried because
of small scores in one or two recent matches—notably
Bannerraan.
# * *
“Jim” Barron has beaten another man in
America, and is slowly working bis way to better things.
Barron was no pick-up before be left Sydney, where he
found it hard work to find “ tucker ” for self and family,
but Barron, well nourished and trained, should make it
sultry for the best of his weight, wherever they may hail
from.
# * *
The “ respectable” press of Melbourne has at
last taken up The Bulletis’s cry that cronk racehorse
owners are “ killing the goose that lays the golden eggs.
Says the Argus; “Latterly, patrons of the sport have
become thoroughly disgusted with the questionable pro-
ceedings witnessed in connection with racing on every one
of the metropolitan courses, and the attendances have, as
a consequence, fallen off.” No habit grows upon rational
business men faster than that of stopping away from race-
meetings, and now that barefaced swindles are the rule,
even at Fkmington, the uninitiated public, which used to
get some fnn for its money whilst James White was
alive, seems to be stopping away altogether. The result of
this falling off in the supply of punters’ cash is a general
tendency towards insolvency among the rogues, who needs
must swindle more and more to earn their daily bred.

To a southern scribe, I*. T. Heywooa recalls
how he attended a certain memorable sale tor the sole
purpose of buying Carbine, but a whisper from Mick
O’Brien induced him to give .£5O more for Tradition.
• # *
The last Caulfield steeplechase was of six horses
trying to get last.
Donald Dinnie benefits in Melbourne under
chaperonage of Miller. Dinnie is now 56, and without
doubt the most wonderfully enduring athlete ever before
the public.

bourne Caledonian gathering, beating the Victorian ama-
teur record, and being 4in. higher than the next man.
The committee, however, disqualified Michael tor not

words to that effect.

A seaman on board the barque Ohipperkyle at
Newcastle, N.S.W., will, at any hour of the day, for halt-
a-sov., dive from the maintopsail-yard of his vessel a
distance of over 60 feet. He dives with all his clothes on,
head-first, and with only one arm extended m front of
him, the other being laid along his side. He is willing to
high-dive anyone on earth.

Dear Bulletin, —Millionaire Sydney Burdekin
M.L.A., offers todonate, up to £lOO, £3 for every £1 sub-
scribed towards holding a regatta on the Hawkesbury
River, provided that “no intoxicants are allowed. How
“ not allowed?” If Burdekin attends, I should like to be
in the neighbourhood of his luncheon-hamper.—D.
* * *
A Wentworth correspondent of an Adelaide
(S. A.) paper tells a racing yarn of the sixties, when John
Crozier had Kulnine station, on the Murray. John
wanted to time the speed of a horse named Phantom,
which he had bought and taken up from Gawler tor some
local races. One Kennedy assisted. Joe Langcake was
jockey. Crozier stood, stopwatch in hand, listening for
the voice of Kennedy. Sure enough, the latter presently
sang out: “ He’s off ! he’s off !” “Right!” called Crozier,
“I’ve got him.” “ But I say he’s off!” “Yes, and I say
i’ve got him all right.” “ I’m blessed if you have. He s
bucked off t”
* * *
The Indian game of polo is becoming the
fashionable amusement in Sydney and Melbourne. It is
somewhat more risky than steeplechasing. So far, in
Sydney, the main casualties have been an aide-de-camp
lamed, probably for life, a lawyer severely hurt m the
eye, and a newspaper owner’s forehead laid open- In
Melbourne, one man got his nose knocked absolutely flat
with his face, and a spectator got his leg broken through
merely looking on at a match. A team of Indian ofnceis
proposes to come out and show “ the colonials all about
this wild amusement. In India, they kill two or three
men a year at it. In N.S. W., theMonaro men havefoimed
a club at Cooma, and once they get fairly going, the
“men from Snowy River ” will make the Indians ride for
their lives. There are no better ponies in the world than
the mountain-bred ponies of Monaro, and no better riders
than the Monaro men.
* * •
A Sydney Sunday paper says “the mistake
continues to be made that Little Bernie is by Cheviot, full
brother to Sir Modred. As a matter of fact he is by
Adventurer.” Truth is here stranger than fiction. Little
Bernie is by Cheviot, full brother to Sir Modred, and
always has been. Mr. Rouse, breeder ot Little Bernie,
has a horse called Cheviot, who is by Adventurer, but
who is in mo way responsible for Little Bernie s existence.

T>m evergreen Charley Messenger won the All-
Comers Light Skiff Handicap. Messenger, who is a son
of the old-time English champion, was the inventor of
the present style of light skiff with a wager-boat bottom.
The light ski if of the Punch, M'Grath, Donnelly and
Connors era had a V-shaped bottom.

The Asylum Experiences of a Dipso-
maniac.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
No. 11.
I entered the Tyson Sanatorium, Rand-
wick, Sydney, on 15th Dec,, 1892, with several
well-defined and justifiable impressions, born of
Manager Sterling’s conversation with me on the
previous Monday. These impressions may be
thus summarised: —l was to make myself
thoroughly at home and to ask for anything in
reason that I felt I wanted —alcoholic stimulants,
or beef-tea, or coffee, or tea—at any time of the
day; I was religiously to take “ the treatment ”
every hour, commencing at 8 o’clock a.rn. and
lasting until 8 p.m., or 13 doses each day, and it
was promised that I would soon acquire an abso-
lute “disgust against liquor,;’’ I would meet at
least one patient already practically cured by the
Tyson method ; Mr. Sterling would generally be
present to administer “ the treatmentbut, in
his absence, his assistant, Mr. Franklin,would be
on the premises, and in Franklin’s absence, “ the
matron ” would always be there and ready to
afford her services; that visiting physicians were
attached to the Sanatorium ; that the treatment
had been explained to and approved by Dr.
MacCarthy, of Sydney (whom I know personally);
and that I rather invited to take “liquor”
whenever I felt “so dispoged ” than expected to
fight shy of it.
As more or less of a chemist, I knew of a dozen
different means by which I could dispel existing
“jimjams,” but Sterling was confident that “The
Tyson Treatment ” would cure me permanently of
all desire for alcoholic beverages. That was a very
different result to anything I knew how to achieve.
I accepted this therapeutic challenge. I had a
disease; the Sanatorium people claimed to have a
cure for it. In three weeks I was to be turned
out as non-alcoholically inclined as I used to be in
the days of ray adolescence, before I took that
first drink of poison, which thousands of suc-
ceeding ones have failed to overtake. Alcohol
was to be chased out of my system in 21 times
13 rounds.
I underwent the test with the full sense of my
responsibility to readers of The Bulletin. It
was no easy task. On the one hand, I had to be
alert against self-deception; on the other, I
desired to give the Sanatorium every iota of
credit that was its just due. In the one balance
rested my own hopes and future prospects ; in the
other scale lay the possibilities to thousands who
might be influenced by my deliberately-recorded
opinions. The greatest danger of error, the wish
being father to the thought, lay in self-deception.
The cure must be absolute. The effort must be
made in complete obedience to the conditions pre-
scribed by my “enters.” Did they order me to
abstain from all intoxicants, I must comply. Did
they prefer that I should take whisky whenever I
felt inclined, it was my duty to drink it. The
results were not in the province of my own direc-
tion. I could merely watch and describe them.
That I personally disbelieved in taking alcohol
for the cure of alcoholic disease was not to the
point. The issue had to be decided by Tyson
views and Tyson conditions. I was entirely in
Tyson hands. As a matter of fact, I had passed
a whole day at home previously, in wretched but
heroic abstinence from grog.
Manager Sterling was away in the city when I
arrived at the Sanatorium—a fine, roomy house,
with over eight acres of picturesque grounds
attached. A lady asked me straight away if I
would “like anything an offer which I declined
nervously, being in a depressed mood from
alcoholic reaction. I was shown into an airy
upstairs room, having two windows opening out
upon two sides of a fine balcony, and containing
two comfortable single beds, one of which I at
once appropriated, the other remaining vacant
throughout my stay.
Upon one of the walls of my bedroom was
posted a printed notice - paper—“ Rules and
Regulations of the Tyson Sanatorium,” which
were ten in number. Of these I will trouble the
reader with only the following :
1. No Patient will be received for less than 21 days.
6. Patients must report themselves punctually at the
dispensing-room to receive their medicine at each hour
from 8 a.ra. till 8 p.m., inclusive, unless excused by the
physician in charge.
6. No Patient shall leave the premises without permis-
sion.
7. No intoxicating liquor shall be brought into the
Sanatorium by Patients or their friends. The physician
in charge will supply stimulants when necessary.
8. No articles of diet shall be brought into the Sana-
torium by Patients or Visitors.
No Patient shall smoke or use tobacco in any form for
the space of ten minutes before and after medicine.
Any Patient guilty of disobedience or non-compliance
with any of the above Rules and Regulations shall be
instantly expelled, and all moneys paid by him or on his
behalf shall be forfeited to the Institution ; nor shall any
Patient, having once been expelled, be permitted at any
future time to become an inmate of the Institution.
The Tyson Sanatorium Company,
H. FRANKLIN, Secretary.
Sydney.
I hereby agree to become an inmate of the Tyson Sana-
torium and to conform with the above Rules and Regula-
tions, and, in default, to forfeit all moneys paid by me.
Signed this day of 189 .
Witness,
I was not asked to sign any such document; but
I honestly complied with all its possible condi-
tions. There was no “physician in charge;”
neither was there any “visiting physician,”
though in answer to a direct question, days
Manager Sterling informed me that the doctor
already named was regarded as filling that office
in posse, but that the Sanatorium was not yet
fairly launched.
There was, I found, only one other patient in
the Sanatorium. On meeting him, I found that I
had enjoyed a slight acquaintance with him in
two other Australian capitals. He also was a
Test Patient, and may be itemised as No. 1, with
myself as No. 2. He had, he asserted, been pro-
minently connected with the wine-and- spirit
trade, and blamed drink for his social and busi-
ness downfall. He was enthusiastic over the
Tyson cure—days afterwards he declared that he
had never then meant the Tyson cure but a
mixture given him outside by Dr. MacCarthy—and
told me that he had been in the Sanatorium since
Saturday, December 3, and that he was a changed
man.
Manager Sterling arrived about 6 p.m. Accom-
panying him was a Sydney Echo reporter, who
stayed to dinner, and who subsequently “ wrote
up ” the institution. That he also was impressed
with No. I’s relation of his marvellous cure, and
that he, as I did, ascribed the cure to the “ Tyson
Treatment,” is abundantly evident from his'des-
criptive report.
As a matter of fact, up till December 15, No. 1
(on his subsequent admissions to myself and
others in my presence) had not taken one solitary
dose of the Tyson treatment until the same minute
that I took my first dose on the evening of
December 15. He had entered the Sanatorium,
per favour of a letter from Dr. MacCarthy, and
had taken a few doses of a mixture prescribed by
that medico.
I was not undeceived on this point until Dec.
19, and the Sydney press reports written between
those two dates, though some were not published
until after the 19th, show that the reporters were
similarly misled. Those reports contained abso-
lute untruths.
These misstatements were re-published as adver-
tisements in at least one Sydney newspaper, and
were quoted (possibly again as advertisements) in
a Melbourne evening paper.
To illustrate the audacity of some of the adver-
tised statements—advertised, by the way, as the
voluntary testimony of a reporter who “ dropped
in ” at Selborne, let me quote from a Sydney
Sunday paper article, bearing date Dec. 18, re-
specting No. 1 :
Now, after If days' treatment by the Tyson process, he
enjoys his meals, abhors all kinds of intoxicants, and
sleeps as soundly and as well as any person.
According to No. I’s later statements, by Dec.
18 he had only had two days’ experience of the
treatment, and not 14 days. No matter how
a reporter may have unwittingly erred, this
extract is from an advb.
The Australian Star (Sydney) of Dec. 19
blossomed out into a panegyric on the “Tyson
Cure.” The young-man reporter of that paper
had called at Selborne on the 16th, when No. 1 (as
well as myself) had only had 24 hours’ actual
experience of the “Tyson Treatment.” Iplenarily
excuse the Star visitor from misunderstanding the
unstinted praise of Tyson dealt out by No. 1.
Anyone listening to that individual’s gasconading
could form no other conclusion than that he had
experienced 12 days’ “ Tyson Treatment ” and had
become a new man. But whence the reporter
derived other scraps of information, it is not for
me to imagine :
The first ease under treatment, at Selborne ....
is being watched with a good deal of concern by a number
of medical men and others interested in the patient.
(Italics mine). Not a medical man ever visited
Selborne to my knowledge during my stay there :
He has now been undergoing the course for 13 dajs,
and a more complete change, &c.
He had “undergone the course” for one day
when the Star reporter visited us ; and for four
days, when the report appeared. Further on, the
same report refers more distinctly still to the
happy results achieved “ after 13 days’ treatment
by the Tyson process.”
In connection with the above, read the following
further extract from the same :
We have been given to understand that the managing-
director, Mr. Sterling, intends to shortly hold a public
meeting in the city for the purpose of introducing the
Tyson Cure to public notice, and on that occasion “ No.
1 Test Case ” will address the gathering. The gentleman
has done a good deal of public speaking, &c.
Yes, I know personally that he has a “ gift of the
gab ’’and is used to platform-speaking.
Now, all the Star’s precious tissue of misstate-
ments were reproduced in the Melbourne Stan-
dard. Accidentally ?
When, on December 19, I became aware of the
facts respecting No. I’s actual period of “ Tyson
Treatment,” when I remembered the preliminary
account of his admission and handling told me by
Sterling, when I recalled his own earnest assevera-
tions in witness of the good that the “ Tyson
Treatment” had effected in him (whereas he had
had no “Tyson Treatment” at all then), and when
I found that both Echo and Star reporters had
accepted and interpreted his statements as I
myself had accepted them and interpreted them—
what could I think ?
What I did think need not be stated. Sufficient
for test purposes should be my own personal
experience.
What is the “ Tyson Treatment ?” The
“dispensing-room ” at Selborne is anywhere.
The whole Tyson dispensary may be carried in
one’s hat. It consists of two small bottles—
probably 3oz. and l|oz.—labelled, as the first
test patients are styled, “No. 1 ” and “No. 2” —
and a glass-CMm-guttapereha “ dropper ” for mea-
suring “minims” or “drops.” The dose is—six
drops of No. 1, with three drops of No. 2, in
water, every hour—or 13 times a day. No. lis a
dark amber in colour ; No. 2 is greenish in tinge.
No. 1 is acrid in taste; No. 2is bitter. The
blend is of a quinine-like, or, more correctly,
strychnia-like bitterness to the palate. Mixed
together, a sediment is precipitated, so that the
two mixtures are “ incompatibles ” from a dis-
penser’s stand-point. This is evidently why two
separate bottles are required.
Upon water being added, the greasy-looking
drops cause that liquid to turn milky. This, to a
chemist, at onco indicates that both No. 1 and
No. 2 are alcoholic tinctures—it may be of essen-
tial oils that are insoluble in water, I have not
made the experiment, for the “treatment”
was not mine to play with; but I would wager
that, if tested, both No. 1 and No. 2, unwatered,
would take flame and burn to extinction of the
last drop. They present the aspect of tinctures
made with rectified spirits.
This at once suggests the debatable question—
Can strongly-alcoholic tinctures, even diluted
diffusely with water, be expected to drive all
alcohol completely out of the system ?
Otherwise Dr. Tyson’s treatment is intelligible
enough, and seemingly rational. His idea, to my
glimmering, appears to be this:—(l) To adminis-
ter, .at one and the same time, a sedative and
tonic—the combination also acting as a deobstru-
ent ; (2) to administer this remedy in one continu-
ous stream, like the drip from a curtain kept web
by capillary attraction, so that a combined effect
of uniform strength is always present.
In an ordinary 13-dose day, a patient absorbs
78 drops of No. 1 and 39 drops of No. 2 ; or 1 3-10th
“medical ” teaspoonfuls of the first and half that
quantity of the second—or, roughly, about an
average domestic teaspoonful and half-teaspoonful
respectively. Instead of exhibiting this in one
dose, which might or might not be hurtful, the
quantity is spread over the whole day.
I swallowed my first dose of the “ Tyson Treat-
ment ” before dinner on Thursday evening, Dec.
15, ’92, and my last on Thursday morning, Jan. 5,
’93. On my first evening I drank three Tysons,
three whiskies, and a sleeping-draught. Friday
saw me shaking down well to the routine—l 4
Tysons and three whiskies. Saturday I let pass
without alcohol; but made up for it by taking five
whiskies on Sunday, in honour of some Melbourne
Sanatorium visitors, among whom was the secre-
tary of the Melbourne Sanatorium, of whom more
anon.
So passed the days. The “Tyson Treatment”
was taken with unfailing regularity. Whisky
was generally accepted when proffered, and once
at lunch I had a glass of stout. There were indi-
gestion, flatulence, and diarrhoea, a bad cold,
neuralgia, headaches, listlessness,, drum-pulsa-
tions in the ears, with - dd spells of breezy cheer-
fulness of feeling; ' ut . fortnight elapsed and
whisky was not distasteful to the palate ; indeed,
at times of lowness of spirits, it was sinfully grate-
ful to the stomach.
To paraphrase a sentence in the special report
already quoted :
This new familiar 1 had taken to myself certainly did
tolerate and welcome alcohol in the form of whisky.

I may mention that, ordinarily, there was no
official dispenser.
Mr. Sterling went to the city every business
day, and “Mr. Franklin” was never visible to
my sight.. Neither have I come across anybody
who has met him. A pressman told me with an
air of confidential patronage that Sterling and
Franklin were one and the same individual. If
that be so, I had cause again to resent a deception
practised upon me in preliminary conversation.
These incidents sharpened my roused suspicions,
and I was at once struck with the assonant coinci-
dence that Sterling’s name is “Frank,” which he
is commonly called by his friends, and the secre-
tary’s name is Franklin.
On Christmas day, I drank one whisky, but,
noticing an apparent stiffness in the rela-
tions between host and patient, I resolved to go
without until the Thursday following, and then
to see whether liquor would be repulsive. And I
may mention that, during Christmas and Boxing
days, Mr. Sterling being at home all day, he (as
he afterwards informed me) doubled my Tysons
to 12 drops of No. 1, and 6 drops of No. 2 at each
dose. Nevertheless, on the Thursday evening, I
felt comforted internally when I took three doses
of whisky.
From that date, 29th Dec., I determined to
finish my term without the aid of any alcoholic
stimulant whatever, in order to see whether total
abstinence, in conjunction with the Tyson treat-
ment, would produce better results.
I had no whisky therefrom. This change of
programme was chiefly due to the morally suasive
fact that both The Bulletin and Manager
Sterling began to think that I was treating the
test too lightly, that I drank whisky from
bravado, and that I looked upon the Sanatorium
as a huge joke.
Nothing could be more unjust to me. I will
admit that the deceptions I have mentioned had
their weight in influencing the trend of my mental
examinings, If such statements as those that
appeared in the Sydney press, to be reprinted in
the Melbourne press, were so slightly connected
with the truth, what (I could not help asking
myself) was the value of the sheaves of rattling
testimonials of which the Sanatorium proudly
boasted ? Were all its alleged feats founded on
such flimsy basis of verity? Then the unre-
strictedly generous hospitality of Mr. Sterling,
the fact that I personally liked him, and was daily
breaking bread and eating salt in his close com-
panionship, and yet was in duty bound to treasure
up, note, and weigh these blemishes, made my
residence at Selborne unhappy and my duty
doubly difficult. A letter in a Sydney evening
paper, signed by Dr. MacOarthy, contained
sufficient inherent proof that he was not “ visiting
physician ” to Selborne ; yet I had been assured
that he was.
Amongst other stretchers, Sydney Truth
asserted (Dec. 18): —
The clergy of the several denominations have paid
extended visits to Selborne.
No clergyman ever presented himself inside the
grounds while I was there! Yet this statement
was contained in a descriptive report inserted as
an ad vertisement. Clearly, then, the public were
being fooled, and on me had been cast the onus of
unfooling them. This disagreeable worry may
have acted injuriously on my health. At any
rate, I know I was mopish every time I con-
trasted my friendship’s urgings with facts. Then,
if Sterling were Franklin and Test Patient No. 1
were—but this line of tangle is profitless.
My last week at the Sanatorium was, as I have
said, grogless; and, additionally, the doses of
“Tyson” from Friday, 30th Deo. until 12 noon
on Monday, 2nd Jan., were once more of double
strength—l 2 drops of No. 1, and 6 drops of No. 2
per hour. On the 12th, owing to rather unhealthy
symptoms and a glaring loss of appetite, the doses
were reduced to 6 and 3 as before. Mr. Sterling
assured me that there had never been a previous
instance of a patient needing a “ double dose ” for
more than one day, I, it will be seen, had two
days of it at one stretch, and three days at
another. With no results but unpleasant ones.
When I left the Sanatorium on January 5 I did
nob, my friends assert, present a healthy or
vigorous or built-up appearance. Also, 1 felt
fagged. For two or three days afterwards I did
little but sleep, or rather, drowse feverishly, and
I was unable to do any work until late on the
Sunday night. The array of testimonials from
others as to the efficacy of the “ Tyson Cure ” is,
to my experience, a mockery. Still, if any con-
solation can be extracted from the admission, I
will state that, on leaving the Sanatorium, I had
no desire whatever to plunge at once into a public-
house and fill myself up with snake-juice. Still
less had I any inclination for voluntary “Tysons.”
(I have not been an absolute teetotaller since.)
I find from my diary notes that, during my
term, I imbibed 25 glasses of whisky and one
glass of stout; and swallowed nearly seven fluid
ozs, of “Tyson’s Treatment” in about as many
pints of water.
a # #
The Secretary of the Melbourne Sana-
torium favoured Selborne with a two days’ visit,
December 17 —19. He is a remarkably cool,
shrewd-looking man-of-the-world, soft-voiced and
silky-mannered an easy-going, good - looking,
blonde.
He gave me an account of how he went over to
the U.S. in search of a “ Cure for Drunkenness ; ”
how he first of all visited the Keely Institute with
an idea of driving a bargain with the Keely Co.
in respect of the Australian right of the Double-
Chloride of Gold or Keely Cure ; how he was dis-
satisfied with the results visible in the Keely In-
stitute, with gruesome touches concerning the
poisonous-looking scabs on the arms of the hypo-
dermically-syringed victims, and whispers of fairy-
tales anent inoculated insanity ; how ho heard
Keely say that the only rival he feared was “ a
fellow down in Ohio ; ” how he therefore went to
Ohio, on a purely prospecting search, and lighted
on “Dr. Luther B. Tysop ” at once; how he in-
vestigated for months the results of Tyson’s
treatment and became convinced that at last he
had got hold of a genuine cure for dipsomania,
and that, with a heart swelling to benefit the poor
slaves to drink, he had come to terms with the
Delivering Genius of the Age. His story of how
that Genius discovered the secret is in itself a
sentimental romance.
“ Will 20,000 dollars buy your recipe, Dr.
Tyson ? ”
“‘No, sirree; nor 50,000; nor any sum. The
secret is known to me alone, and shall be revealed
to my son in good time.’ And the consequence
is that Dr. Tyson is now making a big fortune a
week. Yes, a big fortune a week.”
But the shrewd blonde did not say what the
Victorian syndicate was making.
The Melbourne “ secretary ” is good company.
There are, however, a few conundrums connected
with him. He signs his name officially as “H. E. 1
Peate.” He was introduced to me as “ Mr. Peate,
our Melbourne manager,” by Mr. Sterling. I
heard Sterling afterwards familiarly address him
as “Sam.” Later on, he was trippingly alluded
to in his absence as “Mr. Freshman,” and a Mel-
bourne acquaintance of mine has known him for
years as “Samuel Freshman,”
These little matters may be explained as mere
justifiable commercial conveniences, but, in that
case, the Tyson Sanatorium Co. of Victoria has no
call to paint itself as anything higher than a quack-
medioine-vending, money-making venture, in
common with lots of other much-puffed “ benefits
to the human race.”
* * *
The curse of alcoholism is such a world-
shadowing, mankind-blasting plague that were a
really effective “cure,” or even a meritorious pre-
ventive, discovered and puffed by the arch-cham-
pion of charlatans himself, The Bulletin would
do a good act if it gave it free advertising space.
So much panoramic mirage of reality had been
given to the alleged cures by the “Tyson Treat-
ment ’’that the best results were hoped for, even
in the case of a stringent and thoroughly searching
test. The chief outcome is regret that the “Tyson
Treatment ” is not what its vendors proclaim it to
be. It does not invariably remove the desire for
intoxicants of all kinds, and it is debateable
whether it is “perfectly harmless, morally, men-
tally, and physically,” as its proprietors assert.
Therapeutically, a great deal too much emphasis
is placed upon the direct action of the Tyson
drugs, and far too little to the patient’s physique,
to the beneficial effects of regular habits, strict
regimen, wholesome and generous diet, enforced
cleanliness, cheerful surroundings, absence of busi-
ness worries and sufficient sleep. These consti-
tute in themselves means capable of completing
a physical reformation in many patients;
Estimating the recuperative value of my own
stay in the Randwick Sanatorium, I give it as
my unhesitating opinion that, had I been clapped
by authority into an ordinary gaol for three
weeks, had I been compelled to eat the rough but
wholesome gaol diet and conform to the sanitary
discipline of that establishment, I would, without
the internal exhibition of a single drug, have
emerged from “chokee” a healthier, sounder and
more recuperated man than I was when I left
Selborne.
Would I have taken a drink immediately after
leaving gaol ? Almost without a doubt. That is
quite another question. I should most probably
have immediately poured out libations in com-
memoration of my regained freedom. That is a
matter of choice, and, so far as the Sanatorium’s
lingering influence over me is concerned, I have
no inward feeling of repulsion against the exercise
of that choice.
Gaol is undoubtedly the recuperator of the
half-starved, alcoholised criminal. One observes
its patrons enter lean and gaunt, with hollow
cheeks and sunken eyes, with limpness of gait and
boniness of contour. They are washed, clipped,
shaved, hustled about, provided with regular
though coarse meals, compelled to regular hours,
disciplined in physical exertion, and are turned
out fat, rosy, clear-eyed and staunch of gait.
Only, they have no worldly incentive of sufficient
attraction to cause them to continue over them-
selves the same discipline which they were com-
pelled to observe in prison, So the dogs return to
their vomit.
* * *
My recent experiment has confirmed me
in a long-held opinion that no so-called drink-cure
can be efficacious in the absence of will-power on
the part of the patient. In other words, each
advertised remedy is merely an aid and support
to the will. Which leads to a further considera-
tion.
No lost will-power can be restored to a patient,
unless he or she has an absolute preliminary faith
in the.efficacy of the remedy experimented with.
Here, then, are opened up all the questions
embraced by the Faith Cure, of which the Echo
Farm, at Middle Harbour, is only one of many
illustrations. Faith in healing can work apparent
miracles. Once, when I was a young assistant to
a chemist, I was suddenly confronted, during the
absence of my employer, by a cabman who thought
he was going to die. He was in a bad way,
evidently from heat-apoplexy. With the “front”
of youth, I gave him—simply, 15 drops of
“diluted sulphuric acid” in a wineglassful of
water, coloured with a few drops of “ burnt
sugar.” But the wise way in which I looked at
his tongue, felt his pulse, cross-examined him as
to his symptoms, and finally told him impressively
that the wonderful draught I was about to
administer would make him “a new man in
20 minutes ’’—did the whole trick. He did re-
cover completely within 20 minutes, and would
never again let my employer serve him if I was in
the shop.
Any doctor could reel you off scores of true
anecdotes to similar purpose. With the Faith
Cure, a few drops of pure water have frequently
been hypodermically injected by a Melbourne
doctor into the arms of dipsomaniacs, who
would be told that all desire for grog would now
cease until the clock struck such an hour, when
they must be “here to the tick ” to have the in-
jection repeated. This remedy never failed with
suitable “ faith ” subjects. It is doubtless allied
to hypnotism and cures “by suggestion.”
Its converse is death by “fright,” many
authentic eases of which are extant, as,
for instance, that of the Parisian condemned
criminal who was spared the guillotine and
handed over to members of the Faculty.
These blindfolded him and punctured his arm,
telling him they had inoculated him with
cholera germs. He not only developed cholera
but he died of it. Yet he had only been pricked
with a clean needle-point, and not a hypodermic
syringe at all. Imagination and Will hold strong
sway over the physical human envelope.
# # *
W 1 lore Faith is not, and where, therefore,
a surrendered Will is not, there is nothing in the
world can cure the dipsomaniac but compulsion.
Immure him safely against the possible reach of
all liquors until iiis lit has passed—no matter
whether it be for days or weeks. Keep him abso-
lutely touch-proof from it; he will thank you
afterwards That is the sum of my experience.
[We are very sorry to say that the writer of the
foregoing has, since leaving the Tyson Institute,
relapsed into liquor, giving very strong evidence
that the treatment doesn’t cure everyone. Ed. B.]

Here at Last!
Some seven years ago a strange thing happened to the lady
whose photo, appears herewith, whereby she became
A. MYSTERY
Which no one can explain or account for so far, not even her
husband. How it happened was this ; She lay dying of a compli-
cation of diseases ; often she would faint off, and lie unconscious
as long as 30 hours at a stretch—frigid as a corpse and apparently
dead. The leading medical men of Melbourne were in attendance,
often at a cost of Five, Guineas a day, yet she sank lower and
lower. Everything that medical science could suggest was tried ;
even morphia failed to give enough ease for two hours’ sleep
daily. At last, in desperation, some said, “ Try Mesmerism.”
Mr. Harle, as a Mesmerist,
Had been very successful on the public platform, and had also
saved life by private magnetic treatment; but Mrs. Harle, being
a lady of very pronounced opinions and strong mind of her own,
never could see anything in it but a hobby of his, and so, when
urged to try it, she ridiculed the very idea ; but they said, “ Try
it far steep only you will go mad for want of rest.” For a long
lime she could not bring her mind to submit; at last, after much
prayer to God for guidance and help, she was willing to try.
After about half an hour’s mesmeric influence and passes, she
went ff into a peaceful hypnotic state, which lasted, as was
iniemhd, from eight at night till ten next morning; and she
awoke rested and refreshed. The experiment was repealed, when
Lo! Everyone was Startled
Mrs. J. N. HARLE.
To find that, whilst to all intents and purposes she was asleep, yet in another sense, the mind was far more awake,
and she commenced talking about what the servant was doing on the ground floor, what an acquaintance was doing a
mile away, Vvhat was happening in the home of a dear friend some few blocks away, and so forth, then she was
requested Id look into her own body ; this she did, and was surprised herself, and surprised everyone else, by tracing
the cause of hei* trouble, which proved to be mainly an internal cancer. She then told them how to cure her.
Crowds of Friends Came to See Her*
For she had a large and influential circle of acquaintances. She astounded all by her marvellous pow ers of vision
to see into their bodies as if they were made of transparent glass, to see the members of their families, to know all
about their commercial affairs, seemed quite easy to her ; England; America, New Zealand, etc.; seemed just like next
door.
The Sensation was Immense!
Drawing-room, Sitting-room, Dining-room and Bedroom were crowded with inquisitive people, and the invalid became
ji phenomenon. The doctors were nonplussed with their patient - she gave directions how to Soften the cancer to the
lancing state: this \Vas dohe ; th'en, as they dare not glVe. chloroforth on account of her heart, Mr. Harle put hei undei

But her health was too low. First, she cured herself-in three months’lime she was again taking her accustomed

Seven Thousand People Have Consulted Her

An Afghan witness at Melbourne police
court last week coolly stated, without any attempt
at concealment, that he got his living by stealing
from Hindoo hawkers.
Several American Indians may now be
seen roaming about Melbourne. Chinese, Hindoos,
Kanakas, Syrians, Malays, American Injuns !

When people are married, though they
should have sixty children, they throw the whole
onus on God. When they are not, we hear nothing
about God’s having sent them. Olive Schreiner.
A New York firm is manufacturing arti-
ficial honey—the comb of paraffin wax, the cell-
interiors of manipulated glucose. A Yankee
paper declares this imitation to be “entirely
superior to the best honey ever made by bees.”
Gov. Jersey gets aboard bis horse from
behind the stirrup. It’s well for him that lie’s
not going to stay in Australia, for, if he did, one
day some moon-eyed brumby would hoof him
beyond the precincts of the solar system.
Hawkins, the famous English “ hanging
Judge,” intends to retire, and devote the rest of
his life to cabbages and horse-racing. Some of
’A wkins’ early judgments were most bloodthirsty
mistakes. Several people whom lie sentenced to
be hanged have lived to say “Yah!” to him in
the street. “ Yah,” coming from one whose soul
you have commended to the Lord’s mercy, must
be extremely annoying.

In view of a certain coming event the
English newspapers are busily working up bogus
anecdotage re Princess “ May ”of Teck. London
D.T. sets forth that the other day, whilst out
driving, she saw a costermonger brutally “ per-
suading ” Ids donkey. Pulling up her ponies, she
ordered her groom to take the whip from the man,
who naturally resented this summary proceeding.
A card was promptly handed to him (though a
coster he could read 1) whereupon he fell on his
knees in the dust, “ begged ’er R’yal ’ighnesses’
parding,” and broke his whip over his knees, not
necessarily for publication, bijt as a guarantee of
good faith, vowing that thenceforth ho would
treat his “ neddy ” as a brother. Through his
eontrite tears he could just see a tiny hand
extended, and holding out his own in response
found himself the happy possessor of a real golden
sovereign ; and ere he could dry his eyes the
Princess was far on the road towards home. This
is the sort of stuff with which the “largest cir-
culation” works up the diminishing loyalty of its
readers. Part of the story may be swallowed, but
the sovereign business is a bit too thick. Coins
of that denomination are not too abundant with
the poor Ticks.

11l Australian gold-miners there is perhaps
more real grit than in any other body of workmen.
Life in the early digging days of Australia may
have developed ruffianism in many, but true
character in many more. One of the latter was
killed on Bendigo recently, aild as pool did John
Ellis had refused all personal ahd political endow-
ment while alive, thousands honoured him when
dead by the largest funeral eVer seen in the dis-
trict. It extended from his home almost to the
Cemetery, more than a mile away. The writer, as
a youngster, was broken in as a miner by him,
Ellis, for thirty years, was regarded as the
scholarly miner of Eaglehawk. In parliamentary
and municipal elections his influence was no mean
thing, and in his religion you could find no trace
of humbug. By force of character lie won him-
self a position for good amongst his fellows,
and refused all preferment save the esteem of those
who knew him, which meant everybody, young and
old. He and a party of four came from Plymouth
to Australia in the fifties in a small craft. Settling
in Bendigo he worked 40 years in the mines, and
as a miner met his doom. He was trucking
mullock away from one of the upper levels of the
Catherine Reef shaft, and at his age, 02, it re-
quired all his strength to push the loaded truck to
the cage ; therefore, he put his head down and
pushed with all his might, thinking the cage was
at the shaft to receive it. A few moments before
one of the bosses had, without warning, signalled
the empty cage away. Ellis’ own son-in-law, the
engine-driver, obeyed the signal, and ere the cage
reached the surface poor Ellis had pushed the
truck into the shaft and was carried down with it
300 feet. Little wonder, under the circumstances,
that the company awarded his widow .£4OO.
Three generations wept at his grave, and
grief was felt by many miners scattered over the
gold-fields of Australia when they heard the sad
ending of an old and beloved comrade.

"Bully” Hayes : The Pirate of the
Pacific.
A CHAPTER OF SOUTH SEA HISTORY.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Bully ” Hayes ! O, halcyon days of 70
and ’74, when the Pacific was riot, as now; patrolled
by men-of-war from lonely Pylstaart, in the
Friendlies, to the low-lying, far-away Marshalls
and the coral lagoons of the North-West; when the
Queensland schooners ran full nigger cargoes to
Bundaberg, Maryborough, and Port Maokay;
when the Government Agents, drunk nine days
out of ten (as they probably will be now),
did as much recruiting as the recruiters them-
selves, arid drew (even as they may draw to-
day) thumping bonuses from the planters sub
rosei. In those days the Hawaiian nigger-
catching fleet of Spreckels cruised right away
south to palm-clad Arorai, in the Line Islands,
rind ran the Queensland ships close in the busi-
riess. They came down froiri Honolulu in ballast-
trim, save for the liquor and firearms, and went
Hack full of a sweating niass of black-haired,
coppet-coloured Lirie Islanders, driven below at
dark, to take their chance of being smothered if it
crime on to blow. Better for them had
it so happened, as befel the Tahiti a few
moriths ago, when 400 souls sank on their
way to slavery iri San Jose de Guatemala.
Merry times, indeed, had we then in the trade
“absolutely above reproach” when Queensland
rivalled the Hawaiian Islands in the exciting
business! of nigger-catching, and when Captairi
William Heriry Hayes, of Cleveland, Ohio,
U.S.A., vulgarly called “Bully” Hayes, came
twice a year to Samoa with full cargoes of oil,
copra; and niggers, all obtained on the captain’s
peculiar time-payment system.
• • •
A few weeks ago I met an old shipmaster
coming out of Sydney Exchange. We fell to
talking of bygone days when we were both in
“ the trade,” he a past-master in the art of black-
biiding, and I but a callow recruit, or rather
recruiter, having plenty of enthusiasm and a con-
science sufficiently elastic for the peculiarities of
the business “absolutely above reproach.” Pre-
sently the old fellow —who, by the way, was a
Scotchman and accustomed to speak of the trade
as a “ varraineequeetous business,” said, with a
sigh, as he putdown his “gless”—“ Weel, Weel, it’s
nae use talkin’, there was wheeps o’ money in it
and noo that Saum Greefiths has gone on the ither
tack (the man hasna’ the guts of a shairk) I’m
thinkin’ we’ll have another chance o’ grabbing
niggers’ wool again, and it’s no so unlikely that
‘ , o’ the Hopeful ’ ” (referring to a well-known
Australian politician at one time Samuel W. Grif-
fith’s bete noir) “ willna’ hae the pluck to show his
colours, too, and send oot anither Hopeful. D’ye
mind, noo, that there was nasae lucky a raon at it
a’ than that deevil’s pup of a Bully Hayes, as ye
know weel.”
One hardly ever hears the name of the redoubt-
able Bully mentioned nowadays ; yet it is not so
many years ago since his name was a power all
over the wide Pacific from Manilla to Valparaiso.
In those days did a German trading : vessel in the
islands sight a white-painted brig with yacht-like
lines and carrying Cunningham’s patent top’sails
the Teutonic skipper cracked on all his ship could
stagger under and thanked Gott when he saw the
stranger hull-down ; for Bully, with his jidus
achates , the almost equally notorious Captain Ben
Peese, had a penchant for boarding Dutchmen and
asking for a look at their chronometers, and iri his
absent-minded way taking these latter away with
him.
And iri Sydney, too, many of the pompous;
pauncheous, grey - headed, 1-am-a-Broken-Hill-
shareholder-sir city men will remember the gay,
dashing, black-whiskered Yankee captain who, iri
the “ sixties,” came to Melbourne in a flash clipper
ship and paid also a long visit to Port Jackson,
where he spent his money royally, flirting (alas !
if he had but stopped at that) with every acces-
sible woman of high or low degree—provided she
was fair to look upon—and playing the devil
generally in every known and unknown manner;
and who then sailed gaily away to China, neglect-
ing to attend to many little financial matters in
connection with the refitting of his ship, and
leaving the affections of a number of disconsolate
beauties in a very bad state of repair.
1 knew Bully Hayes well, for I sailed with and
was wrecked with him in his famous brig, and,
after the conversation just alluded to, the memory
of those wild days comes strongly upon me. I am
not going to risk damnation of soiil by spinning a
faricifully-embroidered tale about things that did
riot happen, and wrong the memory of the defunct
pirate (whose bones lie at the bottom of the North-
west Pacific, unless, indeed, as is most likely, they
were long ago digested by the blue sharks), by
even alluding to the murders, iribst foul and un-
natriral, credited td him by his enemies. Many
of these tales are purely missionaries’ babble arid
slander the nidus formed in Micronesia, and
spread by villainous riewspaper reports all over
the United States. I will relate what I did see
and what did occur.
* # *
A man’s true character is always revealed
by sudden misfortune. I was with Hayes when,
perhaps, the heaviest Stroke of ill-luck he ever
experienced befell him. In March of ’74, the lily-
white brig Leonora ground herself to death on the
jagged coral of Strong’s Island, in the Caroline
Group, and Bully seemed for the nonce a broken
man. But I did not sd well know then that
beneath that gay, laughirig, devil-may-care
exterior there lay a whole world of dauntless
courage and iron resolution 5 that six months
after the brig was destroyed he would, by
unwearying toil and the wonderful fascination he
exercised over his fierce and ruffianly creW, find
himself a wealthier man than when he trod his
brig’s deck with a full cargo of oil beneath his
feet and ten thousand dollars in his cabin.
# # *
Let me first of all, though, speak of the
Leonora, once the Waterlily, and alias the Luna,
the Lebnie, and the Racinga. As the Waterlily
she was first known, and under that name did her
maiden voyage in the opium-trade and beat the
record. At this time Hayes made his appearance
at one of the Treaty Ports in a ship named the
Old Dominion. On the way out from New York
his crew had mutinied, headed by the steward, a
Greek. In the fight that ensued Hayes killed one
man outright by a blow of his fist, and threw
another with such violence that he died in a few
hours. An inquiry was held, and Hayes came
out of it well. The Old Dominion was sold, and
Hayes entered the Imperial Chinese service as
commander of a gunboat. Another gunboat was
commanded by one Ben Peese. The two became
friends, and in conjunction with some mandarins
of high tank, levied a system of blackmail upon
the Chinese coasting junks that brought them (not
the junks) iri money very rapidly, and Hayes’
daring attack on and capture of, a nest of (other)
pirates procured for him a good standing with the
Chinese authorities. Peese soon got into trouble,
however, and when a number of merchants, who
had been despoiled, had succeeded in proving that
his gunboat was a worse terror to them than the
pirates whom he worried, he disappeared for a
time. The Waterlily, instead of sailing for Cal-
cutta, was chartered at a big figure by some rich
merchants to take a cargo to Rangoon. Shortly
after her departure, Hayes resigned and went to
Macao. Here he was joined by his colleague, in
commandof the Waterlily. How Peese had got pos-
session of her was not known. Hayes told people
that his friend had bought her, but those intimate
with Peese knew a great deal better. Anyhow,
the merchants who chartered her said that Peese
had pirated her, landed them somewhere in the
Straits, taken all their dollars, sold the cargo to
the Dutch military authorities, and cleared out.
And then with a new ship, a new crew (many of
whom were Hayes’ arid Peese’s former Chinese
naval pirates), the partners sailed for the Bonin
Islands, where Peese was well-known and had
lived before, Two days ere making the Bonins a
ship was sighted ashore bn a reef. It Was a gun-
boat froiri Macao with an official on board bound
id the Boriin Islands to investigate the murder of
a Portuguese captairi and mate. A boat was
lowered frdm ihe Waterlily, and Peese, who spoke
Spanish well, learned from the captain that the
gunboat, which was then hard and fast, had run
ashore iri the night and.bumped a big hole in her-
self just amidships. Peese agreed for a thousand
dollars to stand by them and save all he could, in-
cluding her four guns. The guns were rafted to
the Waterlily, then the small arms and stores
followed in the boats belonging to the gunboat.
At dusk Hayes went aboard and took the brig’s
Chinese carpenter with him. On examination he
Said the ship could be got off again if she could be
canted over and a sail put over the hole. This
the gunboat captain agreed to try, and signalled
for his boats to return from the Waterlily. After
working all night the thing was done, and the
captain and officers were profuse in their expres-
sions of admiration of Hayes’s skill. As the
tide fell the carpenters got to work, and
the gunboat was made water-tight. Under Hayes’s
direction, at flood-tide, she was then hedged over
the reef into the lagoon, and anchored in smooth
water. Peese and Hayes then arranged to sail in
the brig (at next tide), lay her alongside, and put
the guns and stores aboard again, agreeing to take
the captain’s order for 700dols. and SOOdols. in
cash. But next morning the brig was nowhere to
be seen, and although the captain had his ship, he
Was minus his big guns, small arms, stores to the
value of 2000 or 3000 dollars, and his passenger,
the Corregidor, In attempting to get under
weigh he again ran ashore, and remained hard and
fast for a week. Meanwhile, Hayes and Peese
had gone off on a southerly course to the Pelew
Group (the Corregidor was put on board a German
barque met on the way), where the cannons were
sold to the chiefs, and the two captains gave a
great feast, and got rid of nearly all their crew,
taking Pelew men and seven Japanese in their
places. Here Hayes and Peese fell out—over a
woman, of course. teese , ■ bought a very
beautiful girl from one of the chiefs for AiOdols.,
which, he told Hayes privately, he did not intend
to pay. - Hayes, for reasons of lus own, insisted
on his either paying the sum agreed on or giving
her up. Peese, declaring he .would do as he liked,
drew his pistol arid ordered the girl into the boat,
Hayes tore the weapon from him, and seizing the
girl with one hand pointed the pistol at Peese and
told him to go on board. Peese was no co ward,
but he knew his man, and sulkily retired. With
all Hayes s wickedness he was not entirely heart-
less. He asked the girl to tell him if she was
afraid of Peese. She said No. and then Bully
quietly told her to follow his fellow-captain
aboard. But Peese never forgave him, and from
that day the pair were full of mutual distrust.
After cruising about the Western Carolines for
two or three months, and in some mysterious way
filling up the brig with a cargo of cocoanut oil,
and getting half a ton of hawk-bill turtle-shell,
worth (idols, a pound, the two worthies appeared
in Apia Harbour, Samoa. Here they sold the
cargo and obtained a commission from the firm of
Johann Caesar Godeffroy and Sons, of Hamburg
—a firm that in Polynesia rivalled, m a small
way, John Company to procure for them 200 or
300 Line Island labourers at lOOdols. per head. _
In those days the most respected storekeeper in
Apia was a retired mariner a Captain iurnbull
—a stout old man, slow of speech, and profoundly,
but not obtrusively, religious. People used to
wonder how it was that MisiPulu, the shrewdest
brisiness-man ill thegrbup, would supply Hayes With
a thousand or two thousand dollars worth or trade,
and merely take his I O 17; while refusing tb
give credit to any other soul. Spoken to on the
(flatter, the gruff old man replied, That s tny
business, but 111 tell you why I trust a Irian
like Hayes and won’t trust anyone here, i know
the man, and I’ve told him what none of you
would dare to tell him, that I looked upon his
course pi life with horror. He laughed at me
and said with a dreadful oath that if ever he
could do me a ‘good turn he would. I hat
pleased me, and When he came to me a week after-
wards and said that he wanted new canvas and
running-gear, but the Dutchmen woc *u t Sell
him any on credit, I said I would—and did, and
he paid me, and I 11 give him a few thousand
dollars credit any day.”
Bully and Peese sailed for the Ellice and
Gilbert groups, and soon news reached bydney
that they had been playing havoo with the traders
there. With the traders of Captain Eury, and
those of Captain Daly, of the brig Lady Alicia,
they were very rough, appropriating all their oil
and other native produce and giving them sarcas-
tically-written receipts. Hayes stated that this
was in retaliation for Daly haying visited his
stations in some of the Kingsmills, and having
been too friendly with some of the local fair,
When the brig returned to Samoa Hayes alone
was in command ; the voluble, bearded Peese had,
he said, sold him his interest in the ship and gone
to China again. People talked and said that
Hayes had killed him, but as the strength of the
big captain’s right arm was well known m
Samoa nobody talked too loud. It was on
this occasion that Hayes “ had ’ the Gorman firm
for some thousands of dollars. It seems that in
returning through the Kingsmill and Gilbert
groups he found a number of the German firm s
traders in terror of their lives, the natives having

warned them to clear out or be killed. Hayes
consented to give them all passages to Samoa
for a consideration, of course, and they agreed on
behalf of their linn to pay him each SOdols.
passage-money, a reasonable enough sum. Most
of them had large quantities of oil and copra—this
also was shipped. After the last island had been
visited Hayes called them together in the cabin
and addressed them “ Now, boys, I’ve promised
to give you all passages to Samoa, and 1 will—if
you do what i want. Now, you’ve all got money
belonging to the German firm. Well, each of you
must give me SOdols., and if you take my advice
you’ll stick to the remainder. One thing you all
know as well as 1 do, and that is, that the Hutch-
men will take your souls out of their cases if you
owe them anything. As for the oil and copra I'll
see to that. That’s ail I’ve got to say, and if any
of you won’t agree to this let him come on deck
and try and convince me.” The traders grinned
and consented to take the offer of a passage and
the privilege of annexing the firm’s dollars, and
each paid his SOdols. When Hayes got to Samoa,
Weber, the German manager, interviewed Bully,
who detailed the dangers the traders had escaped,
and genially said : “ I hardly like to makh you
pay for your traders’ passages, but as I have such
a heavy cargo for you, you won’t object to pay me
a trifle—say SOdols. each. They’ve all got money
for yoii as well as oil and copra.” Weber paid,
Hayes giving an acknowledgment. Then Weber
sent his cargo-boats to unload the brig. He was
rather surprised when Hayes sent him a note ;
Bng Leonora, Apia. Dear Sir. You have forgotten
that you have not yet made any arrangements with me
about the freight of your oil and’copra. Freight on
200 OOOlbs. copra, at 1 cent per lb., 2000d015., for the oil a
lump sura of 500dols. ; in all 2500d015. Unless the freight is
paid at once, and delivery taken forthwith, I will proceed
to New Zealand, and sell to recoup mysd’. - W. H. Hayes.
The Germans nearly went mad, but knowing
what Hayes was and fearing to lose everything,
they paid and took delivery, and Hayes, as he
paid over, told Weber that he would always have
a good opinion of him in future for his prompt
manner in settling up. Weber gasped, but said
nothing. . ,
Just about this time the U.S.S. Narrangansett
steamed into Apia harbour. It had been rumoured
around Polynesia for some time previously that
certain charges had been made against Bully by
American citizens. What the exact nature of
these charges were has never been known. Any-
how, the captain of the corvette heard that Hayes
was at anchor in Apia, and came down full speed
from Pago Pago in Tutuila. Captain Ed. Hamil-
ton (who died only last year) was then pilot, and
brought the Narrangansett in. The moment the
anchor was down, an armed boat’s crew dashed
aboard the Leonora and took possession. The
officer in command had a surprise in store for him
when, entering the brig’s cabin, he saw seated at
the table, not the truculent, piratical ruffian he
expected to see, but a quiet, stout man of her-
culean proportions, who bowed politely and said,
“Welcome on board the Leonora, sir. Have you
come to seize my ship and myself? Well, now,
don’t apologise, but sit down a while until
my steward brings you a glass of wine, and
then I’ll go and see what all this is about.”
This officer afterwards told Hamilton that he was
so struck with Bully’s cool effrontery and his
equally genial smile that he did sit down and take
a drink, and then Hayes accompanied him to the
corvette. As the boat ran alongside the officers
and bluejackets not on duty thronged to the side
to see the famous, pirate, who walked calmly to
the quarter-deck, and, singling out the captain
(Meade, 1 think, was his name) said : “How do
you do, sir. l am happy to see my country’s flag
again in these seas ; but what the hell do you mean,
sir, by putting an armed crew on my deck i By
God, sir, if you don’t give me good reasons I 11
make you repent it.” The corvette captain stood
quite unmoved, although there was a suppressed
titter heard amongst his officers.
“ I pardon you your offensive language, Cap-
tain Hayes, as” I daresay you feel excited. If you
will come below I will show you good authority
for my action. I have orders to arrest you and
investigate serious charges against you. 1 trust,
however, that you will be able to clear yourself.”
The quiet, gentlemanly manner of the naval
officer acted like a charm upon Hayes. The
fierce glitter in his bright blue eyes died out, and
bowing to the corvette captain he turned to the
group of officers, and in a bluff, sincere manner
said: “Gentlemen, I apologise to your captain
and to you for my insulting manner. I see that
I have acted in an unbecoming way ; but I am a
hasty man, yet quick to make amends when I am
in the wrong.”
The officers returned his salute, and then Bully
went below and listened with an unmoved face to
the warrant for his arrest. He was allowed to
write a letter to his wife, and given the liberty of
the ship whilst the captain of the Narrangansett
was preparing for the trial. A notification was
sent to the three Consuls of his seizure, and asking
them to verify the charges made to them by
various persons against Hayes. None but the
German Consul responded, and his witnesses
(traders whose stations had been cleaned out by
Hayes) utterly broke down. One look at those
steady, steel-blue eyes was enough for them. They
knew what was in store for them if any of them
ever crossed Bully’s path again, and slunk away
to their German protectors. After two hours’
investigation, the captain broke up the court,
and formally told those present that he would
announce his decision in writing. As the German
Consul, followed by two German merchant
skippers, rose from the table, Hayes asked the
captain if he could say a few words. Leave was
granted.
“ I merely wish to tell those gentlemen leaving
that I hate liars—especially Dutch liars.”
“ You forget your position, sir,” said the
captain, turning away to hide a smile.
Two hours afterwards the Narrangansett’s
captain wrote a brief note to the Consuls, stating
that he would not —from the unreliable and con-
tradictory evidence —be justified in taking Hayes
to the United States, and added some severe
remarks about the skulking and terrified manner
of the witnesses.
Then Hayes was .told he was a free man and
straightway the prisoner became the guest, and
Bully made a neat little speech.
“Gentlemen, I thank you for your kindness and
courtesy to me. You have done me a good service.
If I went to the States now and told hpw I had
been seized by a tyrannical American officer, it
would make me a rich man, I could run for
President. I could get in, too. I could paint you
all as a crew of piratical ruffians disgracing the
uniform of the greatest country in the world, and
the papers would back me up. They would make
me President of a big bank, and would send all of
you to serve your country in Alaska —to keep you
from getting lynched by an indignant nation.
But I am just going to be good and generous and
remain in obscurity; and to-morrow night I shall
be proud and happy if you will honour me by
