Tommy’s Hut.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
There’s a clearing in the ranges on the old bush-
road to Yea,
Where a shanty oriefe Was running in the old and
lawless way;
It was here the splitters rallied, and the drifting
traveller soaked,
And the drover, halting; tallied, and the billlocky
unyoked.
It was here the bushman melted down the fruits
of thrift and toil
In cooling draughts of chemicals and nips of fusel-
oil;
It was here they vied in “ shouting ” till the stilly
ranges rang.
And the muffled sounds of “clouting” mingled
with the songs they sang.
It was here the strangest stories, too, were told of
mining lodes —
Of the streaming strings of waggons that were
met upon the roads,
Till listless loungers started as the wondrous tale
was told,
And, sobering up, departed for the dazzling fields
of gold.
There were evenings at the shanty when you’d see
a stranger sight,
And the sound of tuneful voices, then, would fall
upon the night—
When homeless, hopeless wanderers softly sang
some sweet refrain,
And stealing o’er the squanderers the spirit, Rest,
would reign.
They were men who once had started with the
wildest hopes and dreams
That led them from the beaten track to breast the
flooded streams;
In their pride they scorned the compass, and
struck boldly on ahead,
But they noted not the ridges, nor to where the
gullies led.
They were nearer then, those revellers, in their
soft but saddened song#
As it bore them, circling, backward past the
marks they’d blazed along,
Nearer to the well-known places drifting slowly
into view#
With the dim, reproachful faces in the faintly-
surging blue.
Drifting often back so dimly, that the bushman,
filled with doubt,
Strains his eyes to see before him, wondering
where he might “come out,”
Till, in merciless revealing, passes by each Well-
known trace,
And,he sees, with senses reeling, that he’s “made ”
his starting place.
Tommy’s Hut has gone for ever with an age that’s
passed away,
And a grassy plot tells only of a wild, advcntutous
day,
And this scene of noisy riot sleeps ill silehce so
profound
That an air of deathly quiet seems td linger o’ef
the ground.
When the moonlight floods the ranges; ahd the
bush is cast in light—
And the messmate shells are standing, “ rung,” in
ghostly robes of white,
Pale shadows, fast assembling, steal froth scrub-
lined haunts around
And blacken, as they trembling fall athwart the
open ground.
It is then the lonely traveller, should he list, at
length may hear
In faint and feeble whispering words that fain
would reach his ear,
And with the dark enleadened scrawling shadows
strive to tell
Of the men the hush has deadened with its mystic,
subtle spell.
W. E. CAREW.

In Michigan the law requires, under
penalty of £lOO sterling, that every locomotive-
driver, stoker, brakesman, must be a teetotaller.
London Times has just squared up the last
instalment of the expenses of the Parnell Com-
mission. That fatal invoice has hung like a mill-
stone round its neck for years, and even when
Parnell was dead his enemy was slaving to pay up
the price of his vengeance, and couldn’t enjoy his
decease even a little.
The recently-published results of the
yearly Sydney University exams, plainly exhibit
the baneful effect of the new regulations. Very
few passed in Mathematics or Classics, and none
in Science (Arts), while shoals got through in
Mental Philosophy, Logie, and History, AB. A.
obtained in subjects such as these gives not the
slightest guarantee that the holder is a man of
liberal education, for any intelligent youth without
previous training could, after a three months’
Cram, pass the usual exam, in them.
A London reporter was offered £3OO cash
to suppress all mention of a case in which a
wealthy individual was interested. He didn’t
expect "to get more than ss. for a casual paragraph
about the case, but he Was a man with a lofty
ideal, ahd he sternly refused the bribe. His para-
graph was never printed, and he never got the ss.
The defendant had given the £3OO to the editol’
instead, and as that potentate hadn’t any lofty
ideals he took it without a blush. Thete is a
moral in this. The present writer was once offered
10s. by a showman who was making a precarious
livelihood out of a living skeleton, and he has felt
kind of sorry ever since that he refused it.

“TALES OF the CONVICT SYSTEM.”
Ambulanciana.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
They had been married several years, and having
“no encumbrance,” she Was able to devote her
whole attention to his ailments, which were
changeable as the temper of man.
She was certainly a hard-worked slave, until
her emancipation was brought about by the plucky
person who first talked of starting a branch of the
Ambulance Society, in the township, I say
“plucky,” for assuredly he or she who attempts
to instil a new idea into the minds of the dwellers
in a small country town needs a tdugh hide and
unlimited brass.
Both requisites being to hand; the said person
secured an influential committee, who started a
branch of the Ambulance Society in the town-
ship of Three Peaks.
Someone asked the slave-wife to attend thC
classes; but received the reply that “ there is not
nearly time enough in the day as it is, without
wasting two hours a week On one’s inside, which
is bother enough already, goodness knows
referring, of course, to her husband’s inner
machinery. She was too busy to trouble about
her own;
When she told him of her refusal to join the
classes; his anger knew no bounds. “What!” he
cried. “Do you carry your selfishness even so far
as that ? Here, for the last three years, have you
been ignorantly putting down my fearful suffer-
ings to ’imagination’ or ‘fancy,’ never knowing
how to ease or support me; now, when the chance
to learn something of the pains I endure and of
their remedies is offered, literally thrust upon you,
you selfishly refuse to benefit by it. I beg you
will reconsider the matter and attend the classes
from first to last.”
It was as good as done ; she did attend them.
After the second lecture she sat one evening
looking over her notes of the first one, while he,
fidgeting uneasily about the room, at length
settled upon the corner of the table nearest her,
and held forth as usual.
“ Now, I do hope you are going in for this thing
thoroughly, and not in your usual slip-shod
fashion. I shall be willing to give any help I can,
provided I see some result. That’s the worst of
you women, you are all so half-hearted,”
“It’s nice to see you taking an interest in my
progress,” she answered, ignoring his slur upon
women’s zeal. “ I hope you won’t feel bored if I
talk a good deal of the ambulance just now-
one can’t well help it when one is very much in
earnest over a thing.”
“By Jove, now, that’s something like! I’ve
never known you so roused up before. Not even
when I’ve been at my worst.”
“ These bones are pretty difficult to remember
when one comes to over two hundred ; it’s a pretty
big effort to get the name and position of each
one.”
“I should think so ! What’s puzzling you just
this time ? ”
“ Assitabulum I never can remember that
one.”
“You should resort to artificial memory—keep it
in mind by associating it with another object.”
“ How clever of you, dearest! Of course / see
—As-si -tabulum —Yes, I know,” looking up at
him, “an ass sitting upon a table. Thanks,
awfully,”
“You needn’t be so beastly rude!’’—getting
off the table and walking to the window, while she
continued poring over her notes.
After the fifth lecture of the second course, she
told him there was now no further need to call in
a doctor, in any ordinary ailment or accident at
any rate, as she was perfectly confident of being
able to do all that would be needed, and what a
heap of money they could save !
There was something about the manner and
speech now, that he did not altogether like; an
absence of that cowed deference to himself and
his weaknesses, which had existed before she
joined the Ambulance and learnt what a deal of
pain and inconvenience may be caused by a
stretched muscle or an overstrained nerve.
At first he tried to frighten her into the old
attention to his least word of command : “ My
dear,” he would say, “ fetch me a little brandy—
quick ! ”
“ Feeling faint, sweetheart ? ” and rushing up
she seized his ears, and jamming his head down
between his knees held it there until his loud cries
for release caused her to remark i “You don’t
need the brandy now, dear, you’re quite yourself
again, otherwise ”•—holding his wrist—“ the pulsa-
tion in your radial brachial would be feebler.”
He didn’t feel faint any more.
When he declared that his sciatica caused such
agony as to be unendurable, she replied : “It’s
not sciatica, darling, and though painful it
doesn’t involve the slightest danger, so cheer up.
The fact is, your assitabulum (ass on the table—
you remember) fails to secrete sufficient synovial
fluid to lubricate its socket, thus causing you
some pain in moving your leg.”
“ Some pain - 1” but he did not press the
matter.
i n< n, one night, when he complained of head-
ache and swore he had inflammation of the brain
coming on, she merely said :
“ If you will drive for hours, on an afternoon
like this was, without a puggaree, the sun glaring
down upon your medulla oblongata ”
“ My oblong whatl ”
“Gata.”
“ I haven’t a garter about me ! ”
“ Yes ! your medulla oblongata—it’s no wonder
it aches afterwards. I shouldn’t be surprised if
the arteries supplying your cerebrum and cerebel-
lum are also a good deal congested.”
Ho fled gasping for breath.
One day he limped in bemo.aning a sprained
ankle, though there was no visible swelling. After
examination she gave it as her opinion that his
tarsals and metatarsals were out of all proportion
to his astragalus, so who on earth could expect
anything but weakness of the joints—and she in-
sisted on his stopping in bed for a week.
But his last struggle for supremacy and right
to invalidism came to naught when, on his
remarking that he had a new and horrible pain
“beginning about the middle of his back and
extending forwards and downwards,” she
appeared puzzled for a moment, then said slowly ;
“It may—be—from your intercostals, but it is
more likely that your aorta or your main
carotid has got a little mixed up with your com-
mon, external, and internal tliaes. The trouble
will probably descend to the femorals, from thence
to the popliteal, and so down the tibial and fibular
until mortification of the toes will bo the natural
result.”
She loft him with horror depicted in every
line of his countenance.
That was months ago, and he has never dared
to have even one ache since.
MICHAEL AGEN.
